The Third Division of Existents: Non-Material Beings
Classification of existents and critique of theologians' position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division of existents and why the theologians' denial of non-material possible beings leaves their proof for the temporal origination of the world incomplete.

Core Idea
The theologians' proof only covers bodies and accidents, not non-material beings.

This passage introduces the third division of existents—beings that are neither bodies nor states in bodies. The author notes that God is proven to be such, but the question arises about possible beings. Philosophers affirm non-material possible beings, theologians deny them. The author argues that the theologians lack proof against this division, and their standard argument for the temporality of the world does not encompass it. Therefore, their claim that everything besides God is temporal is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the third division
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the definition of the third division: an existent that is neither a body nor a state in a body.
2
Note the two groups and their positions
The philosophers (hukama') affirm the existence of non-material possible beings, while the theologians (mutakallimun) deny them. Observe how the author presents this contrast.
3
Understand the criticism of the theologians
The author points out that the theologians have no proof against the third division. Their proof for huduth (temporal origination) only covers bodies and accidents, not this third division.
This is the key logical gap.
4
Consider the implication for their claim
To maintain that everything besides God is temporal, the theologians must either disprove the third division or provide a proof for its temporality. Since they did neither, their argument is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث من أقسام الموجودات ، وهو الموجود الذي لا يكون متحيزاً ، ولا حالاً في المتحيز . فقد ثبت بالدلائل اليقينية : أن الله سبحانه كذلك . وهل حصل في الممكنات موجود هذا شأنه ، أم لا ؟ فالحكماء أثبتوه والمتكلمون أنكروه . وليس مع المتكلمين دليل يدل على فساد هذا القسم ، ودليلهم على حدوث العالم إنما يتناول المتحيزات والأعراض القائمة بها ، ولا يتناول هذا القسم الثالث ، فعلى هذا دعواهم أن كل ما سوى الله محدث ، إنما يتم إما بإبطال هذا القسم الثالث ، أو بذكر دليل يدل على حدوث هذا القسم الثالث بتقدير ثبوته . ولما لم يذكروا شيئاً في هذين المقامين ، بقي كلامهم غير تام في المقصود
As for the third division of divisions of existents, namely the existent that is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor a state in a body. It has been established by certain proofs that God, exalted, is like that. And has there occurred among possibles an existent of this nature or not? The philosophers (hukama') affirmed it and the theologians (mutakallimun) denied it. The theologians have no proof indicating the corruption of this division. Their proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of the world only covers bodies and the accidents subsisting in them, and does not cover this third division. So upon this, their claim that everything other than God is originated can only be completed either by invalidating this third division or by mentioning a proof indicating the temporal origination of this third division given its existence. Since they did not mention anything in these two positions, their statement remains incomplete with respect to the intended purpose.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the third division of existents: that which is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor a state in a body. The author states that God is proven to be like this by certain proofs. The question arises: are there any possible (mumkin) beings that are also non-material? The philosophers (hukama') affirm the existence of such beings (like separate intellects), but the theologians (mutakallimun) deny them. The author criticizes the theologians: they have no proof against the possibility of such beings. Their standard proof for the temporal origination of the world only applies to bodies and accidents, not to this third division. Therefore, to uphold their claim that everything besides God is temporal, they must either disprove the existence of this third division or provide a proof for its temporality. Since they did neither, their argument is incomplete.

الممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theologians' proof for the temporal origination of the world applies to all existents other than God.
Correction: The passage states that their proof only covers bodies and accidents, not the third division of non-material beings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the theologians' proof for the huduth of the world insufficient to prove that everything besides God is temporal?
A) They did not provide any proof
B) It only covers bodies and accidents, not the third division
C) The philosophers disproved it
D) It only covers accidents

Key Takeaway
The theologians' denial of non-material possible beings lacks proof, and their huduth argument does not cover them, leaving their claim that everything besides God is temporal unsubstantiated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical gap in a widely held position (like the theologians'), do you examine it honestly or do you dismiss it due to loyalty to your school? How does this lesson challenge your willingness to follow truth wherever it leads?
?
The theologians denied non-material possibles because they couldn't prove them. Are there truths about Allah that you reject simply because they don't fit your mental framework? What would it cost you to remain open to evidence even when it contradicts your assumptions?
?
This passage shows that a claim (everything besides God is temporal) was left incomplete because its proponents did not address a missing link. In your own faith, are there claims you hold that rest on incomplete reasoning? What step will you take today to pursue greater intellectual honesty before Allah?

The Third Division of Existents: A Critique of the Theologians' Position
Sanusi critiques the theologians' incomplete proof against non-corporeal possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theologians' claim that everything besides Allah is originated is incomplete unless they address the third division of existents.

Core Idea
The theologians' proof for the world's origination does not cover non-corporeal beings.

The author identifies a third category of existents—neither body nor accident—and notes that Allah belongs to it. He then raises a crucial objection: the philosophers affirm other such beings, while the theologians deny them without proof. This passage exposes a gap in the theologians' argument for the temporality of all created things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third division
Read the passage and note what 'third division' means: an existent that is neither a body nor an accident inhering in a body.
2
Understand the theologians' position
The theologians deny the existence of any possible being of this type besides Allah, but they have no proof against its possibility.
3
Analyze the gap
The standard proof for the world's temporality (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) only applies to bodies and accidents, not to this third division. Thus, it cannot be used to disprove such beings.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The theologians' claim that everything besides Allah is originated is incomplete. They must either disprove the existence of this third division or provide a proof for its origination.
They did neither, so their argument remains insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث من أقسام الموجودات ، وهو الموجود الذي لا يكون متحيزاً ، ولا حالاً في المتحيز . فقد ثبت بالدلائل اليقينية : أن الله سبحانه كذلك . وهل حصل في الممكنات موجود هذا شأنه، أم لا ؟ فالحكماء أثبتوه والمتكلمون أنكروه . وليس مع المتكلمين دليل يدل على فساد هذا القسم ، ودليلهم على حدوث العالم إنما يتناول المتحيزات والأعراض القائمة بها ، ولا يتناول هذا القسم الثالث ، فعلى هذا دعواهم أن كل ما سوى الله محدث ، إنما يتم إما بإبطال هذا القسم الثالث ، أو بذكر دليل يدل على حدوث هذا القسم الثالث بتقدير ثبوته . ولما لم يذكروا شيئاً في هذين المقامين ، بقي كلامهم غير تام في المقصود
As for the third division of existents, namely the existent that is neither a body nor an accident inhering in a body, it has been established by certain proofs that Allah, exalted, is such. And have there occurred among possible beings an existent of this nature or not? The philosophers affirmed it, and the theologians denied it. And the theologians have no proof indicating the invalidity of this division, and their proof for the origination of the world only encompasses bodies and the accidents inhering in them, and does not encompass this third division. Therefore, their claim that everything besides Allah is originated is only complete either by invalidating this third division, or by mentioning a proof that indicates the origination of this third division assuming its existence. Since they did not mention anything in these two positions, their statement remains incomplete regarding the intended purpose.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument about the third division of existents. It first notes that Allah is proven to be a non-corporeal, non-accident being. Then it asks whether any possible being shares this nature. The philosophers (ḥukamā') affirm the existence of such beings (like separate intellects), but the theologians (mutakallimūn) deny it. The author points out that the theologians have no proof against this division; their standard argument for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) only applies to bodies (mutahayyizāt) and accidents (aʿrāḍ), not to this third division. Therefore, their claim that everything other than Allah is originated is incomplete unless they either disprove the existence of such beings entirely or provide a proof that even this type of being is originated. Since they did neither, their argument remains inadequate.

Key Terms
متحيز
حال في المتحيز
حكماء
متكلمون
حدوث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the theologians' proof for the world's temporality automatically disproves non-material beings.
Correction: The author shows that this proof only applies to bodies and accidents, not to the third division of non-corporeal, non-accident existents.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the theologians' claim that everything besides Allah is originated is incomplete?
A) Because they did not prove the existence of Allah
B) Because they did not disprove the possible existence of non-corporeal beings or provide a proof for their origination
C) Because they relied on Greek philosophy
D) Because they denied the possibility of miracles

Key Takeaway
The theologians must address the third division of existents to complete their claim that everything other than Allah is originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi points out that the theologians left a gap in their proof. Is there an area of your own understanding of Allah that you have not fully examined? Will you seek to fill that gap, even if it means challenging what you thought was complete?
?
The philosophers asserted the existence of non-material beings, while the theologians denied them without evidence. Have you ever held a belief on a theological matter simply because it was the common view, without ensuring it was grounded in proof? What will you do about it now?
?
If your entire argument for a core Islamic belief is incomplete, as Sanusi shows here, would you be satisfied to leave it that way? What steps will you take to ensure your faith is built on sound reasoning, not just inherited positions?

The Second Proof: Time's Contingency Demands a Free Agent
The author presents the second proof that time's contingency implies a free agent who precedes time non-temporally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contingency of time implies a free agent who precedes time in a non-temporal manner.

Core Idea
Time's contingency proves its Creator must be a free, non-temporal agent.

The first proof used the contingency of bodies. Now the author turns to time itself. Time consists of successive parts that are each contingent. By a simple logical chain, the sum of time is contingent and requires an effect. That effect cannot be a mere necessitating cause, or time would be eternal and unchanging. Hence the effect must be a free agent who precedes time without being in time.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument structure
Recognize that this is a syllogistic proof with multiple premises and conclusions. The author builds step by step from the parts of time to the nature of the agent.
2
Identify the key premises
Note that each part of time is contingent ( since it occurs after non-existence ), the sum of contingent parts is contingent, and every contingent requires an effect (mu'aththir).
3
Examine the rejection of a necessitating cause
The author argues that if the effect were a necessitating cause, the effect would be eternal and unchanging, contradicting the nature of time. Follow this reductio ad absurdum.
This is the crucial step that forces the agent to be free.
4
Grasp the conclusion: free agent and non-temporal precedence
The effect must be a free agent. Such an agent must precede its act, but this precedence cannot be temporal because that would require time to exist before itself. Hence it is a non-temporal precedence.
5
Connect to the broader theology
This proof establishes that Allah is a free agent who exists before creation in a non-temporal sense, reinforcing Allah's transcendence over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لا شك أن الزمان عبارة عن أقسام متلاحقة ، وأجزاء متعاقبة . فنقول : كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء قد حدث بعد عدمه ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ممكن لذاته وكل واحد من أجزاء الزمان ممكن لذاته وكل ما كان كل واحد من أجزائه ممكناً لذاته ، فإن مجموعه أيضاً ممكن لذاته . ينتج : أن مجموع الزمان ممكن لذاته ، وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر ، فلمجموع الزمان مؤثر ، وذلك المؤثر إما موجب أو مختار ، والأول باطل . وإلا لدام المعلول بدوام ذات العلة ، فكان يلزم أن يكون الزمان موجوداً ، مستقر الذات ، مبرأ عن التبدل والتلاحق ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ليس بزمان ، ينتج : أنه لو كان المؤثر في وجود الزمان موجباً بالذات ، لزم أن لا يكون الزمان زماناً . وذلك محال . فيثبت : أن المؤثر في الزمان فاعل مختار ، والفاعل المختار لا بد وأن يتقدم على فعله . ينتج : أن فاعل الزمان متقدم على وجود الزمان . وذلك التقدم ليس بالزمان وإلا لزم أن يكون الزمان موجوداً ، حال عدمه . وذلك محال . فهذا نوع من التقدم ، مغاير للتقدم بالزمان ، ومغاير لسائر الأقسام المذكورة . وهو المطلوب
The second proof: There is no doubt that time consists of successive divisions and consecutive parts. So we say: Every one of those parts occurred after its non-existence, and everything that is like that is contingent in itself. And every one of the parts of time is contingent in itself. And if every one of its parts is contingent in itself, then the sum of them is also contingent in itself. It follows that the sum of time is contingent in itself, and every contingent in itself has an effect (mu'aththir). So the sum of time has an effect, and that effect is either necessitating (mūjib) or free (mukhtār). The first is false; otherwise the effect would persist with the persistence of the cause's essence, so it would entail that time is existent, stable in essence, exempt from change and succession, and everything like that is not time. It follows that if the effect (mu'aththir) in the existence of time were necessitating by essence, it would entail that time is not time. That is impossible. So it is established that the effect in time is a free agent (fā'il mukhtār), and the free agent must precede his act. It follows that the agent of time precedes the existence of time. And that precedence is not by time; otherwise it would entail that time is existent at the time of its non-existence. That is impossible. So this is a type of precedence, different from precedence by time, and different from the other mentioned divisions. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage gives the second proof. It argues that time is contingent because its parts are contingent. The sum of contingent things is contingent, so time as a whole is contingent and needs an effect (mu'aththir). That effect cannot be a mere necessitating cause (mūjib), because that would make the effect eternal, but time is not eternal. So the effect must be a free agent (mukhtār) who acts voluntarily. A free agent must precede his action. This precedence is not temporal because if it were, time would exist before itself. Therefore, there is a non-temporal precedence of the agent over time. This is the sixth type.

Key Terms
ممكن لذاته
مؤثر
موجب
مختار
فاعل مختار
تقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the agent of time must be within time or that temporal precedence is the only kind.
Correction: The proof shows that the agent must precede time non-temporally, establishing a distinct type of precedence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second proof regarding the agent of time?
A) The agent is a necessary cause
B) The agent is a free chooser who precedes time non-temporally
C) The agent is time itself
D) The agent is contingent

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes that the Creator is a free agent who exists before time in a non-temporal sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time itself is contingent and depends on a free Creator, then every second of your life is sustained by His will. How does that truth confront your anxiety about the future or your regrets about the past? What would it look like to live today with the awareness that your time is held by a free, not a forced, God?
?
The proof shows that the Creator is not bound by time. He sees your entire life as a single present. How does this challenge your excuse 'I'll repent later' or 'God will understand my delay'? What is one decision you have been postponing that you will act on now because you recognize that your time is not your own?
?
You are a creature of time, but your Creator chose to create you freely—not because He needed to, but because He willed. Does your life reflect gratitude for that free gift, or do you live as if God owed you existence? What will you change today to honor the freedom of your Creator?

Third Proof: Time Is Either Originated or Eternal
The author presents the third proof using the alternatives that time is either originated or eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that time's own precedence cannot be temporal, whether time is originated or eternal.

Core Idea
Time cannot be used to explain its own precedence.

In this third proof, the author examines the nature of time itself. The argument considers two possibilities for time—that it is originated or eternal—and shows that in both cases the precedence involved is not temporal. This challenges the assumption that all precedence must be measured by time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the argument is structured as two conditional cases leading to a concluding question.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze each alternative
Examine the reasoning for each alternative: If time is originated, its non-existence precedes its existence, but that precedence cannot be temporal. If time is eternal, its persistence is not by time, otherwise infinite regress.
Think about why the precedence cannot be temporal in either case.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The final rhetorical question asks: if non-temporal persistence is conceivable, why not non-temporal origination? Consider how this supports the idea that the existence of the world may not require time.
This conclusion is key for later proofs about the world's beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الزمان إما أن يكون حادثاً أو قديماً، فإن كان حادثاً كان عدمه سابقاً على وجوده ، وذلك السبق ليس إلا بالزمان ، فقد حصل سبق العدم على الوجود من غير حصول الزمان ، وإن كان قديماً كان إستمراره ليس بالزمان ، وإلا لزم إفتقار الزمان إلى زمان آخر ، وإذا عقل إستمرار الشيء لا بسبب الزمان ، فلم لا يعقل أيضاً حدوثه لا بسبب الزمان ؟
The third proof: Time is either originated (ḥādith) or eternal (qadīm). If it is originated, then its non-existence precedes its existence, and that precedence is only by time, so there occurs a precedence of non-existence over existence without the occurrence of time. And if it is eternal, then its persistence is not by time; otherwise it would entail that time depends on another time. And if the persistence of a thing without time is conceivable, why would its origination without time not be conceivable?
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage gives the third proof by considering two possibilities for time: it is either originated (ḥādith) or eternal (qadīm). If time is originated, then its non-existence precedes its existence, but that precedence itself cannot be by time because that would regress. If time is eternal, its persistence is not by time; otherwise it would require another time. So in both cases, we have a type of precedence that is not temporal. The argument concludes that if non-temporal persistence is conceivable, then non-temporal origination is also conceivable.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
سبق
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All precedence must be temporal, measured by time.
Correction: The proof shows that for time itself, its own precedence cannot be temporal, so non-temporal precedence is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the key insight of the third proof?
A) Time must be eternal
B) Time must be originated
C) The precedence of time's non-existence over its existence cannot be temporal
D) Time depends on another time

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that time's own origin cannot be temporal, making non-temporal origination conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is a created thing, not an absolute container, then your past sins are not merely 'behind you' but are fully present before the Creator of time. How does this change your urgency to repent today, rather than postponing it? Commit to one specific repentance action right now.
?
The proof shows that origination without time is conceivable. Allah originated time itself without time. Are you measuring your spiritual progress by time (e.g., 'I've been Muslim for X years') rather than by genuine transformation? What is one non-time-based measure of your faith you can focus on this week?
?
If time depends on Allah, then your use of time is a direct trust from Him. How does this accountability make you feel about the hours you waste? Write down one time-wasting habit you will replace with an act of obedience starting today.

Fifth Proof: Priority and Posteriority Without Time
Mulla Sadra argues that God and the world cannot be related by priority without time, lest God be contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority, simultaneity, and posteriority between God and the world do not imply time, and how this preserves God's necessary existence.

Core Idea
Time is not needed for priority; otherwise God would be contingent.

This proof begins with an intuitive axiom: any two things the intellect considers are either prior, simultaneous, or posterior. There is no fourth possibility. Applying this to God and the world, the argument shows that if these relations required time, God would depend on time, making Him contingent—an impossibility. Therefore, these relations are non-temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the repeated 'when this is established' – the argument builds step by step.
2
Identify the axiom and its three divisions
The passage opens with an axiom: for any two things the intellect considers, one is prior, simultaneous, or posterior. Find this and note that there is no fourth option.
This is a purely rational classification, not dependent on time.
3
Follow the conditional argument
Assume these relations require time. Since God and the world must fall under one of them, God would need time for these relations, implying contingency.
Contingency contradicts God's necessary existence, so the assumption is false.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to impossibility, the relations do not depend on time. Time is not intrinsic to priority, simultaneity, or posteriority.
This proof uses reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite, derive contradiction, then conclude the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في تقرير هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : صريح العقل حاكم بأن كل أمرين يشير العقل إليهما ، فإما أن يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر ، أو معه أو بعده . فأما أن يكون خارجاً عن الأقسام الثلاثة ، فهذا غير معقول ، بل هو مدفوع في بديهة العقل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : البارىء والعالم لا بد وأن يكونا على أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن كانت القبلية والمعية والبعدية مما لا يحصل إلا بسبب الزمان ، وقد دللنا على أن ذات يمتنع خلوها عن هذه الأحوال الثلاثة ، فحينئذ تكون ذات الله تعالى مفتقراً في بعض لوازمه إلى وجود غيره وكل ما كان مفتقراً في وجوده إلى وجود غيره كان ممكناً لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وهو محال . فيثبت : أن حصول القبلية والمعية والبعدية لا يفتقر البتة إلى الزمان . وذلك هو المطلوب
The fifth proof in establishing this thesis: we say: The clear intellect judges that for every two things to which the intellect points, either one is prior to the other, or with it, or after it. As for it being outside the three divisions, that is unintelligible, rather it is rejected by the intuition of the intellect. When this is established, we say: The Creator and the world must be in one of these three divisions. When this is established, we say: If priority, simultaneity, and posteriority do not occur except because of time, and we have shown that the Essence cannot be devoid of these three states, then the Essence of God Most High would be in need, in some of its concomitants, of the existence of something else; and everything that is in need in its existence of the existence of something else is contingent in itself. So it would follow that the Necessary of Existence in itself would be contingent in itself, and that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of priority, simultaneity, and posteriority does not depend at all on time. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It begins with an axiom: the intellect recognizes that any two things are either prior, simultaneous, or posterior. There is no fourth possibility. Applying this to God and the world, they must fall under one of these relations. If these relations only occur through time, and we have proven that God cannot be free of them (since He relates to the world), then God would depend on something else (time) for these states, making Him contingent. Since that is impossible, the relations do not require time. Thus time is not essential for priority, simultaneity, or posteriority.

Key Terms
القبلية
المعية
البعدية
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority always imply time.
Correction: The proof shows that these relations can exist without time; otherwise God would be contingent, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the fifth proof conclude that priority, simultaneity, and posteriority do not require time?
A) Because God is eternal and timeless
B) Because otherwise God would need time, making Him contingent
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of time
D) Because the world is temporal

Key Takeaway
Priority, simultaneity, and posteriority between God and the world do not depend on time; otherwise God would be contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'before' all creation, do you unconsciously imagine a timeline? What must you let go of to truly accept that His priority is not temporal? Are you willing to restructure your mental picture of God even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If being in need of anything for your existence makes you contingent, how does this lesson deepen your awareness of God's absolute independence? Do you live as though God needs your worship, or as though you need Him? What would change if you truly felt His independence in every moment?
?
This proof shows that rational relations like priority do not require time. How often do you limit God by your own concepts of time and sequence? What practical step can you take today to worship God as the Eternal who transcends your mental categories?

Refutation of the Need Argument (47:38)
Sanusi refutes the verse 47:38 as proof for creation of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy' does not prove the temporal creation of essences, and how the concept of need applies only to attributes, not to essences themselves.

Core Idea
Need applies to qualities, not essences; essences are possible in themselves.

Opponents of the philosophical position often cite Quranic verses to argue that all things, including their essences, are created by God. One such verse is 'Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy.' In this lesson, we examine Sanusi's refutation of this argument, showing that the verse only establishes need in terms of attributes, not the essences themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the claim
Read the Arabic verse and the opponents' claim that it proves need of the world for the Creator in existence. Understand the literal meaning.
The verse is from Surah Muhammad, verse 38.
2
Understand Sanusi's first response
Sanusi says that need applies only to the acquisition of specific attributes, not to the essence. Reflect on the distinction between essence and attributes.
Attributes are accidental; essence is what a thing is in itself.
3
Grasp the second response from philosophy
According to the philosophers, essences are possible in themselves but necessary through their cause. This suffices for need without implying temporal origination.
The concept of need here is ontological dependence, not temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ الرابع: قوله تعالى: {والله الغني وأنتم الفقراء} وهذا أيضاً لا يدل على هذا المطلوب، لأن العالم يحتاج إلى البارىء في حصول الصفات المخصوصة والنعوت المخصوصة. وهذا القدر يكفي في حصول الحاجة. وأيضاً: فالذوات عند القوم ممكنة لذواتها، واجبة بسبب وجوب علتها، وهذا يكفي في حصول معنى الحاجة. اللفظ الخامس: قوله تعالى: {هو الأول} قالوا: والأول هو الفرد السابق. وهذا يدل على أنه لم يوجد في الأزل مع الله غيره. وهذا أيضاً لا يفيد هذا المقصود، لأنه ليس من شرط كونه أولاً أن يكون أولاً لكل شيء، لأن اللفظ المهمل في جانب الثبوت يكفي في حصوله حصول مسماه في فرد واحد. وأيضاً: فبتقدير أنه يجب أن يكون أولاً لكل ما سواه، لكن القوم يقولون: العالم ممكن لذاته، واجب بوجوب علته، والعلة سابقة على المعلول بالعلية وبالذات، فيكون أولاً لكل ما سواه بهذا التفسير. اللفظ السادس: قوله تعالى: {إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون} والمراد بقوله: كن: نفاذ القدرة والإرادة، فدل على أنه تعالى كون الأشياء بقدرته، وهذا أيضاً لا يدل على هذا المطلوب، لأن هذه الآية تدل على أن كل ما أراد الله تكوينه فإنه يكون بهذا الطريق، وعلى هذا الوجه، فلم قلتم: إنه تعالى أراد تكوين الذوات وتكوين الأجسام؟ وهل النزاع إلا فيه؟ فيثبت بهذه البيانات: أنه ليس في القرآن ما يدل على حدوث الذوات.
And the fourth expression: His statement, the Exalted: {And Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy} (47:38). This also does not indicate this sought [point], because the world needs the Creator in the acquisition of the specific attributes and specific qualities. And this amount suffices for the acquisition of need. Also, the essences according to the people [the philosophers] are possible in themselves, necessary due to the necessity of their cause, and this suffices for the acquisition of the meaning of need. The fifth expression: His statement, the Exalted: {He is the First} (57:3). They said: And the First is the solitary precedent. And this indicates that in pre-eternity there was nothing else with Allah. And this also does not benefit this intended [point], because it is not a condition of His being First that He be first of everything, since an unqualified word on the side of affirmation suffices for its occurrence by the occurrence of its meaning in a single individual. Also, even if it were necessary that He be first of everything else, the people [the philosophers] say: the world is possible in itself, necessary by the necessity of its cause, and the cause precedes the effect by causation and by essence, so He would be first of everything else by this interpretation. The sixth expression: His statement, the Exalted: {Indeed, Our saying to a thing when We intend it is to say to it: 'Be,' and it becomes} (16:40). And what is intended by His saying 'Be' is the execution of power and will, and it indicates that Allah, the Exalted, brings things into being by His power. And this also does not indicate this sought [point], because this verse indicates that everything that Allah wills to bring into being becomes in this way, and in this manner. So why do you assert that He, the Exalted, willed to bring into being the essences and the bodies? And is not the dispute only about that? So it is established by these evidences that there is nothing in the Quran that indicates the temporal origination of the essences.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage refutes three Quranic verses that are alleged to prove the temporal creation (ḥudūth) of the essences (dhawāt) themselves. The argument is structured as a series of refutations: each verse is examined and shown not to entail the required conclusion. The author follows the order of the 'expressions' (alfāẓ) cited by opponents. First, the verse {And Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy} (47:38) is interpreted by opponents as implying that the world needs Allah for its existence, thus proving creation. The author replies that need (ḥājah) applies only to the acquisition of specific attributes and qualities, not to the essence itself. Moreover, according to the philosophical position (al-qawm, i.e., the falāsifa), essences are possible in themselves (mumkin li-dhātihā) but necessary through the necessity of their cause; this suffices for the meaning of need without requiring temporal origination. Second, the verse {He is the First} (57:3) is taken to mean that nothing co-exists with Him in pre-eternity. The author points out that being first does not require being first of every individual thing; it suffices that He is first in one sense. Even if it did require being first of all else, the philosophers argue that the effect is caused after the cause in essence and causation, so God is first in that sense, but this does not prove that the world's essence is created after non-existence. Third, the verse {Indeed, Our saying to a thing...} (16:40) is used to show that God creates by His command. The author notes that the verse only says that when God wills something, it happens; it does not specify that He willed to create the essences themselves. The dispute is precisely whether He willed to create essences or merely to give them forms. Thus, the Quran does not explicitly teach the creation of essences.

Key Terms
الذوات (al-dhawāt)
الحاجة (al-ḥājah)
الإمكان (al-imkān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy' proves that everything other than God, including essences, is temporally created.
Correction: The verse only indicates need for specific attributes, not for the essence itself. Moreover, ontological dependence does not necessitate temporal origination.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the verse {And Allah is the Free of need, and you are the needy} not prove the temporal creation of essences?
A) Because the verse is metaphorical and not literal.
B) Because need applies only to attributes, not to essences.
C) Because the verse refers to spiritual poverty, not physical creation.
D) Because the philosophers deny that the world needs God.

Key Takeaway
The verse only indicates need for attributes, not essences; hence it does not prove temporal creation of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that you are independent in your good qualities—your intelligence, patience, or generosity—as if they come from you? If you really need God for every specific attribute, how must you now pray and act today?
?
The philosophers claim you exist by God's necessity, not your own. Does your daily life reflect a constant awareness that at this very moment you are possible in yourself and only sustained by God? If not, what one habit can you change to live in that reality?
?
When you read 'you are the needy' in the Quran, did you previously assume it meant you were created from nothing? Now that Sanusi shows it means you depend on God for every quality you possess, what should change in how you view your own accomplishments and failures? Will you start your next task by acknowledging that God is the one who provides the ability?

Refutation of the 'First' Argument (57:3)
Sanusi refutes the verse 57:3 as proof for creation of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'He is the First' does not necessarily imply that God created all essences temporally, and how priority can be interpreted in terms of causation rather than time.

Core Idea
Being first does not require temporal priority over every individual thing.

Another verse cited to prove creation of essences is 'He is the First' (57:3), taken to mean that nothing co-exists with God in pre-eternity. Sanusi refutes this by showing that 'first' can apply in a restricted sense, and even if it applied to everything, the philosophers' view of causal priority suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the opponents' interpretation
Read the Arabic verse and understand the claim: 'The First' means that nothing else existed with God in pre-eternity, implying all else is created.
The opponents assume 'first' implies temporal priority over all other things.
2
Analyze Sanusi's first point
Sanusi argues that for the word 'First' to be true, it suffices that God is first in some respect; it does not require being first of every individual thing.
Unqualified affirmation is satisfied by a single instance.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
Even if we require God to be first of everything else, the philosophers hold that the cause precedes the effect by essence and causation, not necessarily by time. Thus, God is first in that sense.
Causal priority does not entail temporal creation.

Refutation of the 'Be' Argument (16:40)
Sanusi refutes the verse 16:40 as proof for creation of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Our saying to a thing when We intend it is to say to it: Be, and it becomes' does not prove that God willed to create essences temporally, and that the dispute is precisely whether essences are created or not.

Core Idea
The verse only shows God's power over events, not that He willed to create essences.

The last of the three verses is 'Indeed, Our saying to a thing when We intend it is to say to it: Be, and it becomes' (16:40). Opponents see this as a proof that God creates everything, including essences, by His command. Sanusi argues that the verse only indicates that when God intends something, it happens, but it does not specify that He intended to create the essences themselves. The dispute is precisely over that point.

The verse is general but does not specify the object of will.
3

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Be, and it becomes' proves that all things, including their essences, are created temporally by God's command.
Correction: The verse only shows that when God wills something, it happens; it does not specify that He willed to create essences. The dispute is over the object of His will.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the main reason the verse {Be, and it becomes} does not prove the temporal creation of essences?
A) Because the verse is about a specific historical event, not a general principle.
B) Because the verse does not say that God willed to create the essences themselves.
C) Because the verse is metaphorical and not literal.
D) Because the philosophers deny the power of God.

Key Takeaway
The 'Be' verse does not prove creation of essences because it remains unspecified what God willed to create; the Quran as a whole does not assert temporal creation of essences.

The Torah Does Not Prove Creation of Essences
Sanusi analyzes the Torah's opening verses to argue against an ex nihilo interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's analysis of the Torah's creation account demonstrates that scripture does not explicitly teach the creation of essences from nothing.

Core Idea
Scripture describes organization, not necessarily creation from nothing.

Sanusi now examines the Torah, the scripture of the Jews and Christians, to test whether it supports the claim that essences were created ex nihilo. He quotes the opening verses and shows that each phrase can be interpreted as describing the formation of pre-existing matter rather than absolute creation. This passage deepens the argument that even the most authoritative scriptures lack explicit proof for the temporal origination of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah passage
Read the Arabic quote from the Torah and its translation. Note the key phrases: 'created', 'darkness', 'spirit hovering'.
The Torah text is taken from Genesis 1:1-3.
2
Analyze each phrase
Identify how Sanusi interprets each of the three phrases: 'created' does not imply ex nihilo, 'darkness' indicates pre-existent matter, and 'spirit hovering' suggests motion of pre-existent particles.
Sanusi connects each interpretation to existing positions among theologians.
3
Understand the conclusion
Recognize that Sanusi concludes that neither the Quran nor the Torah explicitly states creation of essences out of nothing, highlighting the difficulty of the issue.
This conclusion undermines opponents who rely solely on scripture for proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التوراة، فقال في أوله: «أول ما خلق الله السماء والأرض، وكانت الأرض خربة خاوية، وكانت الظلمة على الغمر، وريح الله تهب وترف على وجه الماء. فقال الله: ليكـن نـور، فكان نور» هذا لفظ التوراة. فقوله: «خلق الله السماء والأرض» لا يدل على إيجاد تلك الذوات والأجزاء، على ما بيناه. وقوله: «وكانت الظلمة على الغمر» يدل على أنه كان في الظلمة، وهذا عين قول من يقول: إن تلك الأجزاء كانت مظلمة، لأن الظلمة عدم النور عما من شأنه أن يستنير، فتكون الظلمة سابقة لا محالة على النور. وقوله: «وريح الله تهب وترف على وجه الماء» عين قول من يقول: إنه تعالى حرك تلك الأجزاء بعد أن كانت ساكنة، وأظهر فيها النور بعد أن كانت في الأزل مظلمة. فيثبت بهذه البيانات: أنه ليس في القرآن ولا في التوراة لفظ يدل بصريحه على أن هذه الذوات حادثة بعد عدمها، كائنة بعد أن كانت نفياً محضاً وسلباً صرفاً. ولا شك أن هذين الكتابين أعظم الكتب الإلهية، فلما خلا هذان الكتابان عن التصريح بهذا المطلوب، أوهم ذلك أن هذه المسألة بلغت في الصعوبة إلى حيث صارت في محل الصعوبة.
As for the Torah, it says at its beginning: 'In the beginning God created the heavens and the earth, and the earth was formless and void, and darkness was over the deep, and the spirit of God hovered over the face of the waters. And God said: Let there be light, and there was light.' This is the wording of the Torah. His statement: 'God created the heavens and the earth' does not indicate the bringing into existence of those essences and particles, as we have explained. And His statement: 'And darkness was over the deep' indicates that it was in darkness, and this is exactly the saying of those who say that those particles were dark, because darkness is the absence of light from something that has the potential to be illuminated, so darkness necessarily preceded light. And His statement: 'And the spirit of God hovered over the face of the waters' is exactly the saying of those who say that He, the Exalted, set those particles in motion after they had been at rest, and manifested light in them after they were in pre-eternity dark. So it is established by these evidences that there is in neither the Quran nor the Torah any explicit wording indicating that these essences are originated after their non-existence, coming into being after they were pure non-being and absolute privation. And there is no doubt that these two books are the greatest of the divine books, and since these two books lack explicit statement about this sought [point], this suggests that this question has reached such difficulty that it has come to a place of difficulty.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage examines the opening verses of the Torah (specifically Genesis) to see if they explicitly teach the creation of the essences (dhawāt) ex nihilo. The author quotes the Torah text: 'In the beginning God created the heavens and the earth, and the earth was formless and void, and darkness was over the deep, and the spirit of God hovered over the face of the waters. And God said: Let there be light, and there was light.' He then analyzes each phrase. First, 'God created the heavens and the earth' does not necessarily imply the creation of the essences themselves; it could refer to giving form to pre-existing matter. Second, 'darkness was over the deep' indicates that darkness (absence of light) existed prior to light, which is exactly the position of those who say that particles were dark before God brought light. Third, 'the spirit of God hovered over the face of the waters' is interpreted as God setting previously static particles in motion and manifesting light in them. Thus, the Torah does not explicitly state that essences were created out of nothing; it can be read as describing the organization of pre-existing matter. The author then concludes that neither the Quran nor the Torah contains explicit wording for the creation of essences after non-existence. This suggests that the issue is extremely difficult and subtle. The passage thus serves to undermine the opponents' reliance on scripture for proving the temporal creation of essences.

الظلمة (al-ẓulmah)

السكون والحركة (al-sukūn wa al-ḥarakah)

Correction: Sanusi shows that 'creation' can mean organization of pre-existing matter, as the Torah's context of darkness and motion implies pre-existent elements.

Key Takeaway
Scripture does not explicitly state that essences were created from nothing; even the Torah's account can be interpreted as the ordering of pre-existent matter.

Answering the Objection about the Eternity of Essences and Prophethood
Answer to objection about prophethood and eternity of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of prophethood even while holding the eternity of essences, by proving God's existence from the diversity of attributes among those essences.

Core Idea
Prove God from diversity, not from creation.

This passage presents a powerful objection: if the world is eternal, how can we prove God's existence, and thus prophethood? The author responds by shifting the argument from origination to diversity. Even if essences are eternal, their varying attributes require a free chooser. Once God is established, prophethood follows. This lesson will walk through that argument and its implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's claim: prophethood depends on divinity, but if the world is eternal, divinity is undermined, so how can prophethood be established?
Notice the logical link the objector is making: no God → no prophethood.
2
Understand the author's alternative proof
The author does not rely on the creation of essences. Instead, he points to the diversity of attributes among essences (some subtle, some dense). Ask yourself: why this diversity requires a free chooser?
This is the key insight: variety in eternal things points to a cause external to them.
3
See how prophethood follows
Once God's existence is proven, the possibility of prophethood can be built upon that foundation. The author asserts this briefly.
The argument does not prove actual prophethood here, only that it is not impossible.
4
Reflect on the prophets' silence
The author notes that the greatest prophets avoided this topic. Why? Because it is extremely difficult for human intellects. This is a humble conclusion.
This reminds us that not all theological questions need to be answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: النبوة فرع على الإلهية. فمن قال بقدم العالم، لزمه القدح في الإلهية، فكيف يستقيم معه إثبات النبوة؟
فنقول: لمن قال إن الذوات قديمة إنا لا نستدل بوجود تلك الذوات على وجود الإله القادر الحكيم. بل نقول إن تلك الذوات اختلفت في الصفات، فبعضها لطيفة علوية، وبعضها كثيفة سفلية، فاختلافها في هذه الصفات لا بد وأن يكون لأجل القادر المختار. وإذا ثبت وجود الإله بهذا الطريق، فحينئذ يمكن تفريع القول بالنبوة عليه.
فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن أكابر الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - سكتوا عن الخوض في هذه المسألة، وذلك يدل على أنها بلغت في الصعوبة إلى حيث تعجز العقول البشرية عن الوصول إليها. والله أعلم.
If a questioner says: Prophethood is a branch of divinity. So whoever holds the eternity of the world necessarily impugns divinity, so how can he establish prophethood? Then we say: To one who says that essences are eternal, we do not deduce from the existence of those essences the existence of the powerful, wise God. Rather, we say that those essences differ in attributes: some are subtle and heavenly, some are dense and earthly. So their difference in these attributes must be due to the free chooser. And when the existence of God is established by this path, then it is possible to base the statement on prophethood upon it. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the greatest prophets—peace be upon them—refrained from delving into this issue, and that indicates that it has reached a degree of difficulty such that human intellects are incapable of reaching it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that prophethood (nubuwwa) is dependent on divinity (ilāhiyya); if one holds the eternity of the world (qidam al-'ālam), this would undermine divinity, so how could one establish prophethood? The author responds by stating that we do not prove God's existence from the existence of essences themselves, which the opponent claims are eternal. Instead, we prove God's existence from the differing attributes of those essences: some are subtle and heavenly, others dense and earthly. This variety cannot arise without a free and powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār). Once God's existence is established through this argument, prophethood can be built upon it as a further step. The passage concludes by noting that the greatest prophets avoided discussing this question, which shows its extreme difficulty beyond human reason.

الإلهية (al-ilāhiyya)

القادر المختار (al-qādir al-mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the world is eternal, then God cannot exist because there is no creation from nothing.
Correction: The author shows that God can be proven from the diversity of eternal essences, not from their origination. Thus, God's existence is independent of whether the world is created or eternal.

Checkpoint

B) By proving God's existence from the differing attributes of essences

Key Takeaway
The diversity of attributes among eternal essences proves the existence of a free chooser, allowing prophethood to be established despite the eternity of the world.

Proof of God through Differences in Attributes
Argues that differing attributes of essences prove a divine chooser, then notes prophets' silence indicates the issue's difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of attributes among eternal essences leads to the necessity of a divine chooser, and why the prophets' silence on this matter signals its profound difficulty.

Core Idea
Diversity in creation demands a Chooser, not mere existence.

This passage presents a subtle proof for the existence of God. Rather than arguing from the mere existence of eternal essences, it focuses on their differing attributes. This difference cannot be accidental—it must be caused by a powerful, wise Chooser. The author then adds a remarkable observation: the greatest prophets refrained from discussing this issue, highlighting its intellectual challenge. Let's explore how the argument unfolds and what it teaches us about divine existence and human reason.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
Read the opening sentence: 'The essences are eternal.' This is the foundation—the author affirms the eternity of essences (a point from previous discussions).
This sets the stage for the argument; it is not the proof itself.
2
Identify the core argument
Notice that the author explicitly rejects proving God from the mere existence of essences. Instead, he points to their differing attributes (some subtle, some dense) and argues that such difference must be caused by a powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār).
This is the key move: diversity implies a cause.
3
See how prophethood connects
The author then states that once God's existence is established, one can build upon it to prove prophethood. This shows the interconnectedness of theological topics.
4
Reflect on the prophets' silence
Finally, consider the observation that the great prophets avoided delving into this issue. Why? The author says it indicates extreme difficulty—human intellects cannot fully grasp it. This is a humbling note about the limits of reason.
This is not a proof but a supportive remark.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And God knows best.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on the diversity of attributes in essences, and then uses the prophets' silence as evidence of the issue's difficulty. The author first states a premise: essences are eternal. But he clarifies that we do not prove God directly from the existence of these essences. Instead, he argues that although these essences are all eternal, they differ in attributes—some are subtle and celestial, others dense and terrestrial. This difference cannot be arbitrary but must be caused by a powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār). Thus, the existence of God is established. Once God's existence is proven, the author says, one can build upon it the proof of prophethood. Finally, he notes that the greatest prophets remained silent about delving into this matter, which demonstrates that the issue is extremely difficult for human intellects to grasp.

The Possibility of the World: Has It a Beginning?
Sanusi presents the disagreement and the first proof against the beginning of the world's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the possibility of the world's existence has no beginning, based on the immutability of essences.

Core Idea
The nature of a thing's possibility cannot change because its essence is immutable.

This passage introduces a critical debate among Muslim theologians: does the possibility of the world's existence have a beginning? Some argue that if possibility had a beginning, it would imply an impossible change in the world's essence. We will examine their reasoning and the first proof that the essence does not change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to OCR issues.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage states that those who affirm the world's temporality disagree: some say possibility has no beginning, others say it has a beginning.
Focus on the first group's argument.
3
Understand the reductio argument
The first group argues by contradiction: if possibility had a beginning, before that beginning possibility was absent. Then the world would have been either necessary or impossible. Both lead to absurdity.
Necessity implies eternity; impossibility implies change of essence.
4
Reflect on the principle of essence
The first proof states that the requirement of the essence does not change. If a thing is impossible in itself, it can never become possible.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الخامسة
في
أن هذا العالم. هل له أول أم لا؟
القائلون بأنه يمتنع كون العالم قديماً ، ويجب كونه محدثاً : أضطربوا في إمكان وجود العالم . هل له أول ، أم لا ؟ فمنهم من قال : إنه لا أول لهذا الإمكان . ومنهم من قال : له أول
·
أما الذاهبون إلى القول الأول فقالوا : لو كذب قولنا : لا أول ، لإمكان العالم ، لصدق نقيضه ، وهو أنه لا بد لذلك الإمكان من أول . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لزم أن يقال : إن قبل ذلك الأول، كان الإمكان مفقوداً، كان الحاصل، أما الوجوب بالذات أو الامتناع بالذات فإن كان الأول، كان القول بالقدم لازم ، وإن كان الثاني لزم أن يقال : العالم كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته [ ثم انقلب ممكن الوجود لذاته ] وهذا باطل . والذي يدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : وهو أن كل ما كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه ولذاته امتنع أن يقبل الوجود البتة ، لأن مقتضى الماهية لا يتبدل ولا يتغير ، فإن كانت الماهية مقتضية لعدم قبول الوجود ، وجب أن تكون أبداً كذلك ، وإن كانت مقتضية لقبول الوجود ، وجب أن تكون [ أبداً ] كذلك . فيثبت : أن العالم لو صدق
The Fifth Introduction: That this world—does it have a beginning or not? Those who say that it is impossible for the world to be eternal and that it must be originated have disagreed concerning the possibility of the existence of the world: does it have a beginning or not? Some of them said: There is no beginning to this possibility. And some of them said: It has a beginning. As for those who adopt the first statement, they said: If our statement 'there is no beginning to the possibility of the world' were false, then its contradictory would be true, namely that there must be a beginning for that possibility. If this is the case, then it necessarily follows that before that beginning, possibility was absent. What was attained was either necessity by essence or impossibility by essence. If the first, then the statement of eternity is necessary. If the second, then it must be said: The world was impossible of existence in itself, then it turned into possible of existence in itself—and this is false. That which indicates this are proofs: The first proof: That everything which is impossible of existence in its very essence and itself is absolutely impossible to receive existence, because the requirement of the essence does not change nor alter. If the essence necessitates non-reception of existence, then it must always be thus. And if it necessitates reception of existence, then it must always be thus. So it is established: that if the world is...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a definition of a key debate in kalam about whether the world has a beginning. It opens the 'Fifth Introduction' concerning the world's temporal origination. The author states that those who affirm the world's temporal origination (muhdath) have disagreed about the status of the world's possibility (imkan). Specifically, some say this possibility has no beginning, while others say it has a beginning. The passage then presents the argument of those who say the possibility has no beginning. They reason by contradiction: if the possibility of the world had a beginning, then before that beginning possibility was absent. At that time, the world would have been either necessary by essence (wajib bi al-dhat) or impossible by essence (mumtani' bi al-dhat). If it were necessary, then the world would be eternal, contradicting its temporal origin. If it were impossible, then it would have to change from impossible to possible, which is impossible because the essence (mahiyya) of a thing does not change. Thus, the conclusion is that the possibility has no beginning. This leads to the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) which establishes the principle that the nature of an essence cannot change. The argument is that if something is impossible in itself, it can never become possible. The same applies if it is possible. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intent is to show that if the world were impossible, it could never become possible, so it must have been possible from eternity.

Key Terms
إمكان العالم
الوجوب بالذات
الامتناع بالذات
الماهية
مقتضى الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of something can change over time, so the world could have become possible after being impossible.
Correction: The passage argues that the requirement of the essence (muqtada al-mahiyya) is immutable: if a thing is impossible in itself, it can never become possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the problem if the world were impossible by essence before its possibility began?
A) It would require a change from impossibility to possibility, which is impossible.
B) It would mean the world is eternal.
C) It would mean possibility has no beginning.
D) It would contradict God's power.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world's existence has no beginning because if it did, it would imply an impossible change in the world's essential nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument states that the essence of a thing cannot change. Consider your own soul—its fundamental nature. Do you believe your soul's essence is fixed? If so, how does that affect your view of personal change, repentance, and growth? What does this mean for your accountability before Allah?
?
If the possibility of the world had a beginning, it would mean the world's essence changed. Has there been a time in your life when you thought something was impossible for you, yet you later achieved it? Did your essence change, or was it always possible? The passage distinguishes between necessity, impossibility, and possibility by essence. Think of an attribute you believe is essential to your identity. Objection to the Eternity of Possibility and Its Response
The author presents an objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that a beginningless possibility implies an eternal world, by distinguishing between possibility and actuality conditioned by non-existence.

Core Idea
Beginningless possibility does not mean actual beginninglessness.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the idea that possibility can be eternal, and the author's careful response. The objectors argue that if the possibility of the world's existence has no beginning, then God could have created it eternally, forcing the world to be eternal. The author answers by clarifying that the possibility is for a world that is preceded by non-existence, which prevents it from being eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objectors claim that if the possibility of the world's existence has no first, and the possibility of God's power's effect also has no first, then there is no barrier to God creating the world from eternity.
2
Identify the problem
This objection would imply the world is eternal, which is considered absurd. They argue it leads to a contradiction.
3
Read the author's response
The author responds by noting that when a thing is conditioned by being preceded by non-existence, its possibility can be beginningless, but that does not make the thing itself eternal.
The condition of non-existence prevents actual eternity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, the objection fails because it conflates beginningless possibility with actual beginninglessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بإثبات الأول لهذا الإمكان. فقد إحتجوا عليه، وقالوا: إنه إذا كان لا أول لإمكان وجود العالم في نفسه، ولا أول أيضاً لإمكان تأثير قدرة الله تعالى فيه، فعلى هذا التقدير، لا امتناع في تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في وجود العالم من الأزل إلى الأبد. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لزم القطع بأنـه امتناع في كون العالم موجوداً في الأزل، ولا امتناع في كونه تعالى موجداً للعالم في الأزل. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان القول بأن العالم يمتنع أن يكون قديماً ذلك القول، يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. وأيضاً: فالفعل ما له أول، والأزل ما لا أول له، والجمع بينهما محال. لا واعلم أن هذا الكلام مشكل جداً، وقد أجبنا عنه في كتاب «الأربعين» فقلنا: إنا إذا وجدنا الشيء المعين، بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم، فإنه مع هذا الشرط لا أول لصحة وجوده، ثم لا يلزم من قولنا: لا أول لصحة وجوده مع هذا الشرط، صدق قولنا: إنه أن يكون أزلياً، لأنه مع شرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، يمتنع أن يكون أزلياً فيثبت أنه لا يلزم من قولنا: إنه لا أول لصحة وجوده، قولنا: إنه يصح أن يكون أزلياً.
As for those who affirm a first for this possibility, they have argued against it, saying: If there is no first for the possibility of the existence of the world in itself, and also no first for the possibility of the effect of God's power upon it, then on this estimation, there is no impediment to the effect of God's power in the existence of the world from eternity to eternity. And if the matter is so, then it necessarily follows that there is no impediment to the world's being existent in eternity, and no impediment to His being the one who brings the world into existence in eternity. And if the matter is so, then the statement that the world cannot be eternal would necessitate combining two opposites, which is impossible. Also: the act (al-fi'l) has a first, and eternity (al-azal) has no first, and combining them is impossible. No. Know that this speech is very problematic, and we have answered it in the book 'al-Arba'in'. We said: Indeed, when we find a specific thing, on condition that it is preceded by non-existence, then with this condition there is no first for the soundness of its existence. Then it does not follow from our statement 'there is no first for the soundness of its existence' the truth of our statement that it can be eternal, because with the condition of being preceded by non-existence, it is impossible for it to be eternal. So it is established that it does not follow from our statement 'there is no first for the soundness of its existence' our statement 'it is possible for it to be eternal'.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response. The objectors argue that if the possibility of the world's existence is beginningless, then there is no barrier to God's power creating it from eternity, which would imply the world is eternal. They claim this is absurd because the world must have a beginning. The author responds by distinguishing between the possibility of existence and actual existence: something can have a beginningless possibility while being contingent on a condition (being preceded by non-existence). Thus, the possibility being beginningless does not entail actual beginninglessness. The condition of being temporal precludes eternity. Therefore, the objection fails.

الفعل
الشرط
مسبوق بالعدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if something's possibility is beginningless, then the thing itself must also be beginningless (eternal).
Correction: The author shows that a thing can have a beginningless possibility while being itself temporal, if it is conditioned by non-existence.

Checkpoint

B) He says the possibility is for a world conditioned by non-existence, so it cannot be eternal.
C) He says God's power is limited.
D) He says the world is actually eternal.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world being eternal is not the same as its possibility being beginningless; the condition of being preceded by non-existence makes eternity impossible.

?

How does this reality affect your sense of urgency and gratitude? When you pray, do you truly feel that each moment is a gift that could have been otherwise?
?

Why Prerequisites for Divine Action Must Be Eternal
First station of argument for eternity of divine prerequisites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that all prerequisites for God's effecting must be eternal to avoid an infinite regress of causes.

Core Idea
If prerequisites for divine action are not eternal, we face an impossible regress.

The author presents the first of two arguments for the eternity of the prerequisites of divine action. The passage begins with a claim from opponents that everything necessary for God to bring things into existence must be present from eternity. The first station then provides a proof: if the totality of what is necessary for existence and effectivity were not eternal, its coming-to-be would require a cause, leading to a vicious regress unless we admit the prerequisites are eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the overall structure: the claim, then the first station (maqam) with its proof.
The passage is cut off at the second station; focus on the first.
2
Identify the main claim and structure
The claim: all prerequisites for God's effecting are eternal. The proof has two stations; the first is the focus here. Note the conditional statement and the logical steps.
3
Trace the first station's argument
The argument assumes the prerequisites are not eternal. Then their coming-to-be requires a cause. That cause leads to an infinite regress unless the prerequisites are eternal. Follow each step: conditional, disjunction, elimination, regress, conclusion.
The key move is rejecting the option that the coming-to-be has no cause, as that would deny the need for a creator.
4
Understand the impossibility of regress
An infinite regress of causes is considered impossible in kalam. Therefore, the only way to avoid regress is to admit the prerequisites are eternal.
This argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في حكاية الحجة القوية التي لهم في هذا الباب قالوا : كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى موجداً للأشياء ، ومكوناً لها : حاصل في الأزل [ ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون موجداً للأشياء ومكوناً لها في الأزل ] أما المقام الأول : فالذي يدل على صحته : أنه لو لم يكن كل ما لا بد منه في الموجودية والمؤثرية حاصلا في الأزل ، لصدق أن ذلك المجموع ما كان حاصلا في الأزل ، ثم صار حاصلاً . فحصول ذلك المجموع بعد أنه ما كان حاصلاً ، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر أو لا يفتقر . والثاني قول باستغناء الحادث عن المؤثر ، وذلك باطل بالاتفاق ، فبقي أنه لا بد لحدوث ذلك المجموع من مؤثر، والكلام في كيفية تأثير المؤثر في حدوث ذلك المجموع، عائد بتمامه. فيلزم إما التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات ، وهو محال ، وإما الانتهاء إلى الاعتراف بأن كل ما لا بد منه في الموجودية والمؤثرية : حاصل في الأزل وأما المقام الثاني : وهو أنه لما ثبت أن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ، كان حاصلاً في الأزل . فإنه يجب صدور الأثر عنه ، فنقول : الدليل عليه : وهو أنه عند حصول كل تلك الأمور ، إما أن يكون الأثر واجب الصدور عنه مطلقاً
Chapter One: On narrating the strong argument that they have in this chapter. They said: Everything that is indispensable for His being, exalted, an inventor of things and a bringer-into-existence of them is present in eternity [and when the matter is thus, He must be an inventor of things and a bringer-into-existence of them in eternity]. As for the first station: That which indicates its validity is that if not everything indispensable for existence and effectivity were present in eternity, then it would be true that that totality was not present in eternity, then it became present. So the coming-to-be of that totality after it was not present either requires an efficient cause or does not require. The second is the statement of the independence of the originated from the efficient cause, and that is false by agreement. So it remains that the coming-to-be of that totality inevitably requires an efficient cause, and the discussion about the manner of the efficient cause's effect in the coming-to-be of that totality returns in its entirety. Thus either a regress in causes and effects follows, which is impossible, or one ends at the acknowledgment that everything indispensable for existence and effectivity is present in eternity. As for the second station: That is that when it is established that everything indispensable for effectivity was present in eternity, then the effect must proceed from Him. So we say: The proof of it: is that when all those matters are present, either the effect is necessarily proceeding from Him absolutely...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the opponents (likely philosophers or theologians) for the eternity of divine action. It is structured as two stations (maqamān). The first station demonstrates that all prerequisites for God's effecting (being an inventor and bringer-into-existence) must be present in eternity. The argument runs: if the totality of what is necessary for existence and effectivity were not eternal, then it would have come into existence later. That coming-to-be requires a cause. To avoid infinite regress (which is impossible), one must admit that these prerequisites are eternal. The second station (incomplete in this excerpt) argues that once the prerequisites are eternal, the effect must necessarily proceed from God. The proof is cut off, but it appears to examine whether the effect is necessarily concomitant with the presence of all conditions. The passage is a typical kalam argument against the Ash'arite occasionalist view that God's action in time is free and not necessitated by eternal prerequisites; it instead supports the view that if all conditions are met, the effect is necessary.

Key Terms
الأزل
الموجودية والمؤثرية
التسلسل
المؤثر
الحادث
الأثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies that God is compelled to create, making creation necessary.
Correction: The argument does not claim God is forced; rather it shows that if all conditions for an effect are eternally present, then the effect must be eternal or necessarily follow—otherwise we would need a new condition, leading to regress. This is a logical argument about the relationship between conditions and effects, not about divine freedom in the ordinary sense.

Checkpoint
What does the first station's argument conclude if the prerequisites for God's effecting were not eternal?
A) That the effect would be impossible
B) That either an infinite regress occurs or one admits the prerequisites are eternal
C) That God would need a new will to create
D) That the world must be eternal

Key Takeaway
The first station demonstrates that the assumption that prerequisites for divine action are not eternal leads to an unavoidable dilemma: either an infinite regress of causes or acknowledgment that the prerequisites are eternal, and since regress is impossible, the prerequisites must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument proves that an infinite regress is impossible. Is there a chain of excuses or delays in your life where you tell yourself 'I will change later' that amounts to an endless regress? What would it mean to break that chain today, right now, without waiting for another condition?
?
If you truly believed that God's mercy and will to guide you are eternal and unconditional—not waiting for you to become worthy—how would your relationship with Him shift? Will you stop bargaining and simply turn to Him as He is, already ready to receive you?
?
The passage shows that without eternal prerequisites, the very act of causing becomes incoherent. When you pray or act for God's sake, do you assume He is waiting for something in you to be complete before He responds? Or do you trust that His giving is rooted in His eternal perfection, not your temporary state? What is one practical step you can take today to live out that trust?
