The Eternity of God's Causal Conditions
Presents the first station proving the eternity of the necessary conditions for God's causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all conditions necessary for God to be an originator must be eternal, based on the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
If God's causal conditions could arise, they would need a cause, leading to an infinite regress — which is impossible.

The theologians arguing for the eternity of the world reason that for God to be an effective cause, certain conditions must be met. Could these conditions themselves have come into being? This lesson unpacks the first part of their argument: that such a scenario would entangle us in an infinite chain of causes, forcing the conclusion that the conditions are eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the first station passage
Read the Arabic passages for the first station and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by a dilemma.
2
Identify the conditional hypothesis
The argument begins by assuming the opposite: that the necessary conditions were not eternal. Understand why this leads to a problem.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the arising of the conditions requires a cause or not. See how the second option is rejected by consensus, leaving only the first.
4
Trace the regress argument
If the arising requires a cause, then that cause itself would be part of the conditions, leading to an infinite regress. Recognize why this is considered impossible and how it forces the conclusion that the conditions are eternal.
This is the core logical move in the first station.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْفَصْلُ الْأَوَّلُ
فِي حِكَايَةِ الْحُجَّةِ الْقَوِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَهُمْ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ
قَالُوا : كُلُّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُوجِداً لِلْأَشْيَاءِ ، وَمُكَوِّناً لَهَا : حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ [ وَمَتَى كَانَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، وَجَبَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُوجِداً لِلْأَشْيَاءِ وَمُكَوِّناً لَهَا فِي الْأَزَلِ ]
أَمَّا الْمَقَامُ الْأَوَّلُ : فَالَّذِي يَدُلُّ عَلَى صِحَّتِهِ : أَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ كُلُّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَوْجُودِيَّةِ وَالْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ، لَصَدَقَ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعَ مَا كَانَ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ، ثُمَّ صَارَ حَاصِلاً . فَحُصُولُ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ بَعْدَ أَنَّهُ مَا كَانَ حَاصِلاً، إِمَّا أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَى مُؤَثِّرٍ أَوْ لَا يَفْتَقِرَ . وَالثَّانِي قَوْلٌ بِاسْتِغْنَاءِ الْحَادِثِ عَنِ الْمُؤَثِّرِ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ بِالِاتِّفَاقِ، فَبَقِيَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ لِحُدُوثِ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ مِنْ مُؤَثِّرٍ، وَالْكَلَامُ فِي كَيْفِيَّةِ تَأْثِيرِ الْمُؤَثِّرِ فِي حُدُوثِ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ، عَائِدٌ بِتَمَامِهِ. فَيَلْزَمُ إِمَّا التَّسَلْسُلُ فِي الْأَسْبَابِ وَالْمُسَبَّبَاتِ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَإِمَّا الِانْتِهَاءُ إِلَى الِاعْتِرَافِ بِأَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَوْجُودِيَّةِ وَالْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ : حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ
وَأَمَّا الْمَقَامُ الثَّانِي : وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا ثَبَتَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ، كَانَ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ . فَإِنَّهُ يَجِبُ صُدُورُ الْأَثَرِ عَنْهُ، فَنَقُولُ : الدَّلِيلُ عَلَيْهِ : وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ عِنْدَ حُصُولِ كُلِّ تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَثَرُ وَاجِبَ الصُّدُورِ عَنْهُ مُطْلَقاً أَوْ لَا يَكُونَ كَذَلِكَ . وَالْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ، وَالثَّانِي بَاطِلٌ . لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الْأَثَرُ وَاجِبَ الصُّدُورِ، فَمَعَ تَقْدِيرِ حُصُولِ [ كُلِّ ] تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ كَانَ مُمْكِنَ الْحُصُولِ، وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ مُمْكِناً، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَرْضِ حُصُولِهِ مُحَالٌ، فَلْنَفْرِضْ مَعَ حُصُولِ كُلِّ تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ، حُصُولَ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرِ تَارَةً، وَلَا حُصُولَهُ أُخْرَى . فَاخْتِصَاصُ أَحَدِ الْوَقْتَيْنِ بِحُصُولِ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرِ دُونَ الْوَقْتِ الثَّانِي، مَعَ أَنَّ حُصُولَ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْوَقْتَيْنِ عَلَى السَّوِيَّةِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَوَقَّفَ عَلَى انْضِمَامِ قَيْدٍ زَائِدٍ إِلَيْهِ، أَوْ لَا يَتَوَقَّفَ . [ فَإِنْ تَوَقَّفَ ] فَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ هَذَا الْقَيْدُ الزَّائِدُ، أَحَدَ الْأُمُورِ الَّتِي لَا بُدَّ مِنْهَا فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُصْدِراً لِلْأَفْعَالِ، لَكِنَّا كُنَّا قَدْ فَرَضْنَا أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ الْأُمُورِ الْحَاصِلَةِ قَبْلَ هَذَا الْقَيْدِ، كَانَتْ كَافِيَةً فِي الْمَصْدَرِيَّةِ . هَذَا خَلْفٌ . وَأَيْضاً : فَعِنْدَ انْضِمَامِ هَذَا الْقَيْدِ إِلَيْهِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَثَرُ مُمْكِناً أَوْ وَاجِباً . وَيَعُودُ التَّقْسِيمُ الْأَوَّلُ فِيهِ . وَأَمَّا إِنْ قُلْنَا : إِنَّ امْتِيَازَ أَحَدِ هَذَيْنِ الْوَقْتَيْنِ بِحُصُولِ الْأَثَرِ فِيهِ دُونَ الْوَقْتِ الثَّانِي، لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى انْضِمَامِ قَيْدٍ زَائِدٍ إِلَيْهِ، فَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ رُجْحَانُ أَحَدِ طَرَفَيِ الْمُمْكِنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ، لَا الْمُرَجِّحِ . وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ . فَثَبَتَ بِمَا ذَكَرْنَا : أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُؤَثِّراً مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ . [ وَثَبَتَ : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، وَجَبَ حُصُولُ الْأَثَرِ فِي الْأَزَلِ ] وَذَلِكَ يُنْتِجُ الْمَطْلُوبَ
فَهَذَا هُوَ الْعُمْدَةُ الْكُبْرَى لِلْقَوْمِ فِي هَذِهِ الْمَسْأَلَةِ [ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ ]
The first chapter: On relating the strong argument that they have concerning this topic.
They said: Everything that is necessary for His being, exalted, an originator of things and a bringer into existence of them, is present in eternity. And when the matter is such, it is necessary that He be an originator of things and a bringer into existence of them in eternity.
As for the first station: what indicates its correctness is that if everything that is necessary for existence and effect-causation were not present in eternity, then it would be true that that totality was not present in eternity, then it became present. So the coming into existence of that totality after it was not present, either requires an effect-causer [mu'aththir] or does not require it. The second is a statement that the originated [al-hadith] is independent of the effect-causer, and that is false by consensus. So it remains that the origination of that totality must have an effect-causer. And the discussion concerning how the effect-causer brings about the origination of that totality returns in its entirety. So either an infinite regress [tasalsul] of causes and effects follows, which is impossible, or it ends with the admission that everything that is necessary for existence and effect-causation is present in eternity.
As for the second station: namely, since it is established that everything necessary for effect-causation was present in eternity, then the effect must proceed from Him. We say: the proof for it is that when all those things are present, either the effect is necessarily proceeding from Him absolutely, or it is not so. The first is what is sought, and the second is false. Because if the effect is not necessarily proceeding, then with the assumption of the presence of [all] those things, it is possible of occurrence. And everything that is possible, it is not necessary that its hypothetical occurrence entails a contradiction. So let us assume, with the presence of all those things, the occurrence of that effect at one time, and its non-occurrence at another time. So the particularization of one of the two times with the occurrence of that effect over the other time, while the presence of that totality is equal in relation to the two times, either depends on the addition of an extra condition [qayd za'id] to it, or it does not depend. If it depends, then this extra condition is one of the things that are necessary for His being, exalted, an originator of actions. But we had assumed that the totality of things present before this condition was sufficient for originatorship. This is a contradiction [khalf]. And also: when this condition is added to it, either the effect is possible or necessary. And the first division returns to it. And if we say that the distinction of one of these two times by the occurrence of the effect in it over the other time does not depend on the addition of an extra condition to it, then the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible that are equal over the other without a preponderator [murajjih] follows, and that is impossible. So it is established by what we mentioned: that everything that is necessary for His being, exalted, an effect-causer, exists in eternity. And it is established: that when the matter is such, the occurrence of the effect in eternity is necessary. And that yields what is sought.
So this is the greatest foundation [al-'umda al-kubra] for the [theological] school in this matter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents and proves a key argument (hujja qawiyya) used by certain theologians concerning God's causality and the eternity of the world. The argument aims to prove that everything necessary for God to be an effective cause (mu'aththir) must be present from eternity (azal), and consequently the effect itself must be eternal. The argument is divided into two stations (maqam).

In the first station, the claim is defended: if the totality of conditions needed for God to cause existence were not present in eternity, then that totality would come into existence at some point. This coming into existence would require another cause, leading either to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of causes—which is impossible—or to the admission that the conditions are eternal. Thus the first station establishes that the essential conditions for divine causality are eternal.

In the second station, from the eternity of those conditions, it is argued that the effect must also be eternal. The reasoning examines the status of the effect when all conditions are present. If the effect were not necessarily produced, its occurrence would be merely possible. One could then suppose it happens at one time and not another. Given that all conditions are equally present at both times, the difference can only be due to an additional condition (qayd za'id). But that additional condition would then be part of the required conditions, contradicting the assumption that the original set was sufficient. Alternatively, if no extra condition is added, the preference of one time over the other would be without a preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible according to the principle of sufficient reason. Therefore, the effect must necessarily and eternally proceed from God. The passage concludes that this is the main argument of the school in this matter.

الْمُؤَثِّر
التَّسَلْسُل
الْحَادِث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that the conditions for God's causality could arise spontaneously without a cause.
Correction: The passage argues that this would be a statement that the originated is independent of a cause, which is false by consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the first station, what would follow if the conditions necessary for God's causality were not eternal?
A) The conditions would be self-caused.
B) An infinite regress of causes would be impossible.
C) Either an infinite regress or the admission that they are eternal.
D) The effect would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The necessary conditions for God's causal action cannot have come into existence; they must be eternal to avoid an absurd infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's causal conditions are eternal, do you live as if every event depends solely on Him? Reflect on a time you attributed something to a secondary cause rather than to Allah directly. What would change if you consistently traced everything back to His eternal causality? Commit to one specific situation today where you will consciously refer your experience to Allah as the true effect-causer.
?
The argument shows that an infinite regress of causes is impossible, meaning your existence and every good thing you have ultimately traces back to a single, eternal, uncaused Cause. How does this realization affect your sense of gratitude and reliance on Allah versus reliance on created means? What created 'causes' do you tend to trust more than the Creator? Resolve to shift your trust from those means to the Maker of means, starting now.
?
You have just learned that the necessary conditions for God to act as Creator are eternal. If God had all the conditions to create from eternity, why did He create the world when He did? This question might unsettle you. But Scripture tells you that He does what He wills and is not asked about His actions. Does your heart rest in that, or do you demand an explanation? Surrender your need to know the 'why' and accept that His wisdom is perfect, even when not fully disclosed to you. Pray for contentment in His decree.

Allah's Existence
advanced
8 min
From Eternal Conditions to an Eternal Effect
Presents the second station proving the eternity of the effect from the eternity of the conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, given that God's causal conditions are eternal, the effect (the world) must also be eternal.

Core Idea
If all conditions are eternal and sufficient, any delay in the effect would require an arbitrary preference, which is impossible.

Having established that all prerequisites for God's causality are eternal, the argument now moves to the conclusion that the effect itself must be eternal. This lesson unpacks the second station's reasoning, which uses the principle of sufficient reason to rule out any temporal delay.

Guided Path
1
Read the second station passage
Read the Arabic for the second station and its translation. Notice the structure: a initial division into two possibilities.
2
Examine the first division
The argument divides the scenario when all conditions are present: either the effect is necessarily produced, or it is not. The first is the desired conclusion.
3
Analyze the 'not necessary' branch
If the effect is not necessary, it is possible. Then consider its occurrence at one time but not another. This leads to either an extra condition or a preponderance without a preponderator—both impossible.
This is the core of the proof: it shows that any temporal specification would need a reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْفَصْلُ الْأَوَّلُ
فِي حِكَايَةِ الْحُجَّةِ الْقَوِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَهُمْ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ
قَالُوا : كُلُّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُوجِداً لِلْأَشْيَاءِ ، وَمُكَوِّناً لَهَا : حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ [ وَمَتَى كَانَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، وَجَبَ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُوجِداً لِلْأَشْيَاءِ وَمُكَوِّناً لَهَا فِي الْأَزَلِ ]
أَمَّا الْمَقَامُ الْأَوَّلُ : فَالَّذِي يَدُلُّ عَلَى صِحَّتِهِ : أَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ كُلُّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَوْجُودِيَّةِ وَالْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ، لَصَدَقَ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعَ مَا كَانَ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ، ثُمَّ صَارَ حَاصِلاً . فَحُصُولُ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ بَعْدَ أَنَّهُ مَا كَانَ حَاصِلاً، إِمَّا أَنْ يَفْتَقِرَ إِلَى مُؤَثِّرٍ أَوْ لَا يَفْتَقِرَ . وَالثَّانِي قَوْلٌ بِاسْتِغْنَاءِ الْحَادِثِ عَنِ الْمُؤَثِّرِ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ بِالِاتِّفَاقِ، فَبَقِيَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ لِحُدُوثِ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ مِنْ مُؤَثِّرٍ، وَالْكَلَامُ فِي كَيْفِيَّةِ تَأْثِيرِ الْمُؤَثِّرِ فِي حُدُوثِ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ، عَائِدٌ بِتَمَامِهِ. فَيَلْزَمُ إِمَّا التَّسَلْسُلُ فِي الْأَسْبَابِ وَالْمُسَبَّبَاتِ، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، وَإِمَّا الِانْتِهَاءُ إِلَى الِاعْتِرَافِ بِأَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَوْجُودِيَّةِ وَالْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ : حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ
وَأَمَّا الْمَقَامُ الثَّانِي : وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا ثَبَتَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي الْمُؤَثِّرِيَّةِ، كَانَ حَاصِلاً فِي الْأَزَلِ . فَإِنَّهُ يَجِبُ صُدُورُ الْأَثَرِ عَنْهُ، فَنَقُولُ : الدَّلِيلُ عَلَيْهِ : وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ عِنْدَ حُصُولِ كُلِّ تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَثَرُ وَاجِبَ الصُّدُورِ عَنْهُ مُطْلَقاً أَوْ لَا يَكُونَ كَذَلِكَ . وَالْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ، وَالثَّانِي بَاطِلٌ . لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الْأَثَرُ وَاجِبَ الصُّدُورِ، فَمَعَ تَقْدِيرِ حُصُولِ [ كُلِّ ] تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ كَانَ مُمْكِنَ الْحُصُولِ، وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ مُمْكِناً، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَرْضِ حُصُولِهِ مُحَالٌ، فَلْنَفْرِضْ مَعَ حُصُولِ كُلِّ تِلْكَ الْأُمُورِ، حُصُولَ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرِ تَارَةً، وَلَا حُصُولَهُ أُخْرَى . فَاخْتِصَاصُ أَحَدِ الْوَقْتَيْنِ بِحُصُولِ ذَلِكَ الْأَثَرِ دُونَ الْوَقْتِ الثَّانِي، مَعَ أَنَّ حُصُولَ ذَلِكَ الْمَجْمُوعِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْوَقْتَيْنِ عَلَى السَّوِيَّةِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَوَقَّفَ عَلَى انْضِمَامِ قَيْدٍ زَائِدٍ إِلَيْهِ، أَوْ لَا يَتَوَقَّفَ . [ فَإِنْ تَوَقَّفَ ] فَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ هَذَا الْقَيْدُ الزَّائِدُ، أَحَدَ الْأُمُورِ الَّتِي لَا بُدَّ مِنْهَا فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُصْدِراً لِلْأَفْعَالِ، لَكِنَّا كُنَّا قَدْ فَرَضْنَا أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ الْأُمُورِ الْحَاصِلَةِ قَبْلَ هَذَا الْقَيْدِ، كَانَتْ كَافِيَةً فِي الْمَصْدَرِيَّةِ . هَذَا خَلْفٌ . وَأَيْضاً : فَعِنْدَ انْضِمَامِ هَذَا الْقَيْدِ إِلَيْهِ، إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَثَرُ مُمْكِناً أَوْ وَاجِباً . وَيَعُودُ التَّقْسِيمُ الْأَوَّلُ فِيهِ . وَأَمَّا إِنْ قُلْنَا : إِنَّ امْتِيَازَ أَحَدِ هَذَيْنِ الْوَقْتَيْنِ بِحُصُولِ الْأَثَرِ فِيهِ دُونَ الْوَقْتِ الثَّانِي، لَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى انْضِمَامِ قَيْدٍ زَائِدٍ إِلَيْهِ، فَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ رُجْحَانُ أَحَدِ طَرَفَيِ الْمُمْكِنِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ، لَا الْمُرَجِّحِ . وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ . فَثَبَتَ بِمَا ذَكَرْنَا : أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ فِي كَوْنِهِ تَعَالَى مُؤَثِّراً مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْأَزَلِ . [ وَثَبَتَ : أَنَّهُ مَتَى كَانَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَلِكَ، وَجَبَ حُصُولُ الْأَثَرِ فِي الْأَزَلِ ] وَذَلِكَ يُنْتِجُ الْمَطْلُوبَ
فَهَذَا هُوَ الْعُمْدَةُ الْكُبْرَى لِلْقَوْمِ فِي هَذِهِ الْمَسْأَلَةِ [ وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ ]
The first chapter: On relating the strong argument that they have concerning this topic.
They said: Everything that is necessary for His being, exalted, an originator of things and a bringer into existence of them, is present in eternity. And when the matter is such, it is necessary that He be an originator of things and a bringer into existence of them in eternity.
As for the first station: what indicates its correctness is that if everything that is necessary for existence and effect-causation were not present in eternity, then it would be true that that totality was not present in eternity, then it became present. So the coming into existence of that totality after it was not present, either requires an effect-causer [mu'aththir] or does not require it. The second is a statement that the originated [al-hadith] is independent of the effect-causer, and that is false by consensus. So it remains that the origination of that totality must have an effect-causer. And the discussion concerning how the effect-causer brings about the origination of that totality returns in its entirety. So either an infinite regress [tasalsul] of causes and effects follows, which is impossible, or it ends with the admission that everything that is necessary for existence and effect-causation is present in eternity.
As for the second station: namely, since it is established that everything necessary for effect-causation was present in eternity, then the effect must proceed from Him. We say: the proof for it is that when all those things are present, either the effect is necessarily proceeding from Him absolutely, or it is not so. The first is what is sought, and the second is false. Because if the effect is not necessarily proceeding, then with the assumption of the presence of [all] those things, it is possible of occurrence. And everything that is possible, it is not necessary that its hypothetical occurrence entails a contradiction. So let us assume, with the presence of all those things, the occurrence of that effect at one time, and its non-occurrence at another time. So the particularization of one of the two times with the occurrence of that effect over the other time, while the presence of that totality is equal in relation to the two times, either depends on the addition of an extra condition [qayd za'id] to it, or it does not depend. If it depends, then this extra condition is one of the things that are necessary for His being, exalted, an originator of actions. But we had assumed that the totality of things present before this condition was sufficient for originatorship. This is a contradiction [khalf]. And also: when this condition is added to it, either the effect is possible or necessary. And the first division returns to it. And if we say that the distinction of one of these two times by the occurrence of the effect in it over the other time does not depend on the addition of an extra condition to it, then the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible that are equal over the other without a preponderator [murajjih] follows, and that is impossible. So it is established by what we mentioned: that everything that is necessary for His being, exalted, an effect-causer, exists in eternity. And it is established: that when the matter is such, the occurrence of the effect in eternity is necessary. And that yields what is sought.
So this is the greatest foundation [al-'umda al-kubra] for the [theological] school in this matter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents and proves a key argument (hujja qawiyya) used by certain theologians concerning God's causality and the eternity of the world. The argument aims to prove that everything necessary for God to be an effective cause (mu'aththir) must be present from eternity (azal), and consequently the effect itself must be eternal. The argument is divided into two stations (maqam).

In the first station, the claim is defended: if the totality of conditions needed for God to cause existence were not present in eternity, then that totality would come into existence at some point. This coming into existence would require another cause, leading either to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of causes—which is impossible—or to the admission that the conditions are eternal. Thus the first station establishes that the essential conditions for divine causality are eternal.

In the second station, from the eternity of those conditions, it is argued that the effect must also be eternal. The reasoning examines the status of the effect when all conditions are present. If the effect were not necessarily produced, its occurrence would be merely possible. One could then suppose it happens at one time and not another. Given that all conditions are equally present at both times, the difference can only be due to an additional condition (qayd za'id). But that additional condition would then be part of the required conditions, contradicting the assumption that the original set was sufficient. Alternatively, if no extra condition is added, the preference of one time over the other would be without a preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible according to the principle of sufficient reason. Therefore, the effect must necessarily and eternally proceed from God. The passage concludes that this is the main argument of the school in this matter.

Key Terms
الْقَيْدُ الزَّائِد
الْمُرَجِّح
الْمُمْكِن
الْوَاجِب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could delay the creation of the world arbitrarily without a reason (i.e., by sheer will).
Correction: The argument shows that a delay without an additional condition would be a preference without a preponderator, which is rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the second station, if the effect is not necessarily produced from the eternal conditions, what problem arises?
A) The effect would be impossible.
B) An extra condition would lead to a contradiction or a preponderance without a preponderator.
C) The effect would be self-caused.
D) The conditions would not be eternal.

Key Takeaway
If all conditions for God's causality are eternal and sufficient, the effect must also be eternal; any temporal delay would violate the principle of sufficient reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's effect (the creation) is necessarily eternal from His side, then your existence was never a matter of chance or delayed divine whim. How does this truth confront your feelings of insignificance or insecurity? Will you live today as someone who was eternally purposed by God?
?
The argument shows that delay without cause is impossible. Think of a time when you felt God was slow to answer you. Does this lesson demand that you reconsider whether you truly believed He had sufficient reasons? What would it mean to trust that His timing is never arbitrary?
?
If every condition for God's action was eternally present, then your salvation or guidance was not an afterthought but an eternal decree. How does this reality challenge your view of your own role in seeking Him? Does it make you more grateful or more complacent? What concrete step will you take today to align with His eternal purpose?

The Philosophical Proof of the Eternal Effect
Proof by reductio ad absurdum for the eternity of the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that if all conditions for God's agency are present from eternity, the effect must also be eternal, and why this is considered a major argument by the philosophers.

Core Idea
An eternal cause with all conditions eternal cannot have a delayed effect; otherwise, absurdity follows.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic philosophical theology. It uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that if the effect of God is not necessary from eternity, then we must choose between two equally impossible options. Understanding this proof is crucial for grasping the philosophers' position on the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow and terminology.
Pay attention to the terms 'اثر', 'مؤثر', and 'قيد زائد'.
2
Identify the two branches of the dichotomy
The passage presents a conditional: either the specification of time depends on an extra condition or it does not. Locate these two branches in the text.
The text uses 'إما أن يتوقف... أو لا يتوقف'.
3
Trace the reasoning in each branch
For the first branch (with extra condition), see how it leads to a contradiction. For the second branch (without extra condition), see how it leads to preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
Note the recursion in the first branch: the extra condition itself leads back to the same division.
4
Understand the conclusion
After showing both branches are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that the effect is necessary and eternal from the beginning. Read the final sentences.
The author states that this is the 'major foundation' for the philosophers on this issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لا يكون كذلك . والأول هو المطلوب ، والثاني باطل . لأنه إن لم يكن الأثر واجب الصدور ، فمع تقدير حصول كل تلك الأمور كان ممكن الحصول ، وكل ما كان ممكناً ، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض حصوله محال ، فلنفرض مع حصول كل تلك الأمور ، حصول ذلك الأثر تارة ، ولا حصوله أخرى . فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بحصول ذلك الأثر دون الوقت الثاني ، مع أن حصول ذلك المجموع بالنسبة إلى الوقتين على السوية ، إما أن يتوقف على انضمام قيد زائد إليه ، أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف فحينئذ يكون هذا القيد الزائد ، أحد الأمور التي لا بد منها في كونه تعالى مصدراً للأفعال ، لكنـا كنا قد فرضنا أن جملة الأمور الحاصلة قبل هذا القيد ، كانت كافية في المصدرية . هذا خلف . وأيضاً : فعند انضمام هذا القيد إليه ، إما أن يكون الأثر ممكناً أو واجباً . ويعود التقسيم الأول فيه . وأما إن قلنا : إن امتياز أحد هذين الوقتين بحصول الأثر فيه دون الوقت الثاني ، لا يتوقف على انضمام قيد زائد إليه ، فحينئذ يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن المتساويين على الآخر ، لا المرجح . وهو محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً موجود في الأزل . وثبت : أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب حصول الأثر في الأزل . وذلك ينتج المطلوب . فهذا هو العمدة الكبرى للقوم في هذه المسألة . والله أعلم .
Or it is not so. The first is the desired, and the second is false. Because if the effect is not necessarily existent, then given the supposition of obtaining all those things, it would be possible of obtaining, and everything possible, it does not entail impossibility from supposing its obtaining. So let us suppose, with the obtaining of all those things, the obtaining of that effect sometimes, and its not obtaining at other times. Then the specification of one of the two times with the obtaining of that effect over the second time, while the obtaining of that totality relative to the two times is equal, either depends on the addition of an extra condition to it, or does not depend. If it depends, then that extra condition becomes one of the things that are necessary for Him – exalted is He – to be a source of acts, but we had supposed that the totality of the things obtained before this condition were sufficient for being a source. This is a contradiction. Also: upon the addition of this condition to it, either the effect is possible or necessary. And the first division returns to it. And if we say: the distinction of one of these two times with the obtaining of the effect in it over the second time does not depend on the addition of an extra condition to it, then it follows that one of the two sides of the equal possible is preferred without a preponderator. That is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that everything necessary for Him – exalted is He – to be an agent exists in eternity. And it is established: whenever the matter is like that, the effect must obtain in eternity. And that yields the desired. So this is the major foundation for the people (the philosophers) in this question. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof (burhan) for the eternity of the world, attributed to the philosophers. It begins by considering that if the effect of God is not necessary, then given all the conditions for His agency are present, the effect could be or not be. Then it examines the specification of the time of the effect: if the effect occurs at a specific time rather than another, either there is an additional condition that determines it, or not. If there is an additional condition, that condition would be part of the necessary conditions, contradicting the assumption that the previous set was sufficient. This leads to an infinite regress. If there is no additional condition, then the preference of one time without a preponderator is impossible (this is the principle of 'preponderance without a preponderator' which is absurd). Therefore, all conditions necessary for God's agency are present from eternity, so the effect must also be eternal. The author concludes that this is the major argument of the philosophers on this issue. The proof is structured as a reductio ad absurdum of the possibility of a temporal effect given eternal conditions. Note the use of 'equal possible' (الممكن المتساوي) and the notion that an effect must occur immediately when all causes are present, a key principle in falsafa and kalam debates.

Key Terms
الأثر (athar)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
الأزل (al-azal)
قيد زائد (qayd za'id)
رجحان بلا مرجح (tarjih bila murajjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect at a specific time without any additional factor.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that if all conditions (including the cause) are eternal, the effect must be eternal; otherwise, an absurd choice between an infinite regress or preponderance without a preponderator arises.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what follows if the specification of the time of the effect does not depend on an extra condition?
A) The effect is impossible.
B) There must be a preponderator.
C) It entails preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
D) The effect must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that if God's causality is fully active from eternity, the world must be eternal, as any temporal delay would lead to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument claims that if all conditions for God's action are present, the effect must follow immediately. In your own life, when you have all the means to obey God, do you sometimes delay? What excuses do you use? Will you commit to acting immediately when conditions are right?
?
The philosophers here say a delay without a new cause is impossible. Reflect: Is there an area of your faith where you are waiting for a 'sign' or 'feeling' before acting, even though all conditions for obedience are already present? How does this challenge your resolve?
?
If this logical proof were true, it would mean the world has always existed alongside God. How does that sit with your heart? Do you trust God's revelation over philosophical reasoning? Where do you need to surrender your intellect to faith?

Ninth Response: Purpose of Creation and Divine Beneficence
Presents the ninth answer: God created the world to manifest His power and for deduction of His divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth response to the argument against temporal creation: that God created the world to manifest His power and to provide evidence for His divinity, and that the timing of creation is a matter of divine beneficence and free choice.

Core Idea
God created the world not out of necessity, but to reveal His power and invite us to know Him.

The ninth and final response in this series tackles the question of why God created the world when He did. The answer is both simple and profound: creation itself is a sign of God's power and divinity. By examining this argument, we see how the Qur'an itself frames creation as a lesson for humanity, and why the specific timing is not a problem for divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the Quranic citation and the logical flow.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, evidence, rebuttal, conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage says God created the world for two reasons: to manifest His power and to provide evidence for His divinity.
These two purposes are intertwined in the argument.
3
Understand the Quranic support
Look at verse 65:12 and see how it explicitly states the purpose of creation: that you may know God's power and knowledge.
The verse is used as direct evidence for the claim.
4
Consider the issue of time
The passage argues that the precise time of creation does not need an extra cause because the Creator's motive is beneficence and He is free to choose.
This addresses a potential objection to the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes by noting that these nine responses together answer the original argument.
This response is the last in a series.

الوجه التاسع في الجواب: أن يقال: إنه تعالى خلق العالم إظهاراً لقدرته، وليستدل به على إلهيته. قالوا: وهذا هو الجواب المذكور في القرآن وهو قول الله تعالى: {الله الذي خلق سبع سموات، ومن الأرض مثلهن، يتنزل الأمر بينهن، لتعلموا أن الله على كل شيء قدير، وأن الله قد أحاط بكل شيء علما} فبين: أنه تعالى إنما خلق هذه الأشياء ليستدل بها على كمال إلهيته، وكمال قدرته. وأما تعيين الوقت، فغير لازم. لأنه لما كان الداعي له إلى خلق هذه الأشياء: هو الإحسان والتفضل. فالمحسن والمتفضل مختار في تعيين الوقت. فلم يكن هذا التعيين محتاجاً إلى سبب زائد. فهذه الوجوه التسعة هي الأجوبة المذكورة عن الحجة المذكورة.
The ninth face of the answer: that it is said: He, exalted, created the world to manifest His power, and that it might be used to infer His divinity. They said: This is the answer mentioned in the Qur'an, which is the word of God, exalted: "God is He who created seven heavens, and of the earth the like thereof; the command descends between them, that you may know that God is over all things powerful, and that God encompasses all things in knowledge." So He made clear that He, exalted, only created these things to infer by them the perfection of His divinity and the perfection of His power. As for the specification of the time, it is not necessary. Because since the motive for creating these things was beneficence and grace, the Beneficent and Gracious One is free in specifying the time. So this specification does not need an additional cause. So these nine faces are the answers mentioned concerning the mentioned argument.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is part of a series of responses to an argument against the temporal creation of the world based on the problem of a specific time. The ninth response argues that God created the world to manifest His power so that humans can infer His divinity. The author cites the Quranic verse (Surah At-Talaq 65:12) as evidence. He then argues that specifying the exact time of creation is not necessary because God's motive is beneficence, and the Beneficent One has free choice in timing. The passage concludes by noting that these nine responses answer the original argument.

First Argument: Polarity Requires a Preponderator
Razi's first argument from simplicity to preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument from the simplicity of the celestial sphere and why it necessitates a preponderator (God).

Core Idea
The sphere's simplicity forces an external cause to explain specific poles.

Razi begins his series of arguments for the necessity of a preponderator with a clever observation about the celestial sphere. Assuming the philosophers' view that the sphere is simple and homogeneous, he points out that the existence of specific poles among infinite identical points would be arbitrary without a cause. This sets the stage for the core kalam principle: every preponderance requires a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic text and then the English translation. Try to follow the logic in both languages.
Notice the concise structure: premise, then conclusion.
2
Understand the key assumption
The argument relies on the philosophers' claim that the celestial sphere is a simple body. Consider what 'simple' means: uniform, homogeneous, all parts identical.
3
Identify the consequence
If all points are identical, why are two specific points chosen as poles? This selection needs a cause.
This is the crux of the argument.
4
Connect to the main conclusion
Realize that this arbitrary selection would be impossible, so an external preponderator must exist. That is God.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you understood the logical connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن جرم الفلك [ جرم (١) ] بسيط عند القوم ، فتكون جميع النقط فيه متشابهة، فاختصاص نقطتين معينتين من تلك النقط الغير المتناهية بالقطبية دون سائر النقط ، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح .
First: That the body of the celestial sphere is simple according to the people (philosophers), so all points in it are similar. Then the specification of two particular points from among those infinite points with polarity to the exclusion of other points necessitates a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the first of seven arguments for the necessity of a preponderator (al-murajjiḥ) based on the nature of the celestial sphere (al-falak). It is an argument from the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ). The argument assumes, according to the philosophers (al-qawm), that the celestial sphere is a simple body (jirm basīṭ), meaning it is homogeneous in its substance. Consequently, all points within it are similar in essence. Therefore, if two specific points are distinguished as poles (al-quṭbiyya) among infinitely many similar points, this distinction is a preponderance of one possibility over another (i.e., that these points be poles rather than others) without any sufficient reason (lā li-murajjiḥ). Since such arbitrary preponderance is rationally impossible, this points to the necessity of an external preponderator, namely God.

Key Terms
جرم الفلك
بسيط
قطبية
رجحان
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the sphere actually has poles.
Correction: The argument does not assume the sphere actually has poles; it uses the philosophical assumption of simplicity to expose a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does the specification of two poles require a preponderator?
A) Because the sphere is composed of elements
B) Because the sphere is simple and all points are similar
C) Because the poles are the only points
D) Because the sphere rotates

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that the simplicity of the celestial sphere logically requires an external cause to account for the specific positions of its poles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the celestial sphere's simplicity demands an external cause to explain its poles, what does your own existence, with all its specific details (your time, place, talents, circumstances), demand? Are you acknowledging that Preponderator who specified you?
?
This argument shows that arbitrary specification without a cause is impossible. Is there any area of your life where you live as if your own choices or circumstances are self-caused, without recognizing the Preponderator behind them?
?
The philosophers assumed the sphere's simplicity, yet their own assumption led to the necessity of a Creator. Have you ever assumed something about the world that, if consistently followed, would force you to acknowledge God, yet you resist that conclusion? What is that assumption in your own thinking?

The Equality of Hypothetical Orbits
Razi presents the premise of the second argument: equality of orbits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the premise that all hypothetical orbits of the celestial sphere are equal because of the sphere's simplicity.

Core Idea
A simple body has equal capacity in all directions—no inherent preference.

The second argument begins by establishing a crucial point: if the celestial sphere is a simple body, then it is equally capable of moving in any direction. This means that all the infinite hypothetical orbits are equivalent in principle. Razi uses this equality to set up the need for an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once, paying attention to the claim that all orbits are equal and the reason given.
Notice the phrase 'جسم بسيط' (simple body) as the key reason.
2
Understand the reasoning
Ask yourself: why does simplicity of the sphere imply equality of orbits? Because a simple body has no internal parts or differentiation, so it cannot favor one direction over another.
3
Reflect on the infinite orbits
Consider the scope: there are infinitely many possible orbits (east-west, north-south, and all others), yet all are equally possible. This sets the stage for the next step.
The orbits are hypothetical (muftaraḍa), not actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن جميع المدارات المفترضة في الفلك متساوية ، لأن الفلك جسم بسيط ، فكما يكون قابلا للحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب ، فكذلك يكون قابلا للحركة من الشمال إلى الجنوب ، وكذلك القول في سائر المدارات التي لا نهاية لها، فوقوع الحركة على مدار واحد بعينه مع كونه مساوياً لسائر المدارات التي لا نهاية لها ، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح
Second: That all hypothetical orbits in the celestial sphere are equal, because the celestial sphere is a simple body. For just as it is capable of motion from east to west, so also it is capable of motion from north to south, and the same holds for the rest of the infinite orbits. So the occurrence of motion on one particular orbit, despite its being equal to the rest of the infinite orbits, necessitates a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument. It begins by stating that all hypothetical orbits (al-madārāt al-muftaraḍa) within the celestial sphere are equal. The reason is again that the sphere is a simple body (basīṭ), so it is equally capable of motion in any direction—east-west, north-south, or any other infinite possible orbit. Therefore, if actual motion occurs on one specific orbit (e.g., the equator) rather than any other equal orbit, this constitutes a preponderance of one possibility over another with no inherent cause. Thus, an external preponderator is required.

Key Terms
مدارات
مفترضة
متساوية
جسم بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why are all hypothetical orbits equal?
A) Because the sphere is simple
B) Because the sphere is spherical
C) Because the orbits are infinite
D) Because motion is eternal

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's simplicity implies that all hypothetical orbits are equally possible, establishing symmetry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that a simple body has no inherent preference for one orbit over another. In your own life, where do you assume that one choice is 'natural' without examining the evidence? Are you willing to see that your preferences might be equally groundless, and that you need an external standard to choose rightly?
?
This lesson shows that the celestial sphere's simplicity leads to equal possibility. Reflect on the state of your heart: is it a simple, unformed potential, or has it developed inclinations? What does that imply about the need for divine guidance to direct your desires?
?
If all orbits are equally possible, then the actual motion of the spheres requires a determinant beyond themselves. Consider your own actions: are you aware of the hidden determinants that shape your daily choices? Are you living as if you are self-determined, or do you humble yourself before the One who chooses for you?

The Need for a Preponderator
Razi concludes the second argument: actual motion on one equal orbit requires a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the actuality of motion on one specific orbit demands an external preponderator.

Core Idea
Equal possibilities require an external cause to break symmetry.

Having established that all orbits are equal, Razi now draws the conclusion. If motion actually occurs on one orbit rather than another equally possible orbit, then there must be something outside the sphere that gives it a preference. This is the second argument for a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that the occurrence of motion on one particular orbit is highlighted.
The phrase 'مدار واحد بعينه' means 'one particular orbit'.
2
Identify the key inference
Razi says this situation 'necessitates a preponderance' (yūjibu tarajjuḥ). Understand that the preponderance is the fact that one possibility is actualized over others.
3
Understand the demand for a preponderator
Since the sphere itself has no internal cause for this preponderance (it is simple), the preponderance must come from an external cause—a preponderator (murajjiḥ).
This argument is a classic application of the principle of sufficient reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the occurrence of motion on one particular orbit, despite its being equal to the rest of the infinite orbits, necessitates a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary

The reason is again that the sphere is a simple body (basīṭ), so it is equally capable of motion in any direction—east-west, north-south, or any other infinite possible orbit. Therefore, if actual motion occurs on one specific orbit (e.g., the equator) rather than any other equal orbit, this constitutes a preponderance of one possibility over another with no inherent cause. Third Argument: The Fixed Star's Position Requires a Preponderator
Razi presents the third argument from the fixed position of a star.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's third argument uses the fixed location of a star in a uniform sphere to demonstrate the need for an external preponderator.

Core Idea
A star's arbitrary position in the sky points to a cause beyond itself.

Razi continues his series of arguments for a necessary cause by considering the celestial sphere. If the sphere is simple (uniform), why should a star be lodged in one specific spot rather than any other? This arbitrariness points to an external preponderator. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation carefully. Pay attention to the key terms like 'مركوز' (fixed) and 'النقرة' (cavity).
Notice how Razi builds his argument in a single sentence.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two main premises: (1) the star is fixed in a specific location, (2) all parts of the celestial sphere are similar. Then note the conclusion about the cavity's existence.
The similarity of parts is due to the sphere's simplicity, as previously established.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Understand why the specific location of the cavity is a case of 'preponderance without a preponderator' – an arbitrary specification that demands an external cause.
This conclusion is the core of the argument for a preponderator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنَّ الكوكب مركوز في موضع معين من أجزاء الفلك ، وجميع الأجزاء المفترضة في الفلك متشابهة . لما بينا : أنه بسيط . فحصول النقرة التي المكان لذلك الكوكب في ذلك الجانب المعين من ذلك الفلك ، دون سائر الجوانب ، يكون ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح
Third: That a star is fixed in a specific position among the parts of the celestial sphere, and all hypothetical parts in the celestial sphere are similar, because we have shown that it is simple. So the existence of the cavity (al-nuqra) which is the place for that star in that particular side of that celestial sphere, to the exclusion of other sides, would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the third argument. It notes that a star (al-kawkab) is embedded (markūz) in a specific location within the celestial sphere. Since all parts of the sphere are similar (mutashābiha) due to its simplicity, there is no intrinsic reason for the cavity (al-nuqra) that houses the star to be in that particular side rather than any other. Thus, this specification also requires an external preponderator.

Fourth Argument: Eccentric Sphere Thickness Requires a Preponderator
Razi presents the fourth argument from eccentric sphere remnants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses astronomical eccentric spheres to argue that varying thickness in a simple body requires an external preponderator, pointing to a necessary cause.

Core Idea
A simple nature cannot cause two opposite thicknesses without an external preponderator.

This lesson presents Razi's fourth argument for a necessary cause, drawn from the structure of eccentric celestial spheres. He shows that a simple body with a single nature cannot produce uneven thickness on its own, implying the need for an external preponderator. This argument reinforces the kalām principle that contingent phenomena demand a cause outside themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the technical terms used.
The passage uses astronomical terminology; don't worry if it's unfamiliar.
2
Identify the key elements
Locate the representative sphere (فلك ممثل), the eccentric sphere (فلك خارج المركز), the two complements (متممان), and the simple body (جسم بسيط). Understand their roles.
The complement is the residual part after separation.
3
Trace the logical reasoning
Follow Razi's argument: the complement is simple and has one nature, yet that nature produces great thickness on one side and great thinness on the other. Ask why this is a problem.
A single nature should produce uniform effects if acting alone.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Conclude that the varying thickness requires an external preponderator (مرجح) — something that tips the balance. This points to a necessary cause beyond the sphere itself.
This argument supports the existence of a First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن الفلك الممثل إذا انفصل عن ثخنه فلك خارج المركز ، فإنه يبقى منه متممان : أحدهما : من داخل . والآخر : من خارج ، والمتمم يكون مختلف الثخن لا محالة ، وذلك المتمم في نفسه جسم بسيط ، والبسيط [ له (٢) ] طبيعة واحدة ، ثم إن تلك الطبيعة إقتضت في أحد جانب ذلك المتمم ثخناً عظيماً ، وفي الجانب الآخر منه رقة عظيمة وذلك يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن [ على الآخر (٣) ] لا لمرجح
Fourth: That the representative celestial sphere (al-falak al-mumaththal) when it separates from its thickness to become an eccentric sphere (falākhārij al-markaz), then there remain from it two complements: one from the inside and the other from the outside, and the complement is necessarily of varying thickness, and that complement in itself is a simple body, and the simple [has] a single nature, then that nature necessitated in one side of that complement a great thickness and in the other side a great thinness, and that necessitates a preponderance of one side of the possible [over the other] without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the fourth argument, involving an astronomical model: an eccentric sphere (falākhārij al-markaz) is derived from a representative sphere (al-falak al-mumaththal) by removing a thickness, leaving two complements (mutammimān) of varying thickness. The complement itself is a simple body (basīṭ) with a single nature. Yet that single nature produces great thickness on one side and great thinness on the other. This contradicting outcome from a uniform nature indicates an arbitrary preponderance, requiring an external cause.

Key Terms
فلك ممثل
فلك خارج المركز
ثخن
متمم
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple nature always produces uniform results.
Correction: Razi shows that a simple nature can produce varying thickness, indicating an external cause is needed to explain the asymmetry.

Checkpoint
In the fourth argument, what does the simple nature of the complement produce?
A) Uniform thickness
B) Great thickness on one side and great thinness on the other
C) Two equal parts
D) No thickness at all

Key Takeaway
The varying thickness in a simple celestial body implies an external preponderator, supporting the kalam argument for a necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The argument shows that a single nature cannot produce opposite effects. How do you account for the good and evil in your own actions? Are you relying on your own nature or seeking God's preponderating grace to incline you towards righteousness?

The Fifth Argument: Convex and Concave from a Single Nature
Razi enumerates the fifth argument from convex and concave surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the unity of the celestial sphere's nature and the diversity of its surfaces to argue for an external preponderator.

Core Idea
One nature, two surfaces, three differences — diversity demands a preponderator.

In this passage, Razi presents a striking argument from the natural world. The celestial sphere has a uniform nature, yet it produces two distinct surfaces: convex and concave. These surfaces differ in shape, location, and size. How can a single nature yield such variety? Razi argues that this diversity points to an external cause that tips the balance — a preponderator. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the enumeration of three differences.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the premise: the celestial sphere has similar parts in nature. Then find the conclusion: the nature necessitated two different surfaces. List the three differences given.
3
Reflect on the argument's force
Ask yourself: why does this diversity from a single nature imply the need for a preponderator? Consider Razi's overall aim in this section.
This connects to the broader proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الفلك جسم متشابه الأجزاء في الطبيعة ، ثم إن تلك الطبيعة اقتضت حصول سطح محدب في الخارج، وحصول سطح مقعر في الداخل ، والسطح المحدب ، يخالف السطح المقعر في أمور : أحدها : في كون أحدهما محدباً ، وكون الثاني مقعراً . وثانيها : في كون المحدب خارجاً ، والمقعر داخلاً وثالثها : في كون المحدب أعظم ، كون المقعر أصغر . فههنا الطبيعة واحدة ، مع أنه حصل هذا النوع من الاختلاف
Fifth: That the celestial sphere is a body whose parts are similar in nature, then that nature necessitated the occurrence of a convex surface on the outside, and the occurrence of a concave surface on the inside. And the convex surface differs from the concave surface in matters: one of them: that one is convex and the second is concave. Second: that the convex is outside and the concave is inside. Third: that the convex is greater and the concave is smaller. So here the nature is one, while this type of difference has occurred.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the fifth argument. It notes that the celestial sphere has a uniform nature (ṭabīʿa wāḥida) because its parts are similar (mutashābih al-ajzāʾ). Yet from this single nature arise two distinct surfaces: a convex outer surface and a concave inner surface. These differ in three ways: shape (convex vs. concave), location (outside vs. inside), and size (larger vs. smaller). This variety from a single nature indicates arbitrariness and thus requires an external preponderator.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that a single uniform nature producing diverse surfaces indicates the need for an external preponderator, pointing to the existence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If a single nature cannot produce such diversity without a preponderator, what does that mean for your own life? Are you acknowledging that Preponderator daily, or do you take the uniformity and diversity for granted? Commit today to a specific act of gratitude that recognizes Him as the source of both.
?
Razi’s argument forces you to confront the logical necessity of a preponderator. Is your certainty in God’s existence built on such rational foundations, or is it merely inherited or emotional? If you were to face a skeptic, would this argument hold firm in your heart? Write down one way you can strengthen your intellectual conviction using this proof.
?
The celestial sphere’s surfaces—convex and concave—point to a single cause beyond themselves. Look at the 'surfaces' of your own life: your successes and failures, joys and sorrows. Do you see a single hand behind them all, or do you attribute them to random chance? Today, consciously attribute one specific outcome in your life to Allah’s deliberate preponderance, and reflect on His wisdom in making it differ from what could have been.

Sixth Argument: Depth and Surface Parts Require a Preponderator
Razi presents the sixth argument with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth argument for the need of a preponderator based on the depth and surface parts of the celestial sphere, and how he defends it against the objection of continuity.

Core Idea
Differences in position among similar parts prove an external cause.

Razi continues his series of arguments for a preponderator by focusing on the celestial sphere. He argues that since the sphere is a thick body, we can identify parts in its depth and on its surfaces that are identical in essence yet occupy different positions. This difference requires a cause. The passage then addresses a key objection and offers an atomistic defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: argument, objection, answer.
2
Identify the premises
Find the premise that the celestial sphere is thick and that parts can be hypothesized in depth and surface. Note the claim that all parts are identical in essence.
3
Understand the objection
The objector denies actual parts in the sphere, claiming continuity. See how this threatens the argument.
4
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi invokes the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) doctrine to prove that divisible bodies have actual parts. Confirm how this rebuttal saves the argument.
This connects to earlier kalām discussions on atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : الفلك جسم ثخين، فيفترض في عمقه أجزاء ، وفي كل واحد [ من (١) ] سطحيه أجزاء أخرى. وجملة تلك الأجزاء متشابهة في تمام الماهية ، بناء على أن الفلك جسم بسيط، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان اختصاص بعض تلك الأجزاء بالوقوع في العمق ، والبعض في الوقوع في السطح ورجحاناً للممكن ، لا المرجح لا يقال : الفلك جسم واحد متصل وليس فيه أجزاء بالفعل ، فسقط ما ذكرتموه . لأنا نقول : قد بينا بالدلائل القاطعة في مسألة الجوهر الفرد ، أن كل ما يقبل القسمة ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مركباً من أجزاء ، كل واحد منها يكون ممتازاً عن صاحبه امتيازاً بالفعل، وإذا كان كذلك فقد سقط هذا العذر .
Sixth: The celestial sphere is a thick body, so parts are hypothesized in its depth, and in each of its two surfaces other parts. And all those parts are similar in complete essence, based on the fact that the celestial sphere is a simple body. And if the matter is so, then the specification of some of those parts to occur in the depth, and some to occur on the surface, is a preponderance of the possible without a preponderator. It cannot be said: The celestial sphere is one continuous body and has no parts in actuality, so what you mentioned falls. For we say: We have proven by conclusive proofs in the issue of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) that everything divisible must necessarily be composed of parts, each of which is distinct from its companion by actual distinction. And if that is so, then this excuse falls away.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the sixth argument, followed by an objection and its answer. The argument posits that the celestial sphere is thick (thakhīn), so we can hypothesize parts (ajzāʾ) in its depth and on its surfaces. All these parts share the same complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya) because the sphere is simple. Yet some parts are in the depth and others on the surface—an arbitrary specification requiring an external cause. The objector claims that the sphere is one continuous body without actual parts, so the argument fails. The answer references a previous proof (in the issue of the indivisible atom, masʾalat al-jawhar al-fard) establishing that anything divisible is composed of actually distinct parts. Therefore, the sphere must have parts, and the argument stands.

Key Terms
ثخين
أجزاء
العمق
السطح
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The celestial sphere has no actual parts because it is one continuous body.
Correction: According to the doctrine of the indivisible atom, everything divisible is composed of actually distinct parts. The sphere is divisible, so it must have actual parts.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that the sphere has no actual parts?
A) He concedes the point
B) He argues that parts are only conceptual
C) He cites proof from the indivisible atom doctrine
D) He ignores the objection

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth argument shows that the structural differentiation within the celestial sphere necessitates an external preponderator, a conclusion that holds even when the objection of continuity is raised, thanks to atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that essentially identical parts cannot be arbitrarily placed without a cause. In your own life, do you truly believe that your unique characteristics, circumstances, and positions are not random but assigned by a Preponderator? What would change if you lived with that conviction every day?
?
The objector dismisses the evidence by claiming the sphere has no actual parts, but Razi refutes it with proof. Have you ever rationalized away the clear signs of Allah in creation—like the order of the universe—by saying 'it's just how things are'? How can you actively seek the deeper proofs that affirm the necessity of Allah's existence, just as Razi did, and let that transform your certainty?

The Seventh Argument: Infinite Spaces and Rational Intuition
Razi presents the seventh argument from infinite external spaces with an objection and partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument for a preponderator based on infinite external spaces and the rebuttal to the objection that this judgment is merely estimative.

Core Idea
Pure reason's decisive judgment is self-validating; to doubt it is sophistry.

Razi continues his series of proofs by appealing to an intuitive, necessary judgment of the intellect: that there are infinite, distinct spaces beyond the world. This argument is challenged by those who relegate such judgments to the lower estimative faculty. Razi's response is sharp—dismissing such skepticism as sophistry, because a clear, indubitable intuition must be accepted. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the claim about the intuitive intellect's judgment on infinite spaces and the distinction between directions.
The text is slightly truncated at the end; the response is cut off.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: that this judgment comes from the estimative faculty, not the rational faculty. Understand the challenge.
3
Analyze Razi's rebuttal
Razi argues that denying a decisive, doubt-free intuitive judgment leads to sophistry (safsafa). Consider why he considers this a powerful counter.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core rebuttal is clear.
4
Reflect on the epistemic principle
The lesson establishes a principle: if pure instinct gives a certain judgment, we must accept it. How does this ground the argument?
This connects to broader themes in kalam about self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه حصل خارج العالم أحياز لا نهاية لها ، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الجانب الذي يحاذي القطب الشمالي ، مغاير للجانب الذي يحاذي القطب الجنوبي ، وهذا الامتياز في تلك الجوانب أمر يشهد به صريح العقل . لا يقال : الحكم بهذا المعنى هو فطرة القوة الوهمية لا فطرة القوة العقلية . لأنا نقول : إن هذا الكلام يوجب القول بالسفسطة . لأنا إذا وجدنا من صريح الفطرة حكماً جازماً ، لا شبهة فيه البتة ، وجب علينا أن نحكم بصحته. إذ لو قلنا : إنه حصل عندنا قوة وهمية
Seventh: That the intuitive intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) judges that there occur outside the world infinite spaces (ahyāz), for we know by necessity that the side that faces the north pole is different from the side that faces the south pole, and this distinction in those sides is something witnessed by pure reason. It cannot be said: This judgment is the instinct of the estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya), not the instinct of the rational faculty. For we say: This statement necessitates sophistry. Because if we find from pure instinct (al-fiṭra) a decisive judgment with no doubt at all, we must judge its correctness. If we were to say: There occurs in us an estimative faculty...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the seventh argument, with an objection and partial answer. The argument uses the intuitive intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) to assert that there are infinite spaces outside the world. We necessarily know that the side facing the north pole is distinct from that facing the south pole—a distinction perceived by pure reason. The objector claims this judgment belongs to the estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) rather than reason. Razi refutes this by arguing that denying such decisive instincts leads to sophistry (safsafa). If pure instinct gives a certain judgment, it must be accepted. The argument is cut off in the text but clearly rejects the objection.

Misconception: That the judgment of infinite external spaces is merely a product of the estimative faculty and not rationally valid.
Correction: Razi argues that this judgment is from pure instinct (fiṭra) and is decisive and indubitable; rejecting it leads to sophistry.

The Three Distinctions of Convex and Concave Surfaces
Enumeration of three differences and conclusion for a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three differences between convex and concave surfaces serve as a proof for the necessity of a preponderator (external cause) despite the unity of nature.

Core Idea
Unity of nature cannot explain diversity; a preponderator is needed.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah uses a simple geometric observation to establish a key philosophical principle: when a single nature produces diverse effects, an external cause (preponderator) must be at work. The author enumerates three distinctions between convex and concave surfaces, and then points out that despite these differences, the underlying nature is one, leading to the conclusion that a preponderator is required. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Identify the three distinctions
List the three differences between convex and concave: (1) one is convex, the other concave; (2) convex exterior, concave interior; (3) convex larger, concave smaller.
Notice how the differences are paired.
3
Understand the conclusion
The key move: despite these three differences, the nature (طبيعة) of both surfaces is one. The author uses this to argue that an external preponderator must cause the specification.
This is the core argument for a creator in kalam.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
The argument is: (1) there is unity of nature, (2) there is diversity in effects, (3) therefore, a preponderator is needed. This is a form of the argument from specification.
Connect to broader theological proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : في كون أحدهما محدباً ، وكون الثاني مقعراً . وثانيها : في كون المحدب خارجاً ، والمقعر داخلاً وثالثها : في كون المحدب أعظم ، كون المقعر أصغر . فههنا الطبيعة واحدة ، مع أنه حصل هذا النوع من الاختلاف
The first: regarding one of them being convex and the second being concave. The second: regarding the convex being exterior and the concave interior. The third: regarding the convex being greater and the concave smaller. So here the nature is one, while this type of difference has occurred.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents three distinctions between a convex and a concave surface as part of a larger argument for the necessity of a preponderator (مرجح) in the natural world. The author enumerates three points: (1) One surface is convex, the other concave. (2) The convex is outside, the concave inside. (3) The convex is larger, the concave smaller. Despite these differences, the underlying nature (طبيعة) is one and the same. The conclusion is that this diversity in effects, despite the unity of nature, cannot arise without a preponderator that specifies which part becomes convex and which concave. This is a typical kalam proof showing that a simple essence cannot produce varied effects without an external cause.

Key Terms
محدب
مقعر
طبيعة
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is just describing a geometrical observation.
Correction: It is actually a proof for the necessity of a preponderator (creator) because unity of nature cannot produce such differences on its own.

Checkpoint

A) That nature is diverse.
B) That a preponderator is needed to explain the differences despite unity of nature.
C) That convex surfaces are always larger.
D) That concave surfaces are always interior.

Key Takeaway
The three distinctions between convex and concave demonstrate that a unified nature cannot produce diverse effects without an external preponderator, supporting the existence of a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to accept that these differences demand a specific Chooser? What would that mean for how you live today?
?
When you look at the diversity in your own life—your abilities, circumstances, relationships—do you attribute them to blind nature or to the intentional choice of a preponderator? How does your answer shape your gratitude or lack of it? Will you acknowledge today that every detail of your life is specified by a wise Creator?
?
The passage shows that one nature cannot produce diversity without an external cause. Where in your life have you assumed that your own nature—your habits, character, or tendencies—can change on its own? What does this lesson teach you about your need for a preponderator to transform you? Will you turn to Him now?
