فإن قالوا : هب أن الأمر كذلك ، إلا أنا نقول : فلم وقعت حركة الفلك على هذا الوجه الخاص المعين ، دون سائر الوجوه المحتملة ؟ فنقول : هذا هو النقض الثاني ، ولنا في الجواب عنه مقامات : المقام الأول : إنا قد دللنا على حدوث العالم في الوقت الذي حدث فيه ، وحدوثه أيضاً قبل ذلك ممكناً . إذ لو كان حدوثه قبل ذلك ممتنعاً لذاته ثم انقلب ممكناً لذاته لكان الشيء الواحد قد انقلب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : فحدوثه بعد أن حدث بدلاً عنه أيضاً ممكن بعين هذا الدليل . فيثبت : أن ابتداء حدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت وقبله وبعده : ممكن . وأن ذلك الإمكان ثابت في كل الأوقات على السوية . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فإن اختصاص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت المعين ، مع كونه مساوياً لسائر الأوقات ، رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وأنه محال . فهذا تمام تقرير الدليل المذكور . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : النقض المذكور إنما يتوجه على هذا الدليل ، إذا أقمتم البرهان على أن جرم هذا الفلك المعين ، قابل للحركة على هذا الوجه المعين، وقابل لسائر الحركات المضادة لها ، وعلى هذا التقرير فإنه يلزم أن يكون اختصاص جرم الفلك المعين ، وبهذه الحركة المعينة ، رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح . فعليكم أن تقيموا الدلالة على أن ذات الفلك المعين قابلة لجميع أنواع الحركات المختلفة ، حتى يتوجه النقض . ومعلوم أن هذه المقدمة ليست بينة لذاتها ، لأن الحركة من المشرق إلى المغرب مضادة للحركة من المغرب إلى المشرق ومخالفة لها . ولا يلزم من كون الشيء قابلاً لصفة ، كونه قابلاً لما يخالف تلك الصفة وتضادها . لأنه لم يثبت في العقل : أن الماهيات المختلفة يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والأحكام ، فمن المحتمل أن يقال : جرم هذا الفلك لا يقبل إلا هذا النوع من الحركة ، وبهذا التقدير لم يكن اختصاص هذا الفلك المعين بهذه الحركة المعينة رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح ، بخلاف الدليل الذي ذكرناه . لأنا قلنا : العالم لما حدث في ذلك الوقت ، فلو كان حدوثه قبل ذلك ممتنعاً لزم أن يقال : إنه انتقل من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي ، وهو محال . أما ههنا فجرم الفلك لم ينتقل من نوع من الحركة إلى نوع آخر ، بل الحاصل فيه ليس إلا هذا ، فلم يبعد أن يقال : الممكن ليس إلا هذا النوع ، أما سائر الأنواع فهي ممتنعة أبداً . فظهر الفرق بين البابين .
If they say: 'Granted that it is so, but we say: why did the motion of the sphere occur in this specific determined manner, to the exclusion of other possible manners?' We say: This is the second objection, and we have stations in answering it. The first station: We have demonstrated the temporal origination (huduth) of the world at the time it occurred, and that its origination before that was also possible. For if its origination before that had been impossible in itself (mumtani' li-dhatihi) and then turned into possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi), the same thing would have changed from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility, which is absurd. Also, its origination after it occurred, i.e., in place of that time, is also possible by the same proof. Thus it is established that the beginning of the world's origination at that time, before it, and after it is possible, and that this possibility is equally present in all times. If this is the case, then the specification of the world's origination to that particular time, while it was equivalent to all other times, would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator, which is impossible. This is the full articulation of the aforementioned proof. Now that you know this, we say: The aforementioned objection only applies to this proof if you establish the demonstration that the body of this specific sphere is receptive to motion in this specific manner and also receptive to the opposite motions. On this assumption, it would follow that the specification of the body of this sphere to this specific motion is a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. So you must produce the evidence that the essence of this specific sphere is receptive to all kinds of different motions for the objection to apply. But it is known that this premise is not self-evident, because motion from east to west is opposed to motion from west to east and differs from it. And it does not necessarily follow from something's being receptive to a quality that it is receptive to what opposes that quality and contradicts it. For it has not been established in reason that different essences must be equal in their concomitants and rulings. So it is possible to say: The body of this sphere does not accept except this type of motion. On this assumption, the specification of this specific sphere to this specific motion would not be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator, unlike the proof we mentioned. For we said: When the world occurred at that time, if its origination before that had been impossible, it would have to be said that it changed from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility, which is absurd. But here, the body of the sphere did not change from one type of motion to another type; rather, what exists in it is only this, so it is not far-fetched to say: The possible is only this type, while the other types are always impossible. Thus the difference between the two cases has become clear.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and the author's first station (maqam) in response. The objection asks: granted that the motion determines the poles, why did the motion occur in this specific manner rather than other possible manners? The author answers by first citing the proof of the world's temporal origination. He argues that the world's origination at a specific time, while being equally possible at all times, would be a preponderance without a preponderator—but this is only problematic if the celestial body could accept contrary motions. The author then points out that this premise is not proven; in fact, it is not necessarily true that the sphere can accept opposite motions, because essences do not entail all properties. He illustrates with the opposition between east-west and west-east motion. Thus, the specification of the sphere's motion to one type does not require a preponderator if other motions are intrinsically impossible for it. This is different from the case of the world's origination, where the world did change from possibility of non-existence to existence. Here, the sphere never changed from one motion to another; it only ever had one motion. The key is the distinction between a change in state (which would require a preponderator) and a permanent property (which does not).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any specification of a possible without a preponderator is impossible, but the author distinguishes cases where the thing had alternative possibilities from those where it did not.
Correction: Specification requires a cause only when there is genuine alternative possibility; if only one state is possible, no cause is needed.

Checkpoint

B) The world changed from possible to actual, while the celestial body never changed from one motion to another.
C) The world has a preponderator, while the celestial body does not.
D) The motions of the celestial body are all possible, while the world's origination was impossible.

Key Takeaway
The specification of the sphere's motion does not require a preponderator because it never had an alternative possibility; unlike the world's origination, it did not change from one state to another.

How would accepting that change your contentment?
?
The proof of the world's origination forces you to admit a Preponderator behind its specific time. Have you truly internalized that your own existence at this moment—your birth, your family, your capacities—also required a Preponderator? What would it mean for you to live every day as if you were specifically chosen to exist now?

The author distinguishes between a change (the world's coming to be) and a constant state (the sphere's fixed motion). Are there beliefs or habits you hold that you assume could have been otherwise, but in reality they have always been the only possibility for you? Are you seeking unnecessary 'reasons' for what is simply your given nature? How does this affect your reliance on Allah?

Proof from the Correlatives of Agent and Actuality
First argument using correlative nature of agent and product to prove eternal actual agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the correlative relationship between agent and product leads to the conclusion that the Necessary Being must be an eternally actual agent.

Core Idea
Agent and product are inseparable correlatives; eternal agency requires an eternal product.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from the nature of correlatives. The philosophers argue that since 'agent' and 'product' are relative terms, they must coexist in the same mode—potential or actual. This leads to the conclusion that if the world had a beginning, God would have transitioned from potential to actual agency, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the correlative relationship
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states that 'maker' and 'made' are correlatives existing together. Grasp that if one is potential, the other is; if actual, the other is actual.
Correlatives like 'father' and 'son' exist together; here the same logic applies to agent and product.
2
Apply the principle to the world's eternity
Follow the conditional argument: if the world is not eternal, then in eternity the product (world) existed only potentially, so the agent (God) was a potential agent. When the world later becomes actual, God becomes an actual agent.
3
Identify the implication of transition
Recognize that a transition from potential to actual agency requires an external cause, otherwise the agent would have been actual forever. This would make God dependent on something else, implying contingency.
This is the crucial step: the argument shows that originating the world would make God contingent, which is impossible.
4
Conclude the necessity of eternal actual agency
Understand that therefore, the Necessary Being must be an actual agent from eternity, and consequently the world must be eternal, because the correlatives must coexist.
Notice the argument also mentions that the external cause would lead to an infinite regress, reinforcing the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن للقوم أن يقولوا : الصانع والفاعل من مقولة المضاف . فالصانع مقول بالقياس إلى المصنوع ، كما أن المصنوع مقول بالقياس إلى الصانع ، والمضافان موجودان معاً. فإن كان المصنوع موجوداً بالقوة ، كان الصانع صانعاً بالقوة ، وإن كان المصنوع موجوداً بالفعل ، كان الصانع صانعاً بالفعل . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو لم يكن العالم أزلياً ، لما كان المصنوع موجوداً بالفعل في الأزل بل بالقوة . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما كان الصانع صانعاً بالفعل في الأزل ، بل بالقوة . ثم إذا وجد فيما لا يزال المصنوع فقد صار الصانع فيما لا يزال صانعاً بالفعل. فيثبت : أن العالم لو كان محدثاً لزم أن يقال : الصانع كان صانعاً بالقوة ، ثم صار صانعاً بالفعل . وكل ما كان بالقوة ثم صار بالفعل، فانتقاله من القوة إلى الفعل ، ومن العدم إلى الوجود لا يكون لذاته ، وإلا لكان بالفعل أبداً . بل لا بد وأن يكون ذلك الانتقال لأجل أن غيره نقله من القوة إلى الفعل ، فلو كان العالم محدثاً ، لاحتاج الصانع إلى شيء ينقله من كونه صانعاً بالقوة إلى كونه صانعاً بالفعل ، وكل من كان كذلك ، كان محتاجاً في لوازم ذاته إلى الغير، وكل من كان كذلك ، كان ممكناً لذاته . وهذا على واجب الوجود : محال . وأيضاً : فإن هذا الشيء لا يكون مبدأ أولاً ، بل هو يفتقر إلى مبدأ آخر . فيثبت : أن واجب الوجود لذاته ، يجب أن يكون صانعاً بالفعل . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الصانع موجوداً أبداً، ضرورة أن المضافين لا بد وأن يوجدا معاً . والله أعلم.
Know that they (the philosophers) can say: The maker and the agent belong to the category of the relative. For the maker is predicated in relation to the made, just as the made is predicated in relation to the maker, and the two relatives exist together. If the made exists potentially, then the maker is a maker potentially; and if the made exists actually, then the maker is a maker actually. Once this is established, we say: If the world were not eternal, then the made would not exist actually in eternity, but rather potentially. And if that were the case, then the maker would not be a maker actually in eternity, but rather potentially. Then, when the made comes to exist in that which does not cease, the maker becomes a maker actually in that which does not cease. So it is established that if the world were originated, it would necessarily be said: The maker was a maker potentially, then became a maker actually. And everything that was potentially and then became actually, its transition from potentiality to actuality, and from non-existence to existence, is not due to its essence, otherwise it would have been actual forever. Rather, that transition must be because something else transferred it from potentiality to actuality. Thus, if the world were originated, the maker would need something to transfer it from being a maker potentially to being a maker actually. And everything that is like that is needy in the necessities of its essence to another, and everything that is like that is contingent in essence. This is impossible for the Necessary Being. Moreover, this thing would not be a first principle, but rather it would need another principle. So it is established that the Necessary Being in essence must be a maker actually. And if that is the case, then the maker exists eternally, necessitating that the two relatives must exist together. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the nature of correlatives. It begins by noting that 'agent' and 'product' are correlatives, existing together. If the product exists potentially, the agent is potential; if actually, actual. Then it applies to the world: if the world is not eternal, then in eternity it existed only potentially, so the agent was potential. But when the product comes into existence later, the agent becomes actual. This transition from potential to actual requires an external cause, otherwise it would have been actual always. So the world's origination would imply that the agent (God) needed something to bring Him from potential to actual agency, making Him contingent—impossible for the Necessary Being. Therefore, the agent must be actually productive from eternity, and the world must be eternal. The argument also adds that whatever transitions needs another, and that other would then be the true first principle, leading to regress. Hence the Necessary Being must be eternally actual agent.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being must be eternally an actual agent, and this entails the eternity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you approaching Him as if He is only potentially available until you call upon Him, or do you truly believe He is constantly acting in your life right now? How would your daily awareness change if you internalized that God's agency is never dormant?

?
The passage implies that God's attributes are inseparable from His essence. Is there an attribute of God that you treat as if it is only potential in your life—like His mercy, justice, or power? What step can you take today to live as if that attribute is actually present and active right now?

The Necessary Existent Must Be an Actual Creator
Argument that the Necessary Existent must be an actual creator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Existent cannot be a mere potential creator and must be an actual creator, based on the arguments of dependence and primacy.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent cannot be dependent; it must be an actual creator.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful argument to prove that the Necessary Existent (Allah) must be an actual creator, not a potential one. If He were not actually creating, He would depend on something else to bring His actions into being—contradicting His necessary existence. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for our understanding of God's eternal creative act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation without analysis. Let the argument's flow settle in your mind.
Notice the two 'if...then' structures.
2
Identify the two conditional arguments
The passage contains two separate but complementary arguments. Identify the condition and conclusion of each.
First: if not actual creator → needy → contingent. Second: if not actual creator → not first principle.
3
Understand the conclusion and final note
The conclusion is that the Necessary Existent must be an actual creator. The final sentence adds that creator and creation coexist eternally as two relata.
This co‑eternity does not mean the world is eternal; it means God's attribute of creating is eternal.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal stakes
Ask yourself: why does this argument matter? It safeguards God's self‑sufficiency and absolute primacy.
If God were only potentially a creator, He would depend on a cause to actualize His potential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك كان محتاجاً في لوازم ذاته إلى الغير، وكل من كان كذلك كان ممكناً لذاته. وهذا على واجب الوجود محال. وأيضاً: فإن هذا الشيء لا يكون مبدأ أولاً، بل هو يفتقر إلى مبدأ آخر. فيثبت: أن واجب الوجود لذاته يجب أن يكون صانعاً بالفعل كان الأمر كذلك، كان ومتى الصانع موجوداً أبداً، ضرورة أن المضافين لا بد وأن يوجدا معاً [والله أعلم].
Likewise, if it were needy in the necessities of its essence to another, and everything that is so is contingent in itself, and this is impossible for the Necessary Existent. Also, this thing would not be a first principle, but rather it would depend on another principle. So it is established that the Necessary Existent in itself must be an actual creator. If so, then the creator exists always, necessarily because the two relatives must exist together [and God knows best].
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Necessary Existent must be an actual creator. The argument proceeds as follows: If the Necessary Existent were not an actual creator—meaning it is not currently producing the world—then it would be dependent on something else for the necessaries of its essence. But anything that is dependent on another for its essential necessities is contingent (mumkin) in itself. However, contingency cannot apply to the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud). Therefore, the Necessary Existent must be an actual creator. Moreover, if it were not an actual creator, it would not be the first principle; it would depend on another principle. Hence it is established that the Necessary Existent must be an actual creator. If that is the case, the creator exists eternally, because the two relata (the creator and creation) must coexist simultaneously. [And God knows best] indicates the author's humility or a possible end of the argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Necessary Existent could be a potential creator who only creates at a later time.
Correction: This argument shows that potentiality would imply need and thus contingency, which is incompatible with necessary existence. The Necessary Existent must be an actual creator from eternity.

Key Takeaway

?
This argument shows that any need in the Necessary Existent would imply contingency. But you are contingent and full of needs. How does acknowledging your total dependence on the Necessary Existent reshape your sense of self-importance and reliance on others?

The Eternity of the Effect from the Eternity of the Cause
Proof that the eternity of the cause implies the eternity of the effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternity of a cause logically necessitates the eternity of its effect through the nature of causation as a relative attribute.

Core Idea
If the cause is eternal, its effect must also be eternal.

This passage presents a crucial argument in Islamic philosophical theology: if a cause is eternal, then its effect must also be eternal. The argument proceeds by examining the causal relation itself—whether it is originated or eternal—and uses the principle that an infinite regress is impossible. You'll see how the logic unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the terms 'haditha' (originated) and 'qadima' (eternal).
Notice that the text ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The passage splits the causal relation into two possibilities: it is either originated or eternal. Locate where this distinction is made.
This is a classic case of 'division of the possible' in kalam.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
Follow the reasoning: if the causation were originated, it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Understand why this is a strong proof.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered absurd in this tradition.
4
Understand the relative attribute
The passage then states that causation is a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya), which requires both the cause and the effect to exist. Grasp why this implies the effect's eternity.
A relative attribute cannot exist without its two relata.
5
Reconstruct the conclusion
Since the causation is eternal and requires the effect, the effect must also be eternal. Try to complete the missing conclusion in your own words.
The text breaks off, but the logical conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الثاني: وهو أنه لما ثبت هذا لزم من قدم المؤثر قدم الأثر والذي يدل عليه: هو أن هذه المؤثرية إما أن تكون حادثة أو قديمة، لا جائز أن تكون حادثة، وإلا لافتقرت إلى محدث، فيكون إحداث تلك المؤثرية من مؤثرية أخرى ولزم التسلسل وهو محال. فيثبت: أن تلك المؤثرية قديمة. لكن كون الشيء مؤثرا في غيره صفة إضافية، لا يعقل ثبوتها إلا مع ثبوت المضافين، لأن المؤثر في الأثر كما أن الأثر أثر للمؤثر. فلما ثبت
As for the second station: That is, when this is established, it follows from the eternity of the cause the eternity of the effect. What indicates this is: this influencing (mu'thiriyya) is either originated (haditha) or eternal (qadima). It cannot be originated, for then it would need an originator, resulting in another influencing and leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. So it is established that this influencing is eternal. But a thing being an influencer in another is a relative attribute; it cannot be realized except with the realization of the two relata, because the influencer is related to the effect just as the effect is effect of the influencer. So when it is established...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the second station of the argument, which builds on the previous conclusion that the causal relation is additional to the essence. Now the question is whether this causal relation is eternal or originated. If it were originated, it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be eternal. However, since the causal relation is a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya), it requires both relata: the cause and the effect. So if the causal relation is eternal, the effect must also be eternal. The argument is cut off at 'فلما ثبت' but the conclusion is implied: the effect is co-eternal with the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument definitively proves that the world is eternal.
Correction: The passage only proves that if a cause is eternal, its effect must also be eternal. It does not establish that the world (as an effect) is eternal; it only works under the assumption of an eternal cause. Many Islamic theologians reject the eternity of the world, so the argument may be presented to be refuted later or to clarify a conditional relationship.

Checkpoint
Why must the causal relation (المؤثرية) be eternal rather than originated?
A) Because it is a relative attribute.
B) Because if it were originated, it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress.
C) Because the cause is eternal.
D) Because the effect is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of the cause logically necessitates the eternity of the effect because the causal relation itself must be eternal, and as a relative attribute, it requires both relata to coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that if your relationship with a cause is eternal (like your faith in Allah), the effects must also be eternal. Examine your life: Are the effects of your faith, such as obedience and trust, consistently present? Or do they appear only sometimes, as if your faith is intermittent? What does that suggest about the eternity of your faith as a cause?
?
Consider the infinite regress argument: If you keep seeking temporary fixes for your spiritual weaknesses (new resolutions, new habits) without addressing the root cause, you are creating an endless cycle. What is the one root cause in your life that, if made eternal (through sincere reliance on Allah), would stop the regress and produce lasting change?
?
This lesson shows that a causal relation cannot be originated without leading to an infinite chain. Your actions have consequences that are like effects. Are you aware of the eternal weight of your deeds? If a single sin can have eternal repercussions unless repented, does this urgency reflect in how you live your daily life? How can you make your good deeds flow from an eternal cause that is pleasing to Allah?

The Trilemma of Bringing into Existence
Razi presents a trilemma analysis of the meaning of bringing into existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's trilemma regarding the meaning of 'bringing into existence' and how it leads to the conclusion that the world must be eternal.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'bringing into existence' forces a trilemma that ends in the world's eternity.

Razi's original argument for the eternity of the world is here restated in a sharper form. Instead of starting from the agent's power, he now analyzes the very concept of 'bringing into existence' (إيجاد). By splitting this concept into three possible meanings, he shows that each leads to an unacceptable consequence unless the world is eternal. This passage is a masterclass in logical triage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma
Identify the three possibilities for what 'إيجاد' could be: the agent's essence, the object's essence, or a third thing.
The trilemma is stated in the first few lines.
2
Evaluate the first two options
See why identifying إيجاد with either the agent or the object leads to contradiction: if identical to agent's essence, then effect must be eternal; if identical to object's essence, the world is self-caused.
Both are ruled out immediately.
3
Analyze the third option
Consider the attribute 'causing existence' (موجدية) and its implications: if it is originated, it requires another bringing into existence (infinite regress); if eternal, then the object must be eternal.
This is the crucial step that forces the world's eternity.
4
Reach the conclusion
Observe that both possibilities for الموجدية (originated or eternal) lead to an eternal world, so the argument concludes that the world must be eternal.
The phrase 'وبالله التوفيق' ends the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يعبر عن هذه الحجة بعبارة أخرى: فيقال: لو كان العالم حادثاً، لامتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا بإيجاد الفاعل. فنقول: إيجاد الفاعل: إما أن يكون عبارة عن ذات الفاعل، أو عن ذات المفعول، أو عن أمر ثالث. فإن كان الأول، لزم من دوام ذات الفاعل دوام ذات المفعول. وإن كان الثاني كان معناه: أن العالم إنما وجد بنفسه. لأنا لما قلنا: إن العالم وجد بإيجاد الفاعل. ثم قلنا: إن إيجاد الفاعل للعالم هو نفس العالم، كان معنى هذا الكلام: أن العالم وجد بنفسه، لكن كونه كذلك، يمنع من إسناده إلى الفاعل، وذلك محال. فبقي الثالث. فنقول: لا شك أن الموجدية صفة لذات الفاعل، فهي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى إيجاد آخر. وهو محال. وإن كانت قديمة، لزم من قدمها قدم المفعول، لأن كون الموجد موجداً، حال بقاء المفعول على عدمه الأصلي: محال. وبالله التوفيق.
And this proof may be expressed in another way: It is said: If the world were originated, it would be impossible for it to come into existence except by the bringing into existence of the agent. We say: The bringing into existence of the agent: either it is identical with the essence of the agent, or with the essence of the object, or a third thing. If the first, then from the permanence of the agent's essence follows the permanence of the object's essence. If the second, then its meaning would be that the world came into existence by itself. Because when we said that the world came into existence by the agent's bringing into existence, then we said that the agent's bringing into existence of the world is the same as the world itself, the meaning of this statement would be that the world came into existence by itself. But its being so prevents ascribing it to the agent, and that is impossible. So the third remains. We say: There is no doubt that 'causing existence' is an attribute of the essence of the agent. So if it were originated, it would need another bringing into existence, which is impossible. And if it were eternal, then from its eternity follows the eternity of the object, because the existence of the one who causes existence while the object remains in its original non-existence is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This is a restatement of the same argument using a trilemma about the meaning of 'bringing into existence' (إيجاد). First, if it is identical to the agent's essence, then since the essence is eternal, the effect must be eternal. Second, if it is identical to the object's essence, then the world is self-caused, which is absurd. So it must be a third thing: an attribute of the agent called 'causing existence' (موجدية). This attribute, if originated, would require another bringing into existence (infinite regress); if eternal, then the object must be eternal (since an eternal attribute of causing would imply a permanent effect). Thus the world must be eternal.

Key Terms
إيجاد
الموجد
الموجدية
دوام
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the third option (إيجاد as an attribute) avoids the problem entirely.
Correction: The third option merely shifts the problem to the attribute 'causing existence'. Whether that attribute is originated or eternal, it still forces the world to be eternal (either through infinite regress or through the eternity of the effect).

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility for the meaning of 'إيجاد' in this argument?
A) The agent's essence
B) The object's essence
C) An attribute of the agent
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Razi's trilemma shows that any attempt to define 'bringing into existence' as something other than the agent's essence or the object's essence still forces the world to be eternal, either through infinite regress or through the eternity of the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this argument push you to deepen your understanding of God's attributes and His relationship to the universe?
?
When faced with an argument that seems to contradict your faith, do you tend to dismiss it or engage with it? This lesson demands that you follow a rigorous logical path. What does your reaction to this argument reveal about your intellectual humility and commitment to truth?

The Complete Efficient Cause Requires a Temporal Condition
The author presents the ninth proof that the complete efficient cause must include a temporal condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the complete efficient cause cannot be solely eternal and must include a temporal condition to avoid circularity and preserve God's ability to choose.

Core Idea
The complete cause must be temporal to allow a temporal effect.

In this ninth proof, the author argues that if the complete cause of the world were eternal, the world would be eternal. He divides the possibilities and shows that only a cause that includes a temporal condition avoids circularity. This lesson unpacks this logical argument and its implications for divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is a single continuous proof.
2
Identify the three-part division
Find the three possibilities for the complete efficient cause: God's essence alone, with eternal things, or with temporal things.
3
Understand the rejection of eternal causes
Why do the first two options lead to an eternal effect and undermine God's ability to act or refrain?
The author calls this 'yqdah' (undermines).
4
Analyse the third option and circularity
The third option (with temporal things) is necessary but must avoid circularity: the temporal event cannot be the cause of the cause's causality.
Circularity (al-dawr) is impossible.
5
Reflect on the author's generalisation
Notice that the author claims all possible conditions (time, benefit, removal of impediment) are covered by this division.
This shows the proof is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لا شك أنه ما لم يوجد المؤثر التام ، المستجمع جميع جهات المؤثرية، فإنه لا يقع الفعل فذلك المؤثر التام : إما أن يكون هو ذات واجب الوجود فقط ، أو ذاته مع أمور أخرى قديمة ، أو ذاته مع أمور أخرى حادثة . فإن كان الحق هو الأول أو الثاني ، لزم من قدم ذلك المؤثر، قدم ذلك الأثر، وذلك يقدح في كونه متمكناً من الفعل والترك معاً، والثالث باطل . لأن حدوث الحادث، موقوف على حصول المؤثر التام في المؤثرية، فلو كان حصول المؤثر التام في المؤثرية، موقوفاً على حدوث الحادث، لزم الدور . وهو محال واعلم أن هذا التقسيم منحصر مضبوط، سواء قيل : إنه يعتبر في تلك المؤثرية حضور وقت، أو حصول مصلحة، أو زوال مانع . فإن الكل داخل فيما ذكرناه . والأجوبة التسعة المذكورة في الحجة الأولى يرجع حاصلها إلى توقيف مؤثرية الله تعالى في وجود العالم على حصول شرط حادث. وقد دللنا
The ninth proof: There is no doubt that as long as the complete efficient cause, comprising all aspects of effect-causation, has not come into existence, the act does not occur. So that complete efficient cause: either it is the essence of the Necessary Existent alone, or His essence with other eternal things, or His essence with other temporal things. If the truth is the first or second, then from the pre-eternity of that cause would follow the pre-eternity of that effect, and that undermines His being able to act and refrain simultaneously. And the third is false. Because the occurrence of the temporal event is dependent upon the attainment of the complete efficient cause in its effect-causation; so if the attainment of the complete efficient cause in its effect-causation were dependent upon the occurrence of the temporal event, circularity would follow, which is impossible. And know that this division is closed and precise, whether it is said that in that effect-causation there is consideration of the presence of a time, or the attainment of a benefit, or the removal of an impediment. For all of that is included in what we mentioned. And the nine answers mentioned in the first proof, their gist returns to making the effect-causation of God, exalted, in the existence of the world dependent upon the attainment of a temporal condition. And we have demonstrated...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة التاسعة) concerning the complete efficient cause (المؤثر التام). The author states that no act occurs without the complete cause that encompasses all aspects of causation. He then divides this cause into three possibilities: either it is God's essence alone, or His essence with other eternal things, or His essence with other temporal things. If it is the first or second, then the cause being pre-eternal would necessitate the effect being pre-eternal, which contradicts God's ability to choose to act or refrain. The third option (including temporal things) is the only valid one, but it must avoid circularity: the temporal event cannot be the cause of the complete cause's attainment of causality, as that would be a vicious circle. The author then notes that this division covers all possibilities, whether the condition is time, benefit, or removal of an impediment. Finally, he remarks that the nine answers to the first proof reduce to making God's causation dependent on a temporal condition, which he has demonstrated.

Key Terms
المؤثر التام
المؤثرية
الدور
شرط حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The complete efficient cause must be eternal because God is eternal.
Correction: The author demonstrates that including a temporal condition does not diminish God's power and avoids the problem of an eternal effect, thus preserving divine freedom.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, why cannot the complete efficient cause be God's essence alone or with other eternal things?
A) Because that would imply the world is eternal.
B) Because that would make God unable to create.
C) Because that would contradict God's unity.
D) Because that would require a temporal condition.

Key Takeaway
The complete efficient cause of the temporal world must include a temporal condition to avoid circularity and preserve divine freedom to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God could have created the world at any moment He willed, or do you secretly think He was compelled to create at the moment He did? How does recognizing that His act depended on a temporal condition (His own choice) affect your trust in His wisdom?

If the complete cause included a temporal condition such as the removal of an impediment, what impediments might have existed in your own life that prevented you from receiving Allah's mercy? Are you willing to remove those impediments?
?
The author shows that without a temporal condition, Allah would not be truly free to act or refrain. In your own life, do you allow Allah the freedom to act in His time, or do you demand immediate answers? How can you surrender to His timing?

Conclusion: Necessary Cause Entails Eternal Effect
The author concludes the eleven proofs and argues that a necessary cause entails an eternal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a necessary cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat) must produce its effect eternally, and how this argument is used to refute the notion of a capable agent as interpreted by the opponents.

Core Idea
A cause by its very essence cannot delay its effect without contradiction.

This passage concludes a series of eleven proofs against the opponents' interpretation of divine agency. The author now establishes a crucial principle: if God's influence on the world is by virtue of His essence rather than by voluntary choice, then the world must be eternal. He supports this by citing the consensus of the mutakallimun and offering a logical argument that a necessary cause cannot produce its effect after an infinite delay without an external specifying factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the conclusion of the eleven proofs to the principle about causality.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author argues that if God's influence (ta'thir) is by His essence or necessary attributes, then His effects must be eternal. Locate this claim in the text.
Look for the phrase 'يلزم من قدمه الآثار والأفعال والنتائج'.
3
Examine the consensus argument
The author references the mutakallimun who, after proving the world's temporality, argued that if the influencer were a necessitator by essence, the world would be eternal. Understand how this consensus supports the author's position.
This shows that even the opponents agree on the logical entailment.
4
Analyze the additional argument
The author presents a second argument: if a necessary cause exists without its effect for infinite time, and then the effect occurs without a specifying factor, it would be uncaused—which is absurd. Therefore, the effect must be eternal.
This rules out the possibility of a necessary cause producing a temporal effect.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument challenges the view that God is a capable agent (al-qadir) who chooses to create the world in time. The author uses it to show that such a view is impossible if God's influence is by essence.
This ties back to the overall purpose of the eleven proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا أحد عشر وجهاً ، في تقرير أن القول بالقادر على التفسير الذي ذكروه : محال . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ظهر أن تأثيره تعالى في تكوين العالم ، وفي تكوين سائر آثاره إنما كان لعين ذاته ، أو للأمور اللازمة لذاته . وقد اتفق الكل على أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، فإنه يلزم من قدمه الآثار والأفعال والنتائج . ولهذا السبب ، فإن المتكلمين بعد أن أقاموا الدلالة على حدوث العالم ، قالوا : لو كان المؤثر في حدوث العالم موجباً بالذات . لزم من قدمه ، قدم العالم . وذلك يدل على أنهم كانوا معترفين بأن كل ما كان تأثيره لذاته ، لزم من دوامه ، دوام الأثر . وأيضاً : فإذا كان المؤثر الموجب بالذات حاصلاً أنه كان منفكاً مع عن الأثر، مدة غير متناهية . ثم حصل الأثر بعد ذلك ، من غير أن اختص ذلك الوقت ، بإرادة معينة ، أو بنوع آخر من الأمور المقتضية للرجحان . فحينئذ يكون ذلك الحدوث في ذلك الوقت : حدوثاً ، لا لمؤثر أصلاً ، بل محض الاتفاق . وذلك : باطل : فيثبت : أنه يلزم من دوام الموجب بالذات دوام الأثر والنتيجة . والله أعلم
These are eleven proofs establishing that the statement about the capable agent (al-qadir) according to their interpretation is impossible. When this is established, it becomes clear that His influence (ta'alu) in the formation of the world and in the formation of all His effects is only by virtue of His essence (li-'ayn dhatihi) or by matters necessary to His essence. And everyone has agreed that whenever that is the case, then the effects, actions, and results follow from His eternity. For this reason, the theologians (mutakallimun), after they established the proof of the temporality of the world, said: If the influencer in the temporality of the world were a necessitator by essence, then from His eternity would follow the eternity of the world. That indicates that they recognized that whatever has its influence by its essence, from its perpetuity follows the perpetuity of its effect. Also: If the influencer that is a necessitator by essence is such that it was separate from the effect for an infinite duration, and then the effect occurred after that, without that time being specified by a particular will or another type of thing that necessitates preponderance, then that occurrence would be an occurrence without any influencer at all, but pure coincidence. That is false. Thus it is established that from the perpetuity of the necessitator by essence follows the perpetuity of the effect and result. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage serves as the conclusion of the eleven proofs that have been presented. The author first states that the eleven proofs have shown that the interpretation of the 'capable agent' (al-qadir) as given by the opponents is impossible. Now, if God's influence (ta'thir) in creating the world and all its effects is not through such a power, then it must be by virtue of His very essence (li-'ayn dhatihi) or by attributes that are necessary to His essence. The author then notes a consensus: whenever the cause is by essence, the effects necessarily follow from the cause's eternity. To illustrate, the author points to the mutakallimun (theologians) who, after proving the world's temporality (huduth), argued that if the influencer were a necessitator by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat), the world would be eternal. This shows they agreed that a cause's perpetuity entails the effect's perpetuity. The author then offers an additional argument: suppose the necessitator by essence exists for an infinite duration without its effect, and then the effect occurs at some later time without any specific will or preponderating factor to specify that moment. In that case, the effect would occur without any cause at all—a pure coincidence—which is absurd. Therefore, from the perpetuity of the necessitator by essence, the perpetuity of the effect and result necessarily follows. This argument aims to show that if God were a necessary cause, the universe would be eternal, which contradicts the Islamic doctrine of the world's temporality. The author upholds the latter view.

Key Terms
تأثيره
موجب بالذات
دوام
حدوث
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary cause can delay its effect for an arbitrarily long time without any additional factor.
Correction: The author argues that such a delay would make the effect uncaused, which is absurd; therefore, a necessary cause must produce its effect eternally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if the necessitator by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat) existed without its effect for an infinite time and then the effect appeared without a preponderating factor?
A) The effect would be eternal
B) The effect would be impossible
C) The occurrence would have no cause
D) The cause would become non-necessary

Key Takeaway
A cause that acts by its very essence must have its effect co-eternal; otherwise, the effect would be uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence were necessary from God's essence, you would have no choice but to exist. But He created you by His free will. How does this truth change the way you receive each day of your life? What would you do differently today if you truly believed that your very existence is a free gift from God?

The passage argues that a delayed effect without a cause would be mere coincidence. In your life, do you sometimes attribute events to 'coincidence' instead of seeing them as caused by God's will? How can you train your heart to see every moment as intentionally brought about by Him?
?
If God's causation were necessary, worship would be automatic, but He made you free to choose. How are you using that freedom to respond to the One who chose to create you? What is one specific choice you will make today to honor that free gift?

The Fourth Proof: Specification of Time
The author presents a proof by division for the world's temporal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for the world's creation, which demonstrates that the world must have a temporal beginning based on the impossibility of specifying God's intention to a particular time.

Core Idea
God's intention to create cannot be tied to a specific time without absurdity.

This lesson presents a sophisticated logical argument from the Islamic theological tradition demonstrating that the world must have been created in time. The argument, known as the proof from specification of time (تخصيص الوقت), examines two possibilities for God's intention in creating the world and shows both lead to contradictions unless the world is temporally originated. It is a key argument in establishing the doctrine of the world's creation (حدوث العالم).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the translation. Get an overview of the dense logical argument without analyzing details yet.
The argument is divided into two main branches: specific time and non-specific time.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Notice that the argument splits into two cases: God intends to create at a specific time, or He does not specify a time. Each case is examined for contradictions.
3
Follow the first division's sub-arguments
Trace how the specific time case leads to either the world being created in eternity (if the time is eternal) or an infinite regress of times (if the time is created). Both are impossible.
This is the core of the proof: showing that specification leads to absurdity.
4
Understand the second division's implication
See why non-specification of time would imply the world is eternal (pre-eternity), which contradicts the claim that the world is created.
This seals the proof: both alternatives fail, so the world must be created in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو قصد إلى إحداث العالم، لكان إما أن يكون ذلك القصد مختصاً بوقت معين ، أو غير مختص بوقت معين . أما الأول : فكما إذا قصد إلى إحداث العالم في الوقت الفلاني وأما الثاني : فكما إذا قصد إلى إحداث العالم ، ولم يقصد إلى تخصيص إحداثه بوقت معين . وأما القسم الأول : فهو باطل . لأن ذلك الوقت المعين إما أن يقال : إنه كان حاضراً في الأزل ، أو ما كان حاضراً في الأزل ، فإن كان ذلك الوقت حاضراً في الأزل مع أن إله العالم قصد إحداث العالم في هذا الوقت ، فحينئذ يلزم حدوث العالم في الأزل . وعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم كون العالم سرمدياً . وأما إن كان ذلك الوقت غير حاضر في الأزل ، كان ذلك الوقت حادثاً ، ولا بد له من محدث . وهو الله سبحانه . وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول وهو أنه إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث ذلك الوقت في وقت خاص ، أو قصد إحداثه مطلقاً . فإن كان الأول ، افتقر ذلك الوقت إلى وقت آخر ، والكلام فيه كما في الأول ، ويلزم التسلسل . فإما أن تحصل تلك الأوقات الغير المتناهية معاً ، وهو محال بالبديهية. أو على التعاقب ، وهو يوجب القول بحدوث حوادث لا أول لها . وهو المطلوب . وهذا كله إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث العالم في وقت معين . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه تعالى قصد إحداث العالم من غير أن يخصص إحداثه بوقت معين ، بل قصد على وجه يكون موجوداً دائماً أبداً فهذا يوجب القول بالقدم ، وذلك يناقض ثبوت الحدوث.
The fourth proof: If He intended to originate the world, then either that intention was specific to a particular time, or not specific to a particular time. As for the first: it is like when He intended to originate the world at such-and-such a time. As for the second: it is like when He intended to originate the world, and did not intend to specify its origination at a particular time. As for the first division: it is false. Because that particular time either was present in eternity, or was not present in eternity. If that time was present in eternity while the God of the world intended to originate the world at this time, then the origination of the world in eternity necessarily follows. On this supposition, the world being eternal necessarily follows. But if that time was not present in eternity, then that time is originated, and it must have an originator, and He is God, exalted. Then the first division returns: that He, exalted, either intended to originate that time at a specific time, or intended to originate it absolutely. If the first, that time is dependent on another time, and the discussion about it is as in the first, and infinite regress necessarily follows. Then those infinite times either occur together, which is impossible by intuition, or successively, which necessitates asserting the occurrence of originated events that have no beginning. And that is what is sought. All this is if we say that He, exalted, intended to originate the world at a particular time. As for the second division: that it is said that He, exalted, intended to originate the world without specifying its origination at a particular time, but rather intended it in a way that it would exist perpetually, forever. This necessitates asserting pre-eternity, and that contradicts the establishment of origination.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the creation (حدوث) of the world. It is a classification argument that divides the possible intentions of God regarding the time of creation. The argument begins by stating that if God intended to create the world, either that intention is tied to a specific time or it is not. The first case (specific time) is examined: that specific time either existed in eternity (أزل) or did not. If it existed in eternity, then the world would have been created in eternity, implying the world is eternal (سرمدي), which contradicts creation. If it did not exist in eternity, then that time itself is created and requires a creator (God). This returns to the same division: either God intended to create that time at a specific time (leading to infinite regress, تسلسل) or without specification (which would make the time eternal, also impossible). The infinite regress leads to either all times existing together (impossible) or successive times without a beginning (which implies an infinite series of created events, which is the desired conclusion for the proponent of creation). The second main case (no specific time) means God intended the world to exist perpetually, which would make the world eternal (قدم), contradicting the fact that the world is created. Thus, both possibilities lead to contradictions unless the world is created in time. The author follows the logical structure of dividing opposition options and showing each leads to absurdity, establishing the doctrine of temporal creation.

إحداث
وقت معين
أزل
حادث
محدث
تسلسل
قدم
حدوث
سرمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof only refutes the idea of creation at a specific time, but it actually refutes both specific and non-specific intentions, forcing the conclusion that the world must have been created in time by a free agent.
Correction: The argument shows that both alternatives (specific time or no specific time) lead to contradictions, so the only way to avoid contradiction is to hold that the world was created in time, with a beginning.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof conclude if God intended to create the world at a specific time that is present in eternity?
A) The world must be eternal and uncreated.
B) The world would be created in eternity, implying it is eternal.
C) The time itself would need another creator, leading to infinite regress.
D) The world would have no beginning and be self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that the world must have a temporal beginning, as God's intention to create cannot be specified to a time without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you sincerely believed that this world has a definite starting point created by God's free will, how would that change the way you prioritize your daily actions? Do you live as if life is eternal or as if it has a purpose tied to its beginning?
?
When you consider that your own existence is a created event in time, does that make you more aware of your dependence on God every moment? Or do you take time for granted as if it is self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that God's intention to create cannot be attached to a time without contradiction. How does this awareness of God's absolute freedom over time challenge your assumptions about what you 'deserve' from God at specific times in your life?

Two Proofs for God's Eternal Creative Act
Two arguments for God's eternal creativity based on moral and wisdom principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments that God must be eternally creative, derived from the concepts of favor (iḥsān) and perfection (kamāl).

Core Idea
God's perfection requires that He be eternally creative, not temporally inactive.

The author presents two concise proofs that God's creative act is eternal, not a temporal event. These arguments are drawn from moral reasoning about 'the good' and metaphysical reasoning about divine perfection. As you read, notice how each proof appeals to what is rationally necessary for an absolutely perfect being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the two proofs and the start of an objection.
The passage ends mid-sentence; that's fine for now.
2
Understand the first proof (favor-based)
The first proof argues that creating the world is a favor (iḥsān). Since performing a favor is better than omitting it, God's not creating for an infinite past would imply He omitted the better act forever—impossible. Thus He must be eternally creative.
This proof assumes that 'better' is rationally obligatory for God.
3
Understand the second proof (perfection-based)
The second proof states that a being with dominion and disposal is more perfect than one without. If God lacked these in eternity and later acquired them, He would have changed from deficiency to perfection—impossible. So He must have always been sovereign and creative.
4
Identify the objection
The passage begins an objection to the first proof. Note that it says 'If they say: it follows from the first proof the omission of the favor...' The response is cut off, but the objection tries to claim that not creating is not necessarily a fault.
The objection is incomplete; we only see its premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدلائل المستنبطة من الحسن و القبح والحكمة والعبث وهي من وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن الإيجاد للعالم إحسان ، والإحسان أفضل من ترك الإحسان فلو كان الباري سبحانه غير موجد للعالم ، مدة غير متناهية لكان تاركاً للأفضل مدة غير متناهية ، ومرجحاً للأمر الآخر مدة غير متناهية وذلك محال . فوجب كونه فاعلا من الأزل إلى الأبد الحجة الثانية : إن من كان له التصرف النافذ ، والملك العظيم ، والرفع والخفض ، والإبرام والنقض ، أفضل ممن لا يكون له شيء من ذلك . فلو قلنا : إنه تعالى ما كان له في الأزل : ملك ولا ملكوت ولا تصرف ، ثم صار في لا يزال كذلك ، لزم أن يقال : إنه انتقل من النقص إلى الكمال ، وذلك : محال . فإن قالوا : يلزم على الحجة الأولى : ترك الإحسان . وإنما لا
On the proofs derived from the good and the bad, and wisdom and futility: and they are of several aspects. The first proof: that bringing the world into existence is a favor, and a favor is better than omitting the favor. So if the Creator—glorified be He—were not bringer of the world into existence for an infinite duration, He would have been omitting the better act for an infinite duration and preferring the other matter for an infinite duration, and that is impossible. Therefore, it is necessary that He be an agent from eternity past to eternity future. The second proof: that one who has effective disposal, great dominion, raising and lowering, binding and loosening, is better than one who has none of that. So if we say that the Exalted had in eternity neither dominion nor sovereignty nor disposal, and then later became such, it would necessarily be said that He moved from deficiency to perfection, and that is impossible. So if they say: it follows from the first proof the omission of the favor. And but not...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs (hujjatān) that God must be eternally creative, drawn from moral and metaphysical principles. The author first introduces the topic as 'proofs derived from the good and the bad, and wisdom and futility' (al-dalā'il al-mustanbaṭa min al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ wa al-ḥikma wa al-'abath). The first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) runs as follows: creating the world is a favor (iḥsān), and performing a favor is better than omitting it. If God were not an agent of creation for an infinite past, He would have been omitting the better act for infinite time and preferring something else, which is absurd. Therefore, God must be an agent (fā'il) from eternity (al-azal) to eternity (al-abad). The second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) appeals to perfection: one who possesses effective disposal (taṣarruf nāfidh), great dominion (mulk 'aẓīm), and the power to raise and lower, bind and loose, is superior to one lacking these. If God had no dominion or sovereignty in pre-eternity (al-azal) and then acquired it later, that would imply transition from deficiency to perfection—also impossible. Therefore, God must have always been sovereign and creative. The passage then begins an objection (fa-in qālū) to the first proof: 'It entails omission of the favor...' but the text cuts off, leaving the answer incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs claim that God is compelled by an external standard of good and evil.
Correction: The arguments are based on God's own perfection, not on an independent moral standard. The reasoning is internal to God's essence: since God is perfect, He must act in a way consistent with perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā)?
A) God created the world at a specific moment in time.
B) God must be an agent from eternity to eternity.
C) God could have delayed creation without imperfection.
D) The world is eternal and uncreated.

Key Takeaway
God's eternity and perfection entail that He must be eternally creative, as both moral and metaphysical reasoning demonstrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's perfection demands that He be eternally generous and creative, what does that say about your own tendency to withhold goodness? In what area of your life have you been 'omitting the favor'—excusing yourself from kindness or service? Write down one specific act of iḥsān you will do today without delay, and ask God to make you consistent like Him.
?
The second proof states that moving from deficiency to perfection is impossible for God. Yet you and I move from deficiency to perfection every day—we grow, change, and improve. How does this contrast humble you? If you were to stand before God tomorrow, would He find you having used your time to move toward perfection or away from it? What small step can you take right now to turn from a 'deficiency' habit?
?
These proofs argue that God was never inactive—He has always been sovereignly disposing, raising, lowering, binding, loosening. You often complain that your life feels stagnant or that God seems silent. Could it be that you are not looking closely enough at His continuous activity? What one situation in your life right now are you dismissing as 'futile' that actually reveals God's ongoing work? Commit to thanking Him for His perpetual creativity in that situation starting tonight.

The Seventh Proof: Existence Opens the Door to Afflictions
Al-Razi presents the seventh proof that existence is evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that existence is evil relative to non-existence because it entails unavoidable harms, and why this challenges the concept of a wise agent.

Core Idea
Existence brings fear and pain; non-existence is free from harm.

Al-Razi presents the seventh proof that existence is evil. He argues that with existence come afflictions like fear and pain, which are harms. Since non-existence has no pain, existence is worse. A wise agent would not create evil, so this argument poses a problem for theists who claim God is wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, explanation, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the comparison between existence and non-existence.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi states: 'Bringing into existence opens the door to afflictions.' Understand what afflictions (āfāt) mean here: fear of losing benefits, fear of pain, and pain itself.
3
Understand the comparison with non-existence
He contrasts existence (where harm occurs) with non-existence (where no harm is possible). Why is non-existence free from harm? because pain requires life and existence.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Grasp the conclusion and implication
The conclusion: existence is evil compared to non-existence, so a wise agent would not create existence. Reflect on why this is a theological challenge.
The proof is intended to show that the world's existence is incompatible with a wise creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: الإيجاد فتح لباب الآفات. وذلك لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم.
بيان الأول: أن عند حصول الوجود، يحصل الخوف من زوال مصالح الوجود، ويحصل الخوف من حصول الألم. والخوف ألم وضرر، وأما في حال العدم، فما كان فيه مؤلم أصلاً، لأن حصول الألم مشروط بحصول الوجود والحياة، فعند عدم الوجود، امتنع حصول الضرر والخوف. فيثبت أن عند حصول الوجود يحصل الضرر، وعند العدم لا ضرر. فكان هذا الوجود شراً من العدم، والعدم خيراً منه، والحكيم لا يفعل الشر.
فإن قالوا: السؤال عليه من وجوه:
الأول: أن الشر عبارة عن العدم. ألا ترى أن من قتل إنساناً، فذلك القتل شر، وليس هو شر، لأن الآلة كانت قطاعة، ولا لأجل أن القاتل كان قادراً على استعمال تلك الآلة القطاعة بالقوة القوية، ولا لأجل أن عنق المقتول كان قابلاً للقطع، فإن كل ذلك خيرات. بل إنما كان شراً، لأنه زالت الحياة عن المحل القابل للحياة، وهذا الزوال عدم. فثبت: أن الشر ليس إلا العدم. فكيف يقال: الوجود شر؟
الثاني: سلمنا أن الوجود قد يكون موصوفاً بكونه شراً، إلا أنه وإن حصل عند الوجود أنواع الخوف والألم، فقد حصل أيضاً أنواع السرور واللذة.
فنقول:
أما الجواب عن السؤال الأول: فهو أن نقول: مراد جميع العقلاء من الشر والمفسدة والبلاء: حصول الألم والغم. ولا شك أن الألم معنى وجودي. فيثبت: أن هذا الكلام الذي ذكرتموه في السؤال لفظي محض. وأقول: لما كان هذا الكلام منقولاً عن أكابر الحكماء، وجب حمله على محمل صحيح، فنقول: الشر إما أن يقع في الذات أو في الصفات أو في الأفعال. أما الشر في الذات: فليس إلا العدم، وكذا القول في الصفات. فإن العمى والصم والبكم: شرور. وهي مفهومات عدمية، لأن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر، عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. وكذا القول في البواقي. وأما الشر في الأفعال: فمعناه: الإيلام. ولا شك أنه معنى موجود، فعلى هذا يجب أن يقال: الشر.
The seventh proof: Bringing into existence is opening the door to afflictions. And that is not befitting the wise agent.
Elucidation of the first: that upon the occurrence of existence, fear of loss of the benefits of existence occurs, and fear of the occurrence of pain occurs. And fear is pain and harm. As for the state of non-existence, there was no pain in it at all, because the occurrence of pain is conditioned upon the occurrence of existence and life. So upon the absence of existence, the occurrence of harm and fear is impossible. So it is established that upon the occurrence of existence, harm occurs, and upon non-existence, there is no harm. So this existence is evil relative to non-existence, and non-existence is better than it, and the wise agent does not do evil.
If they say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
First: that evil is a term for non-existence. Do you not see that if someone kills a person, that killing is evil, and it is not evil because the tool was cutting, nor because the killer was able to use that cutting tool with strong force, nor because the neck of the slain was susceptible to cutting, for all that are good things. Rather, it was only evil because life ceased from the locus capable of life, and this cessation is non-existence. So it is established that evil is nothing but non-existence. So how can it be said that existence is evil?
Second: we grant that existence may be described as being evil, however, even though types of fear and pain occur upon existence, types of joy and pleasure also occur.
We say:
As for the answer to the first objection: it is that we say: The meaning of intelligent people concerning evil, corruption, and affliction is the occurrence of pain and grief. And there is no doubt that pain is an existential meaning. So it is established that this speech which you mentioned in the objection is purely verbal. And I say: Since this speech is transmitted from the leading philosophers, it must be interpreted in a correct manner. So we say: Evil either occurs in the essence, or in the attributes, or in the actions. As for evil in the essence, it is nothing but non-existence, and likewise for the attributes. For blindness, deafness, and muteness are evils, and they are non-existential concepts, because blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to be seeing. And likewise for the rest. As for evil in actions, its meaning is inflicting pain. And there is no doubt that it is an existential meaning. So upon this it must be said: evil...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi’s seventh proof that existence is evil and includes two objections with the answer to the first. The argument is a proof (hujja) structured as follows: First, the premise: existence opens the door to afflictions (aftat) and is unbefitting the wise agent. He explains that with existence come fear of losing benefits and fear of pain, and fear itself is pain. In non-existence, no pain exists because pain requires life. Therefore, existence brings harm while non-existence has none, making existence evil and non-existence better; the wise agent avoids evil. Then an objection (al-sual) is raised: evil is actually non-existence, as in killing—the evil is the cessation of life, not the act itself. Also, existence includes pleasures. Al-Razi answers the first objection by clarifying that common usage defines evil as pain, which is existential. He then distinguishes three types of evil: evil in essence and attributes is non-existential (like blindness), but evil in actions is existential (inflicting pain). Thus, the claim that existence is evil refers to the pain it brings, not to existence itself. The second objection is not answered in this passage (it is cut off), but the answer to the first is complete.

الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is always good because it brings pleasures and opportunities.
Correction: Al-Razi focuses on the unavoidable harms (fear and pain) that come with existence, arguing that these make existence evil relative to non-existence, regardless of any pleasures.

Checkpoint

B) Existence is evil relative to non-existence.
C) Non-existence is evil relative to existence.
D) Both are equal in value.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's seventh proof asserts that existence entails unavoidable harms, making it evil relative to non-existence and problematic for the concept of a wise creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience fear and pain daily. This lesson says that existence itself causes these harms. Do you ever resent being alive because of the suffering you've endured? How does that resentment—or its absence—reflect on your faith in a wise Creator?
?
Non-existence is free from harm, yet you exist. Are you grateful for your existence despite its inevitable pains, or do you sometimes wish you hadn't been created? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's plan?
?
This proof was a challenge to the idea of a wise creator. Have you ever felt that some of your trials contradict Allah's wisdom? How do you reconcile that? This lesson demands that you face that question honestly.
