Objection and Answer: Is Evil Merely Non-Existence?
Al-Razi presents two objections to the proof and answers the first, distinguishing types of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that evil is only non-existence, and al-Razi's response that evil in actions is existential pain, distinguished from evil in essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Evil in actions is real, existential pain, not just absence of good.

After presenting the proof, al-Razi anticipates two objections. The first objector claims that evil is actually non-existence, using the example of killing. Al-Razi responds by clarifying that common usage defines evil as pain, which is an existential reality. He then distinguishes three types of evil: in essence and attributes (non-existential) and in actions (existential pain).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage containing both objections and al-Razi's answer to the first. Note the structure: objection (first and second) then answer.
The second objection (pleasures) is mentioned but not answered here; it may be addressed later.
2
Understand the first objection
The objector argues that evil is non-existence, using the example of killing: the evil is the cessation of life, not the act or tools. Consider why this might seem plausible.
3
Analyze al-Razi's answer to the first objection
Al-Razi says intelligent people define evil as the occurrence of pain (which is existential). He then distinguishes evil in essence/attributes (non-existential) from evil in actions (existential). See how this refutes the objector's claim.
The objection is called 'purely verbal' (lafẓī maḥḍ).
4
Reflect on the threefold classification
Identify the three categories: essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), actions (af‛āl). Give examples: blindness (non-existential) vs inflicting pain (existential).
This classification is crucial for understanding al-Razi's broader theory of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: الإيجاد فتح لباب الآفات. وذلك لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم.
بيان الأول: أن عند حصول الوجود، يحصل الخوف من زوال مصالح الوجود، ويحصل الخوف من حصول الألم. والخوف ألم وضرر، وأما في حال العدم، فما كان فيه مؤلم أصلاً، لأن حصول الألم مشروط بحصول الوجود والحياة، فعند عدم الوجود، امتنع حصول الضرر والخوف. فيثبت أن عند حصول الوجود يحصل الضرر، وعند العدم لا ضرر. فكان هذا الوجود شراً من العدم، والعدم خيراً منه، والحكيم لا يفعل الشر.
فإن قالوا: السؤال عليه من وجوه:
الأول: أن الشر عبارة عن العدم. ألا ترى أن من قتل إنساناً، فذلك القتل شر، وليس هو شر، لأن الآلة كانت قطاعة، ولا لأجل أن القاتل كان قادراً على استعمال تلك الآلة القطاعة بالقوة القوية، ولا لأجل أن عنق المقتول كان قابلاً للقطع، فإن كل ذلك خيرات. بل إنما كان شراً، لأنه زالت الحياة عن المحل القابل للحياة، وهذا الزوال عدم. فثبت: أن الشر ليس إلا العدم. فكيف يقال: الوجود شر؟
الثاني: سلمنا أن الوجود قد يكون موصوفاً بكونه شراً، إلا أنه وإن حصل عند الوجود أنواع الخوف والألم، فقد حصل أيضاً أنواع السرور واللذة.
فنقول:
أما الجواب عن السؤال الأول: فهو أن نقول: مراد جميع العقلاء من الشر والمفسدة والبلاء: حصول الألم والغم. ولا شك أن الألم معنى وجودي. فيثبت: أن هذا الكلام الذي ذكرتموه في السؤال لفظي محض. وأقول: لما كان هذا الكلام منقولاً عن أكابر الحكماء، وجب حمله على محمل صحيح، فنقول: الشر إما أن يقع في الذات أو في الصفات أو في الأفعال. أما الشر في الذات: فليس إلا العدم، وكذا القول في الصفات. فإن العمى والصم والبكم: شرور. وهي مفهومات عدمية، لأن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر، عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. وكذا القول في البواقي. وأما الشر في الأفعال: فمعناه: الإيلام. ولا شك أنه معنى موجود، فعلى هذا يجب أن يقال: الشر.
The seventh proof: Bringing into existence is opening the door to afflictions. And that is not befitting the wise agent.
Elucidation of the first: that upon the occurrence of existence, fear of loss of the benefits of existence occurs, and fear of the occurrence of pain occurs. And fear is pain and harm. As for the state of non-existence, there was no pain in it at all, because the occurrence of pain is conditioned upon the occurrence of existence and life. So upon the absence of existence, the occurrence of harm and fear is impossible. So it is established that upon the occurrence of existence, harm occurs, and upon non-existence, there is no harm. So this existence is evil relative to non-existence, and non-existence is better than it, and the wise agent does not do evil.
If they say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
First: that evil is a term for non-existence. Do you not see that if someone kills a person, that killing is evil, and it is not evil because the tool was cutting, nor because the killer was able to use that cutting tool with strong force, nor because the neck of the slain was susceptible to cutting, for all that are good things. Rather, it was only evil because life ceased from the locus capable of life, and this cessation is non-existence. So it is established that evil is nothing but non-existence. So how can it be said that existence is evil?
Second: we grant that existence may be described as being evil, however, even though types of fear and pain occur upon existence, types of joy and pleasure also occur.
We say:
As for the answer to the first objection: it is that we say: The meaning of intelligent people concerning evil, corruption, and affliction is the occurrence of pain and grief. And there is no doubt that pain is an existential meaning. So it is established that this speech which you mentioned in the objection is purely verbal. And I say: Since this speech is transmitted from the leading philosophers, it must be interpreted in a correct manner. So we say: Evil either occurs in the essence, or in the attributes, or in the actions. As for evil in the essence, it is nothing but non-existence, and likewise for the attributes. For blindness, deafness, and muteness are evils, and they are non-existential concepts, because blindness is the absence of sight from that which is supposed to be seeing. And likewise for the rest. As for evil in actions, its meaning is inflicting pain. And there is no doubt that it is an existential meaning. So upon this it must be said: evil...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi’s seventh proof that existence is evil and includes two objections with the answer to the first. The argument is a proof (hujja) structured as follows: First, the premise: existence opens the door to afflictions (aftat) and is unbefitting the wise agent. He explains that with existence come fear of losing benefits and fear of pain, and fear itself is pain. In non-existence, no pain exists because pain requires life. Therefore, existence brings harm while non-existence has none, making existence evil and non-existence better; the wise agent avoids evil. Then an objection (al-sual) is raised: evil is actually non-existence, as in killing—the evil is the cessation of life, not the act itself. Also, existence includes pleasures. Al-Razi answers the first objection by clarifying that common usage defines evil as pain, which is existential. He then distinguishes three types of evil: evil in essence and attributes is non-existential (like blindness), but evil in actions is existential (inflicting pain). Thus, the claim that existence is evil refers to the pain it brings, not to existence itself. The second objection is not answered in this passage (it is cut off), but the answer to the first is complete.

Key Terms
الشر
العدم
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال
الإيلام
الألم
المفسدة
البلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all evil is merely the absence of good (non-existence).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that evil in actions (like inflicting pain) is an existential reality, not just absence of good.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer, what is the meaning of evil in actions?
A) Absence of a good attribute.
B) Inflicting pain (īlām).
C) Loss of life.
D) Fear of harm.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi distinguishes evil in essence/attributes (non-existential) from evil in actions (existential pain), rebutting the claim that evil is only non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer a painful event, do you tend to philosophize it away as 'merely the absence of good,' or do you confront it as a real, existent trial from Allah? How does Al-Razi's insistence that evil in actions is existential change the way you should respond to your own pain? Will you now allow your pain to drive you to sincere du'a and repentance rather than intellectual dismissal?
?
Think of a sin you committed—an action of injustice or harm. Have you ever excused it as 'just a lack of good' in the moment, rather than acknowledging it as an existent, blameworthy act? Al-Razi teaches that evil in actions is real and existential. How does that truth hold you accountable for even the 'small' sins you commit? Are you willing to repent from them with the same seriousness as if they were physical pain?
?
Al-Razi says that 'the meaning of intelligent people concerning evil... is the occurrence of pain and grief.' Are you intellectually honest about the pain you cause others? Is there someone you have hurt whose pain you have dismissed as 'not that bad' or 'just their perception'? How does this lesson demand that you re-evaluate your actions and seek to repair the existential harm you have inflicted?

The Three Reasons Evil Does Not Deny Divine Wisdom
Author distinguishes evil types and gives three reasons for divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments that demonstrate why the existence of pain and pleasure in this world does not contradict God's wisdom.

Core Idea
Pain outweighs pleasure, yet existence itself is not evil; pleasure is merely the removal of pain.

The author now answers a key objection: if God is wise and good, why does He create a world filled with more pain than pleasure? He provides three distinct reasons, grounded in empirical observation and logical reasoning, to show that the presence of pain does not undermine divine wisdom. This lesson walks through those reasons.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish evil types
Read the opening line where the author distinguishes between evil in essence/attributes (non-existent) and evil in actions (existing). Understand that evil in actions is the focus.
This sets the scope of the argument.
2
First reason: pleasures vs pains
Examine the claim that pleasures are much weaker than pains, using examples like eating vs colic. Note the empirical basis.
Experience shows pain outweighs pleasure.
3
Second and third reasons
Study the second reason: non-existence avoids pain, so safety from pain is better. Then the third: pleasure is merely repelling pain (e.g., eating removes hunger).
These reasons show pain does not negate wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الذات والصفات . أمر عدمي . وأما في الأفعال : فلا شبهة أن الشر فيها معنى موجود وأما السؤال الثاني : فالجواب عنه من وجوه : الأول : أن لذات هذا العالم ، أضعف بكثير من ألم هذا العالم . والتجربة تدل عليه . فإن ألذ الأشياء : قضاء شهوة البطن ، وشهوة الفرج ونحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن ألم القولنج ، وسائر الأمراض الكثيرة أصعب بكثير من لذة الأكل والوقاع . والثاني : إن عند البقاء على العدم، لا يحصل ، لا اللذة ولا الألم وعند الوجود يحصل أنواع الآلام ، ولا شك أن السلامة من الألم ، أفضل من الوقوع في الألم . والثالث : أن ما يتوهم لذة في هذا العالم ، فهو في الحقيقة عبارة عن دفع الألم. فإن لذة الأكل عبارة عن دفع ألم الجوع، لذلك فكلما كان الجوع أقوى ، كان الأكل ألذ وقال بعض من يتمسك بأمثال هذه الكلمات إنه تعالى خلق القاتل والمقتول ، والظالم والمظلوم ، وعلم أن هذا يقتل ذلك الآخر ، ثم توعد القاتل بأن قال : متى قتلت ذلك الآخر : عذبتك بالنار . فلو لم يخلقهما ، لكان قد سلم المقتول من عذاب الدنيا ، والقاتل من عذاب الآخرة . فيثبت : أن الخلق فتح لباب الشر والآفات . وذلك لا يليق بالصانع الحكيم . وأما إثبات أن إله العالم مؤذي ، وليس بحكيم ولا رحيم ، ولا ناظر لعباده ، محسن إليهم. فذلك باطل ، بإجماع العقلاء المعتبرين . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : مبدأ وجود العالم : آنية موجبة بالذات ، والماهية لوجود العالم . وذلك يوجب دوام العالم ، بدوام وجوده والله أعلم
In the essence and attributes, it is a non-existent matter. As for actions: there is no doubt that evil in them is an existing meaning. As for the second question: the answer to it is from several aspects: First: that the pleasures of this world are much weaker than the pain of this world. And experience indicates it. For the most pleasurable things: satisfying the appetite of the belly and the appetite of the private part, and we know necessarily that the pain of colic and the rest of the many diseases are much more severe than the pleasure of eating and intercourse. Second: that upon remaining in non-existence, neither pleasure nor pain occurs; and upon existence, types of pains occur; and there is no doubt that safety from pain is better than falling into pain. Third: that whatever is imagined as pleasure in this world is in reality an expression of repelling pain. For the pleasure of eating is an expression of repelling the pain of hunger; therefore, whenever hunger is stronger, the eating is more pleasurable. And some of those who adhere to such words said that He, exalted, created the killer and the killed, the oppressor and the oppressed, and knew that this one would kill that other, then threatened the killer by saying: When you kill that other, I will punish you with fire. So if He had not created them, the killed would have been saved from the punishment of this world, and the killer from the punishment of the Hereafter. So it is established that creation opened the door to evil and afflictions. And that is not befitting the Wise Maker. And as for proving that the God of the world is harmful, and not wise nor merciful, nor caring for His servants, beneficent to them — that is false by consensus of the rational considered ones. So nothing remains except to say: the origin of the existence of the world is a self-necessitating quiddity, and the quiddity for the existence of the world. And that necessitates the perpetuity of the world, with the perpetuity of its existence. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection about the existence of evil in the world. The author first distinguishes between evil in essence/attributes (which is non-existent) and evil in actions (which exists). Then he answers a second question with three aspects: (1) Pleasures are weaker than pains, as known from experience; (2) Non-existence involves no pain, whereas existence involves many pains, so safety from pain is preferable; (3) Pleasures are merely the removal of pain (e.g., eating removes hunger), so there is no pure pleasure. Next, he refutes a further objection: if God creates a killer and a victim, He could have prevented both from suffering by not creating them. The author replies that this implies God is harmful and not merciful, which is false by rational consensus. Therefore, the only tenable conclusion is that the world originates from a necessary cause, implying its eternity. The author here is defending God's wisdom against the problem of evil by showing that creation involves more pain than pleasure, but this is not a defect; rather, it proves God's wisdom as the source of existence.

Key Terms
شر
ألم
لذة
دفع الألم
عدم
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is a positive, independent reality that outweighs pain in this world.
Correction: The author argues that pleasures are weaker than pains, and even pleasures are just the removal of pain, so they do not outweigh the pains experienced.

Checkpoint
According to the third aspect, what is the pleasure of eating?
A) A positive sensation added by God.
B) The removal of the pain of hunger.
C) An illusion with no basis.
D) A reward for good deeds.

Key Takeaway
The three reasons show that the presence of pain does not contradict divine wisdom; rather, they highlight that pleasure is derivative and pain is a test of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you chase after worldly pleasures—like food, comfort, or entertainment—do you admit to yourself that you are actually just trying to escape pain? How does that shift your responsibility for your own desires?
?
If the greatest pleasures of this world are far weaker than its worst pains, why do you still invest so much energy in maximizing pleasure and avoiding pain? What does that reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
You have experienced both pleasure and pain. Now that you know pleasure is merely the removal of pain, how will you train yourself to seek the lasting pleasure of the afterlife rather than the temporary relief of this world? What one concrete change will you make today?

Refuting the Injustice Objection and Affirming a Necessary Cause
Author refutes a specific objection about injustice and concludes with a necessary cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that God's creation of killers and victims is unjust, and see how this leads to the conclusion of a self-necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Creating a world with evil does not make God unjust; rather, it is a proof of His wisdom as the necessary source of existence.

After presenting three reasons why pain does not contradict wisdom, the author addresses a specific objection: if God creates a killer and a victim, He could have prevented both sufferings by not creating them. He refutes this by showing it leads to denying God's mercy and wisdom, and then draws the ultimate conclusion that the world must originate from a necessary cause. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that God creates the killer and victim, knowing the killer will kill, then punishes him. Why not simply not create them?
This objection implies creation opens the door to evil.
2
Author's counter-argument
Notice the author says this objection would prove God is harmful and not wise—which is false by consensus of rational people.
The refutation is based on consensus and reason.
3
The necessary conclusion
The only remaining explanation, according to the author, is that the world originates from a self-necessitating cause, implying its eternity.
This is a key theological point.

Source Text

The Eighth Proof: Creation as Beneficence
Author presents the eighth proof that creation is an act of beneficence from an eternal will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof from creation as beneficence and how it demonstrates the necessity of eternal beneficence from God.

Core Idea
Creation is either beneficence, harm, or futility—only beneficence fits God.

This lesson presents the eighth proof in the series, which argues that because God is supremely merciful and wise, creation must be an act of beneficence. The author examines the alternatives and shows that any omission would imply an imperfection in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Notice the logical structure: trilemma, elimination, then further reasoning.
The passage is concise; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Analyze the trilemma
Identify the three possibilities for creation: beneficence, harm, or futility. Understand why harm and futility are eliminated based on God's attributes.
Harm conflicts with mercy; futility conflicts with wisdom.
3
Examine the omission argument
Consider why God cannot omit beneficence: it would be due to inability, ignorance, or miserliness—all impossible for God. Conclude that beneficence must be eternal.
This reinforces the necessity of creation as an act of divine goodness.
4
Understand the role of eternal motive
Note that the full argument requires showing that the motive for beneficence is eternal, conditions were present, and obstacles absent. This makes the act necessary from eternity.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on divine will and eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : الإيجاد. إما أن يكون من باب الإحسان ، أو من باب الإضرار ، أو لا هذا ولا ذاك . والثاني لا يليق بأرحم الراحمين ، والثالث عبث فلا يليق بالحكيم . فبقي الأول . فنقول : ترك الإحسان ، إما أن يكون للعجز أو للجهل أو للبخل . والكل على الله محال ، فوجب أن يكون الإحسان صادراً من الله أبداً . وهذا الكلام في الحقيقة، إنما يتم ببيان أن الداعي إلى الإحسان : أزلي . والشرائط كانت حاصلة في الأزل والموانع كانت زائلة . فوجب الفعل وهذه المقدمات إنما تتم بالوجوع إلى بعض ما ذكرناه فهذه جملة الوجوه المستنبطة من الحسن و القبح ، والحكمة والعبث والله أعلم
The eighth proof: Origination. Either it is from the category of beneficence, or from the category of harm, or neither this nor that. The second is not befitting the Most Merciful of the merciful, and the third is futility, so it is not befitting the Wise. So the first remains. We say: leaving beneficence is either due to inability, or ignorance, or miserliness. All of these are impossible for God, so it is necessary that beneficence emanate from God eternally. And this speech, in reality, is completed only by the explanation that the motive for beneficence is eternal, and the conditions were present in pre-eternity and the obstacles were absent. So the act was necessary. And these premises are completed only by reference to some of what we have mentioned. These are the totality of the points derived from good and evil, and wisdom and futility. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (الحجة الثامنة) concerning origination (الإيجاد). The author argues that creation must fall into one of three categories: beneficence (إحسان), harm (إضرار), or neither (عبث). Harm is unworthy of the Most Merciful, and futility is unworthy of the Wise, so only beneficence remains. Then he argues that if God omitted beneficence, it would be due to inability, ignorance, or miserliness—all impossible for God. Therefore, beneficence must eternally emanate from God. The author further notes that this argument relies on showing that the motive for beneficence is eternal, the conditions were present from pre-eternity, and the obstacles were absent, making the act necessary. He concludes by stating that this is one of the points derived from the concepts of good/evil and wisdom/futility. This is a classic kalam argument for the necessity of creation based on God's attributes of mercy and wisdom.

Key Terms
إيجاد
إحسان
إضرار
عبث
عجز
جهل
بخل
داعٍ أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could have chosen not to create; creation is not necessary.
Correction: The argument shows that because God is essentially beneficent and wise, creation as an act of beneficence is necessary from eternity. Omitting it would imply imperfection in God.

Checkpoint
In the eighth proof, what are the three possibilities for creation?
A) Beneficence, harm, or wisdom.
B) Beneficence, harm, or futility.
C) Beneficence, harm, or mercy.
D) Wisdom, futility, or ignorance.

Key Takeaway
God's creation is an act of beneficence, necessarily flowing from His eternal mercy and wisdom, making creation essential rather than optional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's creation is an act of pure beneficence, why do you sometimes feel entitled to complain about your circumstances? What does that reveal about your trust in His mercy and wisdom?
?
The proof argues that leaving beneficence would imply defect in God. When you withhold goodness from others, what does your omission say about you? Will you commit today to actively seek opportunities for beneficence, knowing that it is the very character of your Creator?
?
The author says creation is either beneficence, harm, or futility. If you are not actively living for God's pleasure, what category does your existence fall into? Are you willing to realign your purpose to be an act of beneficence?

The Eighth Proof: Divine Generosity Necessitates Eternal Creation
The author presents the eighth proof based on divine generosity and refutes alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine generosity implies the eternal issuing of beneficence and thus the eternity of creation.

Core Idea
Divine generosity must be eternal, otherwise God would be imperfect.

The author now presents a proof based on divine attributes. Since God is perfectly generous and wise, His beneficence cannot be temporary or arbitrary. This lesson unpacks the logic that leads to the necessity of eternal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by rejecting a previous view.
2
Identify the three possibilities for divine action
The passage presents three categories for bringing into existence: beneficence, harm, or neither. Find these and note why harm and futility are rejected.
Harm is unbecoming of God's mercy, futility of His wisdom.
3
Understand why only beneficence is fitting
Having eliminated harm and futility, only beneficence remains. The author then argues that omitting beneficence would imply a deficiency in God (incapacity, ignorance, or miserliness), which is impossible.
This leads to the conclusion that beneficence must be eternal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and its import
The act of beneficence must be eternal because the motive is eternal, conditions were present, and obstacles absent. This necessitates the world's perpetuity.
This proof ties divine attributes directly to the eternity of creation.

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (الحجة الثامنة) for the eternity of the world, based on the concept of divine beneficence (الإحسان). The author begins by rejecting a previous view that God is not regardful of His servants, calling it false by consensus of rational people. Then he concludes that the only remaining position is that the cause of the world's existence is necessary in itself, implying the world's perpetuity. He then explicitly introduces the eighth proof: bringing into existence (الإيجاد) must be either beneficence, harm, or neither. He concludes by summarizing that these are the arguments derived from the concepts of good and bad, wisdom and futility.

The Seventh Article: The Material Cause and Contingency of the World
Introduces the article and defines world as contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the material cause provides an argument for God's existence by establishing the world as contingent.

Core Idea
The world's contingency points to its need for a necessary cause.

This lesson opens the seventh section of Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author introduces a new angle of argument: the material cause. By defining the world as contingent in itself, he sets the stage for a classic kalam proof for the existence of a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice how concisely the author states the topic.
The phrase 'ممكن الوجود لذاته' is a technical term.
2
Understand the material cause
The material cause is one of Aristotle's four causes. Here, it refers to the intrinsic constitution of the world. The world's very makeup (as contingent) serves as evidence.
3
Grasp the significance of contingency
Contingency means the world could have not existed. This implies it requires an external cause to bring it into being, leading to the necessary being.
This is a foundational step in kalam arguments.

Source Text

المقالة السابعة في الوجوه المستنبطة من العلة المادية وهي كون العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته
The seventh article on the aspects derived from the material cause, and it is that the world is possible in its essence.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage opens the seventh section of the work by stating its subject: the aspects or arguments that are derived from the material cause (العلة المادية). It then defines the key premise: the world is possible in its essence (ممكن الوجود لذاته). This is a foundational concept in kalam proofs for a necessary being, as contingency implies need for a cause. The author is here classifying types of arguments.

The Second Proof: The World's Contingency in Eternity Necessitates a Necessary Cause
Proof that the world is possibly existent in eternity and necessarily obtained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the world in eternity leads to the necessity of a necessary cause, and why the alternative is impossible.

Core Idea
If the world were impossible in eternity, it could never become possible.

This lesson presents a second rational proof for the existence of a necessary being. The argument focuses on the world's status in eternity: if it were possible, it must be obtained; if impossible, it could not transition to possibility. By eliminating impossibility, the proof establishes the necessity of a cause that makes the world necessarily obtained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main premise
Read the first sentence: 'The world is possibly existent in eternity'. This is the key claim. Notice that possibility here is not temporal but essential (in eternity).
The term 'eternity' (al-azal) means pre-temporal, infinite past.
2
Follow the conditional argument
The author then considers the falsehood of the premise. If it were not possible, its contradictory would be impossibility in eternity. This would imply a transition from essential impossibility to essential possibility.
Essential impossibility means something cannot be by its own essence.
3
Understand the refutation
The author claims this transition is impossible, citing many proofs. Therefore, the world was not impossible in eternity.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the world was not impossible, it must be possible in eternity. And being possible in eternity, it requires a cause to bring it into existence necessarily.
The conclusion is implied: the world is necessarily obtained through a necessary cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : العالم ممكن الوجود في الأزل، فوجب أن يكون واجب الحصول في الأزل . أما بيان المقام الأول ، فهو أنه لو كذب قولنا : إنه ما كان أنه كان الوجود لذاته في الأزل صدق نقيضه ، وهو ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، فيلزم أن يقال : إنه انتقل من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي . وقد بينا بالدلائل الكثيرة : أن ذلك محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العالم ما كان ممتنع ممتنع
The second proof: The world is possibly existent in eternity, so it must be necessarily obtaining in eternity. As for the clarification of the first position, it is that if our statement 'it was not that existence by itself in eternity' is false, then its contradictory is true, which is that it is impossible of existence in eternity. Then it would follow that it moved from essential impossibility to essential possibility. And we have already shown with many proofs that that is impossible. So by what we have mentioned, it is established that the world was not impossible.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument concerning the world's possibility in eternity. The author states that the world is possibly existent in eternity, so it must be necessarily obtained in eternity. The proof proceeds: if it were not the case that the world had existence by itself in eternity (i.e., it was not necessary), then its contradictory would be that it was impossible in eternity. That would imply a transition from essential impossibility to essential possibility, which the author claims has been refuted by many proofs. Therefore, the world was not impossible in eternity; hence it must be possible, and being possible in eternity, it necessitates a cause that brings it into existence necessarily in eternity.

ممكن الوجود

Misconception: Some may think that the world could be impossible at one time and possible later, as in temporal creation from nothing.
Correction: The argument deals with essential impossibility, which cannot change. Temporal impossibility is different; essential impossibility is inherent and immutable.

B) It would be impossible in eternity

Key Takeaway
The world must be possible in eternity, and its possibility necessitates a cause that makes it necessarily obtained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument rejects the notion that impossibility can become possibility. Where in your life are you hoping for a change in your essential reality—like making what is impossible for you possible without God? Will you surrender that to Him now?

The Second Proof: Eternal Possibility Necessitates Eternal Existence
The author presents a second proof that the world's possibility in eternity necessitates its existence in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's possibility in eternity entails its actual existence in eternity, and how the impossibility of a non-eternal becoming eternal is established.

Core Idea
Possibility in eternity forces actual existence; the past cannot be changed.

In this passage, the author presents a second rational proof for the existence of a necessary being. The argument hinges on the nature of possibility and time: if the world was merely possible in eternity, it must have been actual in eternity, because what is not eternal cannot later become eternal. We will walk through the two main premises of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its overall structure
Notice that the author first states the conclusion, then gives two parts: proving the world was possible in eternity, and proving that such possibility forces actuality.
2
Focus on the first premise: proving the world was not impossible in eternity
Understand the contradiction that would arise if the world were impossible in eternity—it would have to transfer from impossibility to possibility, which is impossible.
The author references previous proofs.
3
Examine the second premise: the impossibility of a non-eternal becoming eternal
The author distinguishes between two types of non-existence and argues that what is not eternal cannot become eternal because that would require changing the past.
This part is cut off, but the logic is clear.
4
Appreciate the contrapositive reasoning
The author concludes that if something is not existent in eternity, it cannot become existent in eternity. This reflects the contrapositive of the earlier point.
The end of the passage is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : العالم ممكن الوجود في الأزل، فوجب أن يكون واجب الحصول في الأزل . أما بيان المقام الأول ، فهو أنه لو كذب قولنا : إنه ما كان أنه كان الوجود لذاته في الأزل صدق نقيضه ، وهو ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، فيلزم أن يقال : إنه انتقل من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي . وقد بينا بالدلائل الكثيرة : أن ذلك محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العالم ما كان ممتنع الوجود في الأزل ، بل كان صحيح الوجود في الأزل . وأما بيان أنه متى كان كذلك ، لزم أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل : فلان كل ما لا يكون موجوداً في الأزل ، استحال عقلاً أن يكون عدمه أزلياً . فإن غير الأزلي يمتنع عقلا أن ينقلب أزلياً . وهذا الكلام يستدعي مزيد تقرير فنقول : الذي لا يكون حاصلاً قسمان : أحدهما : الذي لا يكون حاصلاً مع أنه يمكن أن يصير حاصلا . وذلك مثل الجسم الذي لا يكون متحركاً ، فيصير بعد ذلك متحركاً . والقسم الثاني : الذي لا يكون حاصلاً ، مع أنه يمتنع أن يصير حاصلاً وهذا مثل الشيء الذي يصدق عليه : أنه ما كان أزلياً . فإن ذلك الشيء يمتنع أن يصير أزلياً ، لأن الذي لا يكون أزلياً معناه أنه ما كان موجوداً فيما مضى والأزلي هو الذي كان موجوداً فيما مضى فلو قلنا : إن الشيء الذي ما كان موجوداً في الأزل، ينقلب فيصير موجوداً في الأزل : كان معنى هذا الكلام : أن الذي كان معدوماً في الزمان الماضي ، صار الآن موجوداً في الزمان الماضي وذلك يقتضي إيقاع التصرف في الزمان الماضي. وهو محال . فيثبت بهذا التقرير : أن الذي ما كان موجوداً في الأزل ، يمتنع أن يصير بعد ذلك موجوداً في الأزل . وهذا ينعكس انعكاس النقيض ، وهو أن الذي لا أن يكون) موجوداً في الأزل [ فإنه يجب أن يقال : إنه كان موجوداً في
The second proof: The world was possibly existent in eternity (al-azal), so it must have been necessarily obtained in eternity. As for the explanation of the first premise, it is that if our statement is false that it was not that existence was necessary for it in eternity, then its opposite is true, which is that it was impossible in eternity, so it would follow that it transferred from essential impossibility to essential possibility. We have demonstrated with many proofs that that is impossible. So it is established by what we mentioned that the world was not impossible in eternity, but rather it was valid in existence in eternity. As for the explanation that whenever it is such, it must have been existent in eternity, it is because everything that is not existent in eternity, it is rationally impossible for its non-existence to be eternal. For the non-eternal is rationally impossible to become eternal. This statement requires further elaboration, so we say: That which is not obtained is of two types: one is that which is not obtained but it can become obtained, like a body that is not moving then later becomes moving. The second type is that which is not obtained with the impossibility that it become obtained, like something of which it is true that it was not eternal. For that thing is impossible to become eternal, because what is not eternal means that it was not existent in the past, and the eternal is what was existent in the past. So if we say that a thing that was not existent in eternity turns into being existent in eternity, the meaning of this statement is that what was non-existent in past time has now become existent in past time, and that entails intervening in past time, which is impossible. Thus it is established by this elaboration that what was not existent in eternity is impossible to become later existent in eternity. And this reflects the contrapositive, which is that what is not (?) existent in eternity, then it must be said that it was existent in...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) that the world must have been existent in eternity because it was possible in eternity. The proof has two parts: first, showing that the world was not impossible in eternity, otherwise it would have transferred from impossibility to possibility, which is impossible. Second, showing that if it was not impossible, then it was possible, and whatever is possible but not existent in eternity cannot become eternal because non-eternal cannot become eternal. The author then elaborates by dividing things that are not existent into two types: those that can become existent (like movement) and those that cannot become existent (like being eternal). The latter is impossible because it would require changing the past. Thus the world must have been existent in eternity. The text ends before completing the contrapositive.

Key Terms
الأزل
ممكن الوجود
واجب الحصول
الامتناع الذاتي
الإمكان الذاتي
غير الأزلي
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility does not imply actual existence.
Correction: In the context of eternity, possibility forces actual existence because the past is fixed and a non-eternal cannot become eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the world were impossible in eternity, what would have to happen for it to become possible?
A) It would transfer from impossibility to possibility, which is impossible.
B) It would remain impossible.
C) It would become necessary.
D) It would become eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that the world's possibility in eternity necessitates its actual existence in eternity because a non-eternal cannot become eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is entirely contingent and dependent on a necessary being who exists in eternity, how does this change the way you view your own life and priorities? What would it mean to live in constant awareness that apart from Him you are nothing?
?
The proof shows that the past cannot be changed—what God has decreed is fixed. Do you live as though you trust that God's decree is unalterable, or do you secretly wish you could change past mistakes? How can you surrender that regret to Him today?
?
Eternity (al-azal) is real and not just an abstract concept. Your actions are happening in time, but they have eternal consequences in God's knowledge. Does this thought make you more careful about what you do now? What specific habit will you change starting today?

The World's Eternity from Possibility
Applies contrapositive to prove the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contrapositive argument leads to the conclusion that the world must be eternal if it is possible.

Core Idea
If the world can be eternal, it must be eternal.

This short passage applies a logical argument to the question of the world's eternity. It builds on the earlier proof that the world is not impossible to exist in eternity, and then invokes a key principle: whatever is not impossible to exist in eternity must actually exist in eternity. Let’s examine this compact argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage presents two conditional premises. Find them: (1) the world is not impossible to exist in eternity, (2) everything not impossible to exist in eternity must exist in eternity.
Notice the structure: 'since we proved... and we proved...'
3
Understand the maxim of the verifiers
The author cites a maxim: 'The eternal is everything for which it is correct [to be eternal] must [be eternal].' Reflect on how this encapsulates the argument.
The Arabic uses 'صح' (correct/possible) and 'وجب' (necessary/must).
4
Reflect on the logical implication
Ask yourself: Why does possibility of eternal existence imply actual eternal existence? The argument treats the possibility as a lack of impossibility, which then necessitates existence in eternity.
This is a key move in kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما دللنا على أن العالم لا يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل ودللنا على أن كل ما لا يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل وجب أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل وجب القول بأنه كان موجوداً في الأزل. وهذا معنى قول المحققين: إن الأزلي كل ما صح في حقه وجب.
Since we proved that the world is not impossible to exist in eternity, and we proved that everything that is not impossible to exist in eternity must exist in eternity, it is necessary to say that it existed in eternity. And this is the meaning of the statement of the verifiers: 'The eternal is everything for which it is correct [to be eternal] must [be eternal].'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage applies the previous contrapositive argument to the world. The author states that since it has been proven that the world is not impossible to exist in eternity, and that everything not impossible to exist in eternity must exist in eternity, it follows that the world existed in eternity. He then cites the maxim of the verifiers: 'The eternal is everything for which it is correct [to be eternal] must [be eternal].' This means that if something can be eternal, it necessarily is eternal.

Key Terms
الأزلي
المحققون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility of eternal existence does not imply actual eternal existence.
Correction: In this argument, the possibility (lack of impossibility) is taken to necessitate actual existence in eternity. The verifiers' maxim states that if something is 'correct' (possible) for eternity, then it must be eternal. This is a specific logical move.

Key Takeaway
The world's possibility of eternal existence, when properly understood, necessitates its actual eternal existence.

The Argument for Eternity and the Answer to the Objection on Accidents
Argument and answer to objection about accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that whatever is possible in eternity must be eternal, and how it differs from temporal things that are conditional on prior causes.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility implies existence; temporal possibility is conditional.

Sanusi presents a key argument for the eternity of the world: if something is not impossible to be eternal, then it must be eternal. But an objection arises: this would make all accidental events eternal too. In this passage, you will see how the author defends the argument by distinguishing absolute possibility from conditional possibility. This distinction is crucial for understanding the difference between necessary existence and contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: argument, objection, answer, conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'imkān' (possibility) and 'ḥawādith' (temporal events).
2
Identify the main argument
The author begins with a conditional: if something is not impossible to be eternal, then it must be eternal. This is the principle of the 'muḥaqqiqīn'. Find this statement.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says this principle applies equally to every accident, making all accidents eternal. See how the author frames the objection.
The objection is that if we apply the same logic, temporal things would also be eternal, which is absurd.
4
Analyze the answer
The author responds by saying the possibility of each accident is conditional on a prior accident. Therefore, they are not possible in an absolute sense in eternity. This blocks the objection.
The key word is 'sharṭ' (condition). Each temporal event's possibility depends on its preceding cause.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that this reasoning actually supports his position: each event is preceded by another, implying an infinite chain, which is what the author argues for (no beginning).
This shows how the objection is turned into evidence for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأزل، فلما دللنا على أن العالم لا يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل، ودللنا على أن كل ما لا يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل: وجب أن يكون موجوداً في الأزل وجب القول بأنه كان موجوداً في الأزل. وهذا معنى قول المحققين: (إن الأزلي كل ما صح في حقه: وجب)، فإن قالوا: فهذا التقرير الذي ذكرتم قائم بعينه في كل واحد من الصور والأعراض الحادثة، فيلزم كونها بأسرها أزلية. فنقول: الفرق ظاهر بين البابين. وذلك لأن إمكان كل واحد من هذه الحوادث، مشروط بوجود الحادث الذي كان متقدماً عليه، فلما كانت هذه الإمكانات مشروطة بهذه الشرائط المخصوصة، لا جرم امتنع حصول هذه الإمكانات بدون هذه الشرائط. فيثبت: أن إمكان وجود كل حادث، إنما حصل في ذلك الوقت المقدر المعين. وذلك يقتضي كون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر، لا إلى أول. وذلك يوجب عين ما ذهبنا إليه. فهذا تقرير هذه الحجة.
The eternal (al-azal): After we proved that the world is not impossible to be existent in eternity, and we proved that everything that is not impossible to be existent in eternity must be existent in eternity, then it is necessary to say that it was existent in eternity. This is the meaning of the saying of the investigators (al-muhaqqiqīn): 'The eternal is whatever is correct in its case: it is necessary.' So if they say: This presentation that you have mentioned applies exactly to each individual form (ṣūra) and accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs, so it follows that all of them are eternal. We say: The difference between the two categories is clear. That is because the possibility (imkān) of each one of these occurrences (ḥawādith) is conditional upon the existence of the occurrence that preceded it. Since these possibilities are conditioned by these specific conditions, inevitably it is impossible for these possibilities to occur without these conditions. Thus it is established that the possibility of existence of each occurrence only occurs at that specific estimated time. This requires that each occurrence be preceded by another occurrence, without end. And that necessitates exactly what we have gone to. So this is the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument and refutes an objection. First, the author states that after proving the world is not impossible to be eternal, and proving that whatever is not impossible in eternity must exist in eternity, they conclude the world is eternal. This is the principle of the investigators: 'The eternal is whatever its possibility is correct, it is necessary.' Then an objection is raised: if this principle is applied to every individual form and accident, then all of them would be eternal, which is absurd. The answer is that there is a clear difference: the possibility of each temporal event is conditional upon the prior event that caused it. Therefore, these possibilities cannot occur without their specific conditions. Hence, each event's possibility arises only at its determined time, implying an infinite chain of prior events. This actually supports the author's position (that the world has no beginning in time). The author concludes that this exposition upholds their proof.

Key Terms
أزلي
إمكان
امتناع
حادث
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the principle 'whatever is possible to be eternal must be eternal' applies identically to all things, including temporal accidents.
Correction: The principle only applies when possibility is absolute and unconditional. Temporal accidents have conditional possibility, tied to the existence of prior causes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the difference between the world and accidents that prevents the argument from making accidents eternal?
A) Accidents are not real entities
B) The possibility of each accident is conditional on a prior accident
C) Accidents are not subject to necessity
D) The world is not subject to change

Key Takeaway
Temporal things have conditional possibility that depends on prior causes, while the world's possibility is absolute, supporting its eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is not absolute; it is conditional on countless prior causes—your parents, the air you breathe, the food you eat. Do you live as if your life depends on the Unconditioned One, or do you take these conditions for granted? What would change if you truly believed your next breath is a gift from the Eternal?
?
The author says that what is possible in eternity must exist. You carry deeds that are eternal in consequence. Are you investing your fleeting moments in what will endure, or are you building on sand? Name one temporal attachment you are treating as absolute that you need to let go of today.

Alternative Formulation of the Argument from Impossibility
Author gives a second version of the argument: refuting the opponent's claim of impossibility undermines their entire case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how refuting the opponent's claim of impossibility undermines their entire case for denying the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
One false impossibility claim can bring down an entire argument.

This short passage presents a second version of the argument from impossibility. The author shows that if the opponent's basic claim—that the world cannot be eternal—is disproven, then everything they build on that claim collapses. It is a crisp, logical punch that leaves the opponent with no ground to stand on.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent claims it is impossible for the world to have existed in eternity. Notice the terms muḥāl and mumtaniʿ.
Muḥāl and mumtaniʿ both indicate impossibility.
2
See how the proof refutes the claim
The proof shows this claim is false. Ask yourself: what would a successful proof of falsity look like?
3
Recognize the collapse of the opponent's case
Once the impossibility claim falls, all arguments relying on it are invalid. This is a refutation that cuts at the root.
The author says everything they rely on is invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقررونها من وجه آخر، وهو أنهم قالوا: المخالف يزعم أن كون العالم موجوداً في الأزل محال ممتنع. فلما أقمنا الدليل على فساد ذلك القول، فقد بطل قولهم في ادعاء هذا الامتناع، وقد كل ما يحتجون به، ويعولون عليه في إثبات هذا الامتناع.
And they may present it from another aspect, which is that they said: The opponent claims that the existence of the world in eternity is impossible (muḥāl mumtaniʿ). So when we produced the proof that this statement is false, then their claim of this impossibility is invalidated, and everything they argue with and rely on to prove this impossibility is [invalidated].

Commentary
This is a shorter alternative formulation of the same argument. The author states that the opponent's claim is that the existence of the world in eternity is impossible. Once proof is given that this claim is false, the opponent's entire case collapses because they have nothing left to rely on. This is a direct refutation of the opponent's position by showing that their alleged impossibility is not proven; it does not rely on the earlier argument about possibility implying necessity.

The Eternity of Matter from Self-Evident Truths
Author argues for eternity of body via three premises and an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses self-evident truths to argue that matter (and thus the body) is eternal.

Core Idea
Clear reason shows everything generated comes from prior matter — matter must be eternal.

This passage builds on previously established self-evident truths to argue for the eternity of the body. The author presents three interlocking premises and then begins to defend the first one with vivid examples from everyday observation. The argument is a classic example of how kālām uses intuitive reason to arrive at metaphysical conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Read the first few lines and list the three premises the author presents. Notice how they build on each other.
2
Examine the proof of the first premise
The author uses two examples. Focus on the first example of the old man. Why does he consider anyone who doubts it insane?
3
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider how the author uses self-evident truths to establish an eternal material substrate (body). What does this imply about the world?
The text cuts off during the second example, but the pattern is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة، فنقول: صريح العقل يشهد بأن الشيء لا يحدث إلا عن مادة سابقة. وذلك يوجب قدم المادة. ولا مادة إلا الجسم. ومجموع هذه المقدمات الثلاثة، يوجب قدم الجسم. أما بيان المقدمة الأولى، وهو أن الشيء لا يحدث إلا عن مادة سابقة، فتقريره من وجوه: الأول: إنا إذا شاهدنا إنساناً شيخاً، فإن صريح العقل يحكم بأنه ما حدث الآن مع صفة الشيخوخة دفعة واحدة، بل إنه تولد عن الأبوين، وكان جنيناً، ثم طفلاً، ثم شاباً، ثم صار الآن شيخاً. ولو أن الإنسان تشكك في هذه الحالة، وخطر بباله أنه لعله حدث الآن دفعة واحدة مع صفة الشيخوخة، فإن كل أحد يقضي عليه بالجنون والعته. وأيضاً: إذا شاهدنا قصراً مشيداً، وبناءً رفيعاً، ثم جوز مجوز حدوثه دفعة واحدة، من غير سابقة وجود التراب.
When you understand this premise, we say: Clear reason (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) testifies that a thing does not occur except from a prior matter (mādda). That necessitates the eternity (qidam) of matter. And there is no matter except the body (jism). And the combination of these three premises necessitates the eternity of the body. As for the exposition of the first premise, which is that a thing does not occur except from prior matter, its presentation is from [several] aspects: The first: When we observe an old man, clear reason judges that he did not occur now with the attribute of old age all at once, but rather he was generated from parents, and was a fetus, then a child, then a youth, then now he has become old. If a person were to doubt this state and it occurred to his mind that perhaps he occurred now all at once with the attribute of old age, then everyone would judge him to be insane and foolish. Also: When we observe a built palace and a lofty building, then if someone were to permit its occurrence all at once, without the prior existence of dirt...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage applies the previously established self-evident truths to a specific metaphysical claim. The author presents three premises: (1) nothing occurs except from prior matter, (2) that necessitates the eternity of matter, (3) matter is body, therefore body is eternal. The first premise is proven by clear reason with examples: an old man does not appear instantaneously but through a generative process; a palace does not appear instantaneously but requires prior materials. Anyone who doubts these is considered mentally unsound. Thus, the argument concludes that the body (or matter) is eternal, supporting the eternity of the world. The text cuts off mid-example, but the point is clear.

Razi's Two Premises: Eternal Matter and Body
Razi argues that matter must be eternal and identifies it as the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two premises: that matter must be eternal to avoid infinite regress, and that this eternal matter is the body.

Core Idea
Infinite regress proves an eternal matter, and that matter is the body.

In this passage, Razi continues his argument by establishing two essential premises about matter. He shows that the matter required for originated things cannot itself be originated, and he identifies this eternal matter as the body. These premises are crucial for his proof of a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: two premises, each with a brief proof.
The passage ends abruptly at 'كان', but the main argument is complete.
2
Understand the second premise
Razi argues that matter must be eternal. The proof: if it were originated, it would need another matter, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is a classic kalam argument against infinite regress.
3
Understand the third premise
Razi identifies the eternal matter as the body. He argues that the body is the bearer of form and accidents, and it must subsist by itself rather than having a locus.
The body (jism) here means a self-subsistent substance.
4
Reflect on the self-subsistence of body
Consider why Razi rejects the possibility that the body has a locus. He implies that a locus would itself be in space, leading to circularity or regress.
5
Avoid a common misunderstanding
Razi is not arguing for the eternity of the world, but for the eternity of the material substratum relative to originated things. This is a step in proving a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي قولنا : إنه يجب أن تكون تلك المادة قديمة . والدليل عليه : أنها لو كانت حادثة ، لافتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل . وأما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن تلك المادة ليست إلا الجسم . فالدليل أن نقول : لا شك أن الحامل للصورة والأعراض المحسوسة هو الجسم . فالجسم إما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه ، أو يكون له محل . والأول هو المقصود والثاني باطل لأن محل الجسم ، إن كان مختصاً بالحيز ، كان
As for the second premise: it is our statement: that matter must be eternal. The proof for it: that if it were originated, it would need another matter, and infinite regress would follow. As for the third premise: that matter is nothing but body. The proof is that we say: there is no doubt that the bearer of form and sensible accidents is the body. So the body either subsists by itself, or has a locus. The first is the intended [meaning] and the second is invalid because the locus of the body, if it is specific to space, it would be...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents two consecutive premises in Razi's larger argument. The second premise states that the pre-existing matter (which all originated things require) must itself be eternal. The proof is simple: if this matter were also originated, it would in turn need another matter, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must end with an eternal matter. The third premise identifies this eternal matter as the body (jism). Razi argues that the bearer of form (surah) and sensible accidents is the body. The body either subsists by itself (i.e., is a substance) or has a locus. The first is what is intended—that the body is self-subsistent—while the second is invalid because a locus of a body would itself have to be in space, leading to circularity or further regress. The argument is cut off at the end of the page, but the point is to establish that the eternal substratum is the body itself, which is the fundamental constituent of the material world.

Key Terms
المادة (matter)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (originated)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
الجسم (body)
الصورة (form)
الأعراض (accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is arguing for the eternity of the world.
Correction: He is arguing for the eternity of the material substratum relative to the observed originated things, not necessarily the world as a whole. This is a step in proving the existence of a necessary being by showing that regress must end.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, why must the prior matter be eternal?
A) Because God created it.
B) Because if it were originated, an infinite regress would occur.
C) Because matter is not a body.
D) Because forms cannot exist without matter.

Key Takeaway
The chain of originated things must end in an eternal matter, and that matter is the body, which subsists by itself.

The Necessity of Prior Material Is Not Proven
Refutes the necessity of prior material and answers three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of prior material for originated things cannot be known by necessity, and how the opponent's three arguments are refuted.

Core Idea
The necessity of prior material is not self-evident, and God's free will allows creation from nothing.

In this passage, the author addresses a second objection to the argument for a creator by denying that every originated thing must arise from prior matter. He argues that this premise is neither intuitive nor proven, and then refutes three specific arguments that were raised. This lesson unpacks each of these moves and clarifies the distinction between certainty of non-occurrence and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the text ends abruptly on the third aspect; focus on the logic available.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first argues that the premise of prior material is not necessary (daruri) and that no proof has been given. Then it addresses three aspects (wujuh) raised by the opponent.
3
Analyze the refutation of necessity
The author gives two reasons: disagreement among rational beings (Muslims) and the symmetry of claiming self-evidence (if you claim it, opponents can claim theirs).
This shows that the premise cannot be self-evident.
4
Examine the three refutations
The first refutation distinguishes certainty of non-occurrence from impossibility. The second denies the analogy between builder and material. The third invokes God's free will.
Pay attention to the distinct logic of each.
5
Reflect on the incomplete third aspect
The text cuts off, but the key idea is that God can create bodies from nothing as a free agent.

السؤال الثاني : هب أن حدوث الشيء عن المادة السابقة : معقول . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ وتقريره : إنكم إما أن تقولوا : إن هذه المقدمة ضرورية أو نظرية . لا جائز كونها ضرورية . لأن جميع المسلمين القائلين بحدوث الأجسام : اتفقوا على أنها حادثة عن محض العـدم ، ولو كان افتقار الحادث إلى المادة السابقة : معلوماً بالضرورة ، لامتنع اختلاف العقلاء فيه . وأيضاً : فلو جاز لكم ادعاء البديهة في قولكم ، لجاز الخصومكم ادعاء البديهة في قولهم . فقد ظهر بهذا الكلام : فساد ادعاء الضرورة فيه . وأما الدليل . فأنتم ما ذكرتم دليلاً في أن كل محدث، فلا بد له من مادة . فقد سقط هذا الكلام ولتتكلم الآن على الوجوه التي ذكرتموها . فنقول : . أما الوجه الأول من الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها : فجوابه : إنه لا نزاع في أن من تشكك في أن هذا الشخص الذي نشاهده الآن ، لعله حدث الآن على هذا الوجه . فإنه يقضي عليه بالجنون . إلا أن هذا ، إنما يدل على كوننا قاطعين بأن هذا المعنى لم يقع الآن . فلم قلتم : إنه يدل على كوننا قاطعين بأنه يمتنع وقوعه ؟ وتقريره : إن الوقوع غير ، وجواز الوقوع غير ، فنحن قاطعون بعدم الوقوع فأما ادعاء كوننا قاطعين بامتناع الوقوع على هذا الوجه ، فهذا ممنوع . فما الدليل عليه ؟ وأما الوجه الثاني : فلا نسلم أن حكم العقلاء بإفتقار البناء إلى الباني جمهور مثل حكمهم بإفتقار البناء إلى المادة والمدة ، والدليل عليه : أن المتكلمين قاطعون بافتقار البناء إلى الباني ، وقاطعون بإفتقاره إلى المادة والمدة . وأما الوجه الثالث : فنقول : إنه تعالى فاعل مختار ، فله أن يفعل ما شاء ، كما شاء . فلعله خلق الأجسام ابتداء ، لا عن مادة أصلا ، ثم إنه تعالى
The second question: Assume that the occurrence of a thing from prior material is conceivable. Why do you say that it is so? Its exposition: You either say that this premise is necessary or theoretical. It is not possible that it is necessary. Because all Muslims who claim that bodies are originated agree that they are originated from sheer nothingness. And if the need of the originated for prior material were known by necessity, then disagreement among rational beings would be impossible. And also: if it were permissible for you to claim self-evidence for your statement, then it would be permissible for your opponents to claim self-evidence for their statement. So it has become clear from this speech: the invalidity of claiming necessity for it. And as for proof, you have not mentioned a proof that every originated thing must have a material. So this statement has fallen. And let us now speak about the aspects that you mentioned. So we say: As for the first of the three aspects that you mentioned, its answer: There is no dispute that if someone doubts that this person we now see, perhaps he occurred now in this manner, he would be judged insane. But this only indicates that we are certain that this matter did not occur now. Why do you say that it indicates that we are certain that its occurrence is impossible? Its exposition: Occurrence is different, and possibility of occurrence is different. So we are certain of non-occurrence. As for the claim that we are certain of the impossibility of occurrence in this manner, this is denied. So what is the proof for it? And as for the second aspect: We do not concede that the judgment of rational beings about the building needing a builder is like their judgment about its need for material and time. The proof of it: The theologians are certain that a building needs a builder, and they are certain that it needs material and time. And as for the third aspect: We say: He, exalted, is a free agent, so He can do whatever He wills, as He wills. Perhaps He created bodies initially, not from any material at all, then He, exalted, ...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection and provides detailed responses. First, it argues that the premise that every originated thing needs prior material cannot be necessary (daruri) because if it were intuitively known, all rational beings would agree, but many Muslims believe bodies are created from nothing. Second, it rejects the claim that the opponents have a proof. Then it refutes three specific aspects (wujuh) raised by the opponent. The first aspect's response distinguishes between certainty of non-occurrence and certainty of impossibility: we can be sure something did not happen without conceding it is impossible. The second aspect's response argues that the need of a building for a builder is not analogous to need for material and time; theologians are said to be certain of both, but the opponent's claim that the analogy is exact is denied. The third aspect's response invokes God's free will: He can create bodies directly from nothing without pre-existing matter.

Misconception: If we are certain that something did not happen, then it must be impossible for it to happen.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between certainty of non-occurrence and certainty of impossibility. We can be sure something did not occur without judging that its occurrence is impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The claim that every originated thing needs prior material is neither self-evident nor proven, and God's free will suffices to create from nothing.

Refuting the objection that prime matter has no volume
Answer to the objection that matter has no volume and cannot produce body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against prime matter having no volume is answered by distinguishing between two concepts of matter.

Core Idea
The objection only applies if matter is a simple substance; indivisible particles avoid it.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the possibility of bodies arising from prime matter: if matter has no volume, how can it form a body? The author offers a crisp response by distinguishing between two conceptions of matter. By the end, you will see that the objection only targets one view, not the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection as stated by the opponent.
The objection: prime matter has no volume, so a body cannot arise from it.
2
Distinguish the two views on matter
The author presents two different conceptions of prime matter. Identify them and understand how each relates to the objection.
One view leads to the objection (simple substance), the other does not (indivisible particles).
3
Analyze the author's response
See how the author argues that the objection does not apply to the second view. Think about the analogy of the palace built from small units.
The analogy shows that aggregation of small volume-possessing units creates a large body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثالثاً : و الهيولى ليس لها حجم ، ولا مقدار . وما كان كذلك ، امتنع حدوث الجسم عنه ، فنقول : إن هذا يلزم على من يقول : إن هيولى الأجسام جوهر مجرد ، ليس له حجم ولا تحيز ، أما من يقول : هيولى الأجسام هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، وهي التي يسميها القدماء بالهباءات . فالسؤال زائل ، لأنه تقرر في عقل كل أحد : أن هذا القصر إنما تركب عن أجسام كل واحد منها في نفسه شيء صغير ، إلا أنه لما انضم البعض إلى البعض حصل الجسم الكبير .
And as for his third saying: 'Prime matter (hayūlā) has no volume and no magnitude. What is like that cannot have a body arise from it,' we say: This is necessary only for those who say that the prime matter of bodies is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) having no volume and no spatial occupancy. As for those who say that the prime matter of bodies is the indivisible particles (ajzā' allatī lā tatajazzā), which the ancients call 'hebahāt' (dust-like particles), then the objection disappears. Because it is established in the mind of everyone that this palace is composed of bodies each of which in itself is a small thing, only when some are joined to others does the large body occur.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection: matter has no volume, so how can a body come from it? The author distinguishes two views of matter. If matter is a simple substance without volume (as some philosophers hold), the objection stands. But if matter is made of indivisible particles (as some mutakallimūn hold), then the objection is removed. The author argues that the latter view is intuitive: a palace is built from small units (bricks, stones) that themselves have volume, and their aggregation forms the large body. Thus, the objection only applies to one conception of matter, not the other.
