The Seventh Proof: Totality of Conditions Argument
Author argues that if the complete cause exists eternally, the effect must be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the eternal totality of causal conditions proves the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
A complete eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect.

This lesson unpacks the seventh proof, which uses the concept of the total set of conditions for causation. The author argues that if the complete cause existed from eternity, the effect (the world) must also be eternal. We'll examine three necessary consequences of this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the proof is structured into three clear parts.
2
Identify the three parts of the proof
The passage presents three explanations (bayan) of the main claim. Find each one and note what it argues.
The first deals with the necessity of the totality being eternal, the second with the impossibility of the effect lagging behind, and the third with rejecting the impossibility of eternal causation.
3
Understand the absurdity of the contrary
For each part, ask yourself: what would happen if the opposite were true? The author shows that each alternative leads to an absurdity.
This is a standard kalam method of proof by contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the eternal is caused by the eternal, meaning the world (the effect) is co-eternal with God (the cause) in a specific sense. Consider how this addresses objections.
This proof is one of several used to establish the eternity of the world in the context of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: مجموع ما لأجله كان البارىء، مؤثراً في وجود العالم كان حاصلا في الأزل. ومتى كان ذلك المجموع حاصلا، امتنع تخلف الأثر عنه. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، فقد لزم القطع بكون الأزلي معللاً بالأزلي. أما بيان الأول: فلانه لو لم يكن ذلك المجموع حاصلاً، لكان حصوله حادثاً. فافتقر إلى مؤثر آخر، ويعود الكلام الأول فيه. وهو محال. وأما بيان الثاني: فهو أن عند حصول مجموع ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية. لولم يحصل الأثر، لزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا لمرجح، وهو محال. وأما بيان الثالث: فهو أنه لما كان الأثر حاصلاً مع المؤثر في الأزل، فقد سقط القول: بأن التأثير الأزلي: محال. وقد تقدم تقرير هذا الدليل
The seventh proof: The totality of that for which the Creator is an efficient cause for the existence of the world was present in eternity. And whenever that totality is present, it is impossible for the effect to lag behind. And when the matter is thus, then it necessitates the certainty that the eternal is caused by the eternal. As for the explanation of the first: because if that totality were not present, its coming to be would be originated, thus it would need another efficient cause, and the previous discussion would recur concerning it, which is absurd. As for the explanation of the second: when the totality of what is necessary for efficient causation is present, if the effect does not occur, there would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the contingent over the other without a preponderator, which is absurd. As for the explanation of the third: since the effect is present with the cause in eternity, the statement that eternal causation is impossible falls away. And the exposition of this proof has preceded.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which is structured as an argument from the complete set of causal conditions. It asserts that the total set of conditions for God's causing the world existed eternally. From this, three consequences follow: (1) If the set were not eternally present, its coming about would be temporal, requiring another cause, leading to an infinite regress. (2) If the set is eternally present but the effect does not occur, then the world, being contingent, would have one of its two possible states (existence or non-existence) without a preponderator, which is absurd. (3) Since the effect (the world) is simultaneous with the cause in eternity, the claim that eternal causation is impossible is refuted. The author notes that this proof has been expounded earlier, but he repeats it here to reinforce the point.

Key Terms

تخلف
مؤثر
أثر
ممكن
مرجح
رجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal cause does not necessarily produce an eternal effect; the effect can be temporal.
Correction: The argument shows that if the complete cause (all conditions) exists eternally, the effect cannot lag behind; otherwise, absurdities follow.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the seventh proof?
A) The world is not caused
B) The complete cause being eternal necessitates the effect being eternal
C) The Creator is not eternal
D) The mutakallimun reject causality

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof demonstrates that the eternal totality of conditions for creation entails the eternity of the world, refuting the objection that eternal causation is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the world's existence is necessary or contingent? How does this proof challenge your assumption that time is independent of God's will?
?
When you think of God as eternal, do you also accept that His power necessitates immediate existence? Are you comfortable with an eternal world, or do you feel it diminishes God's sovereignty?
?
What change in your daily awareness of God's causality does this proof demand? Will you now see every moment of existence as directly and eternally caused by Him?

The Proof for the Temporal Origination of Bodies
The author states the argument for the temporal origination of bodies and enumerates the three required premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof for the temporal origination of bodies based on the impossibility of motion and rest in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
An eternal body cannot be moving or at rest, so it cannot exist.

This lesson introduces the central proof for the createdness of bodies, a cornerstone of Islamic theology. The author begins with a concise conditional argument and then outlines the three premises that must be established. As you read, notice how the argument's structure mirrors the logical categories of necessary, impossible, and possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall flow. Notice the conditional 'if...then' structure and the enumeration of premises.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author posits that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest. Why are these the only two options? Think about the principle that any body, when existing, is characterized by one of these states.
3
Understand the contradiction
The proof shows that both motion and rest are impossible for an eternal being. Consider why: motion implies change and temporal sequence, while rest in pre-eternity would imply a static eternity that cannot account for the universe's diversity.
The commentary will elaborate on these impossibilities.
4
Note the three premises
The author breaks the proof into three premises: (1) every existing body is either moving or at rest, (2) an eternal body cannot be moving, (3) an eternal body cannot be at rest. These will be proven in subsequent sections.
The guided breakdown below maps these premises.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Consider why this proof is a 'dilemma' argument. It shows that both alternatives lead to absurdity, forcing the conclusion that bodies are temporal. This is a common pattern in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : لو كان الجسم أزلياً ، لكان في الأزل . إما أن يكون متحركاً ، أو ساكناً . والقسمان باطلان ، فيمتنع كونه أزلياً واعلم : أنا نفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل، إلى إثبات مقدمات ثلاثة : أولها : إن الجسم متى كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون إما متحركاً، وإما ساكناً . وثانيها : بيان أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون في الأزل متحركاً . وثالثها : بيان أنه يمتنع كونه ساكناً . وحينئذ يتم الدليل .
We say: If the body were eternal, it would be in pre-eternity. Either it would be moving or at rest. Both alternatives are false, so its being eternal is impossible. And know: we need, in establishing this proof, to prove three premises: First: Whenever a body exists, it must be either moving or at rest. Second: The demonstration that a body cannot be moving in pre-eternity. Third: The demonstration that it cannot be at rest. Then the proof is complete.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage opens the first chapter of the proof (for the temporal origination of bodies). It begins with a succinct formulation of the controversial argument: If the body were eternal (azaliyyan), it would exist in pre-eternity (al-azal). In that state, it must be either moving (mutaharrikan) or at rest (sakinan). Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, therefore the body cannot be eternal. This conclusion is then deferred, as the author notes that to fully establish the argument we need to demonstrate three premises (muqaddimat). The first premise is that whenever a body exists, it must be either moving or at rest. The second premise proves that a body cannot be moving in pre-eternity. The third premise proves that it cannot be at rest in pre-eternity. Once these are established, the proof is complete. The reader is thus given the overall structure: a conditional argument whose conditions are to be proven through the three premises.

أزلي
أزل
متحرك
ساكن
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument requires that the body actually be moving or at rest in pre-eternity, when in fact it only requires the logical impossibility of both states for an eternal entity.
Correction: The argument does not posit actual motion or rest; it posits that an eternal body would necessarily have to be either moving or at rest, and then shows that both are impossible, thereby proving its impossibility.

Checkpoint

A) That it be either moving or at rest
B) That it be in pre-eternity
C) That it exist
D) That it be both moving and at rest

Key Takeaway
The proof for the temporal origination of bodies rests on showing that both motion and rest are impossible for an eternal body, requiring three premises to be established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that an eternal body cannot be moving or at rest. Do you live as if your own existence is eternal, avoiding the reality that your life had a beginning and will have an end? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed every moment is temporal?
?
The three premises are carefully laid out. Are you as rigorous in examining the foundations of your own beliefs, or do you accept them without proof? What premise in your life's direction needs to be proven to you?
?
If an eternal body cannot be moving or at rest, then what does that say about your own state? Are you moving towards Allah or resting in complacency? The proof shows that neither state can be eternal—so what state will you choose, knowing it will not last forever?

The First Premise: Body Implies Motion or Rest
Ibn al-Khatib proves the first premise by showing a body must be spatially located and either stationary or transitory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that every body necessarily implies either motion or rest, and how this premise is derived from the definition of a body as a spatial entity.

Core Idea
Every body, by occupying space, must be either fixed in place or moving between places.

This lesson unpacks the first premise in a cosmological argument for God's existence. The author, Ibn al-Khatib, argues that any existing body must be either at rest or in motion. This may seem obvious, but it is a crucial step in a larger proof that the universe requires a cause. Understanding this reasoning is foundational for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal English translation. Try to follow the flow of the argument before interpreting it.
Notice the key terms: mutahayyiz, hays, mustaqirr, sakin, mutaharrik.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the passage into its logical components: the initial claim, the dichotomy, the definitions, and the conclusion. This is a deductive argument from definition.
The argument uses the law of excluded middle: a body is either stable or not.
3
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why this premise is necessary. How does establishing that every body is either moving or at rest serve a larger theological proof? Consider that change (motion/rest) requires an external cause.
This connects to the broader argument for God's existence from contingency or change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : فهو أنا نقول : لا شك أن كل ما كان متحيزاً ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إما أن يكون مستقراً في ذلك الحيز ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون متنقلاً من حيز إلى حيز آخر . والأول هو الساكن ، والثاني هو المتحرك . فيثبت : أن الجسم متى كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون ، إما متحركاً ، وإما ساكناً .
As for the demonstration of the first premise: it is that we say: there is no doubt that everything that is spatial (mutahayyiz) must be located in a specific space (hays mu'ayyan). And if this is established, we say: either it is stable (mustaqirran) in that space, or it is not so, but rather moving from one space to another. The first is the resting (sakin), and the second is the moving (mutaharrik). So it is established that whenever a body exists, it must be either moving or at rest.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed proof of the first premise announced earlier. The author's reasoning is straightforward: Every body occupies space (mutahayyiz) and must be situated in a particular space (hays mu'ayyan). Once that is accepted, we consider two possibilities: the body is either fixed in that space (mustaqirr) or it is not fixed—meaning it shifts from one space to another. The first condition is what we call 'at rest' (sakin), the second 'in motion' (mutaharrik). The conclusion follows: any existing body is necessarily either moving or at rest. This reasoning chains from the definition of a body as a spatial entity to its two possible states relative to the space it occupies.

Key Terms
متحيز
حيز
مستقر
متحرك
ساكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the premise already assumes the body's existence in space, but it is derived from the definition of body itself.
Correction: The premise is not an assumption; it is a logical consequence of what it means to be a body (a spatial entity).

Checkpoint
What are the two possible states of a body relative to its space, according to this passage?
A) Existent or non-existent
B) Moving or at rest
C) Temporal or eternal
D) Simple or composite

Key Takeaway
The first premise is established by a simple logical dichotomy: any body must be either at rest or in motion based on its occupation of space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This logical truth about bodies—that they must be either moving or at rest—is so basic that it is often overlooked. But it serves as a starting point to prove the existence of Allah. How aware are you of the logical necessity behind your own physical existence? Does this awareness prompt you to think of the Creator in your daily life, or do you usually take your body for granted?
?
In this lesson, motion and rest are shown to be the only possible states for a body. This means there is no third option. How does this absolute dichotomy challenge any assumptions you might have about being 'neutral' in your spiritual journey? Are you moving towards Allah or resting in negligence?
?
The argument does not rely on empirical observation but on logical necessity. This means that even if you never saw a body, you could deduce its states. How does this shift your understanding of the certainty of Allah's existence? Are you willing to be certain about logical proofs even when you cannot see the truth with your eyes?

Objection from the Hayula Theory
Objector presents the hayula theory to deny the necessity of body being spatial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that body may not be spatial because volumetricity is an accident inhering in a non-spatial substrate (hayula).

Core Idea
Body's spatiality is not inherent; it could be an accident on a non-spatial substrate.

The discussion begins with a challenge to a fundamental premise: that every body must occupy space. The objector introduces an ancient philosophical idea—the hayula theory—claiming that what we perceive as a body is actually a composite of a non-dimensional substrate and an accidental property of volume. This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's precise wording: they do not reject the existence of body but question its necessary connection to spatiality.
The term 'hayula' derives from Greek 'hyle', meaning matter.
2
Understand the Hayula Theory
The objector claims that 'volumetricity' (hajmiyya) and 'spatiality' (tahayyuz) are attributes that inhere in a substrate. This substrate itself is non-volumetric and non-spatial. Identify how this challenges the premise.
3
Follow the Logical Consequence
If the substrate can exist without volumetricity, then a body (the composite) might not necessarily occupy space. The objector concludes that the original claim is not conceded.
This sets up the author's response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون متحيزاً . وذلك لأن من الناس من قال : الحجمية والتحيز صفة قائمة بمحل ، وذلك المحل في نفسه ، ليس بحجم ولا بمتحيز . ويسمون ذلك المحل : بالهيولى. وهذه الحجمية : بالصورة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ وبتقدير أن تكون تلك الذات خالية عن الحجمية ، لم يجب كونها حاصلة في حيز أصلا ؟ وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم . سلمنا أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً كان متحيزاً . لكن قولكم : وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين ، كلام مبني على تصور أصل الحيز ، فما المراد من هذا الحيز ، الذي جعلتموه ظرفاً لهذا الجسم ؟ وتقريره أن نقول : هذا الشيء المسمى بالحيز ، إما أن يقال : إنه عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وإما أن يكون موجوداً . فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، امتنع كون الجسم واجب الحصول فيه . لأن جعل المعدوم ظرفاً للموجود محال . وأما إن كان موجوداً ، فإما أن يكون مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس ، أو غير مشار إليه. والأول باطل . لأن المشار إليه ، بحسب الحس إما أن يكون كذلك بحسب الاستقلال ، أو بحسب التبعية . فإن كان الأول فهو الجسم فالقول بأن الجسم حاصل في الحيز يكون معناه : أن الجسم حاصل في الجسم. فإن كان المراد من هذه الظرفية : الزمان فحينئذ يلزم تداخل الأجسام، وإن كان المراد منها المماسة ، لزم أن يكون كل جسم متصلا بجسم آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال .
If it is said: We do not concede that whenever a body exists, it must be spatial. This is because some people say: volumetricity (hajmiyya) and spatiality (tahayyuz) are attributes inhering in a substrate, and that substrate in itself is neither a volume nor spatial. And they call that substrate: the hyle (hayula). And this volumetricity: the form (sura). So why is it not permissible that the matter be thus? And if that essence were devoid of volumetricity, it would not be necessary that it be located in a space at all? Then their argument collapses. We grant that whenever a body exists it is spatial. But your statement: 'and everything that is such must be located in a specific space' is a statement built on the concept of the space itself. So what is meant by this space which you have made a container for this body? Its explanation: we say: this thing called space is either pure non-existence and sheer negation, or it is existent. If the truth is the first, then it is impossible for the body to be necessarily contained in it, because making non-existence a container for existence is absurd. As for if it is existent, then it is either perceptible by the senses or not perceptible. The first is false, because the perceptible by the senses is either so independently or dependently. If it is the first, then it is the body itself; so the saying that the body is located in space would mean that the body is located in a body. If they intend by this containment: time, then the interpenetration of bodies would follow. And if they intend by it: contact, then it would follow that every body is in contact with another body ad infinitum, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of the author's response concerning the first premise. The objector first challenges the claim that every body is necessarily spatial (mutahayyiz). He cites the philosophical theory (attributed to 'some people') that volumetricity (hajmiyya) and spatiality (tahayyuz) are accidental attributes inhering in a non-dimensional substrate, which is called 'hyle' (hayula). The body, in this view, is a composite of hyle and form, not a self-subsisting spatial entity. Therefore, it could be that the substrate (the underlying entity) is not itself a volume or spatial; only the accident of volumetricity gives the appearance of body. If that substrate were stripped of that accident, it would not occupy space. Hence, the objector concludes that the premise that body necessarily implies spatiality is not universally accepted. The author then concedes the point for argument's sake ('we grant that body is spatial') but now attacks the notion of 'space' (hays). He asks: what is this space that you claim is the container of the body? He divides space into two possibilities: either it is pure non-existence (adam mahd) or it is existent. If it is non-existence, it cannot serve as a container for an existent body—that is logically absurd. If it is existent, then we ask whether it is sensible (mushar ilayhi bil-hiss) or not. If it is sensible, then it is either independent (mustaqill) or dependent (tabi'iyya). If it is independent, then that sensible entity is itself a body; therefore, the claim that a body is located in space reduces to 'a body is located in a body', which leads to problems. If the intended meaning of 'containment' is temporal, then interpenetration of bodies (tadakhul al-ajsam) would be entailed. If it means 'contact' (mumassa), then it would lead to an infinite regress of bodies contacting bodies. Both consequences are impossible. The argument thus shows that the very concept of a spatial container is problematic, thereby defending the first premise against the objection.

Key Terms
حجمية
تحيز
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hayula theory is easily dismissed as absurd and irrelevant.
Correction: The author takes the objection seriously because it forces a deeper analysis of what 'space' means, showing the argument's sophistication.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about the substrate called hayula?
A) It is itself a volume and spatial.
B) It is neither a volume nor spatial.
C) It is the same as the body.
D) It is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The hayula theory challenges the necessity of body's spatiality by treating volume as an accident on a non-spatial substrate.

?
The hayula theory introduces a non-spatial substrate that we can never perceive. Reflect: are there aspects of your faith that you have accepted only because they are 'proven' by rational arguments, while your heart remains untouched? What will you do today to move from intellectual acceptance to heartfelt conviction?
?
The objector challenges that spatiality might not be necessary for body. What 'necessary' beliefs in your life are actually assumptions you have never questioned? How would you respond if someone challenged the foundations of your faith in a way that exposed your lack of deep understanding? Will you seek to strengthen your knowledge, or will you be content with the comfort of familiar ideas?

Refuting the Notion of Space as a Container
Author analyzes the concept of space to refute the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical analysis of 'space' (hays) and why it cannot serve as a container for body, thereby defending the first premise.

Core Idea
Space cannot be a container unless it is non-existent or a body itself, both absurd.

Having temporarily granted that body is spatial, the author now turns to the very concept of 'space' that the opponent assumes. By dividing space into either non-existence or existence, and then into independent or dependent existence, the author shows that no coherent notion of space can allow it to be a container for body without leading to absurdities like interpenetration or infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the Response
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the author first concedes the opponent's point for argument's sake, then shifts the ground to the definition of space.
This is a common dialectical technique.
2
Follow the Division of Space
The author divides space into two possibilities: pure non-existence or existence. Then further divides existence into sensible (independent or dependent). Follow each branch and its consequence.
3
Understand the Reductio Arguments
Each possibility leads to an absurdity: non-existence cannot contain, independent sensible space means body is within a body, time-containment implies interpenetration, contact-containment implies infinite regress.
All these are impossible in the author's framework.
4
Draw the Conclusion
Since all alternatives fail, the original notion of space as a container is incoherent, and the objection fails.
This strengthens the first premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون متحيزاً . وذلك لأن من الناس من قال : الحجمية والتحيز صفة قائمة بمحل ، وذلك المحل في نفسه ، ليس بحجم ولا بمتحيز . ويسمون ذلك المحل : بالهيولى. وهذه الحجمية : بالصورة . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ وبتقدير أن تكون تلك الذات خالية عن الحجمية ، لم يجب كونها حاصلة في حيز أصلا ؟ وحينئذ يبطل كلامهم . سلمنا أن الجسم كلما كان موجوداً كان متحيزاً . لكن قولكم : وكل ما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين ، كلام مبني على تصور أصل الحيز ، فما المراد من هذا الحيز ، الذي جعلتموه ظرفاً لهذا الجسم ؟ وتقريره أن نقول : هذا الشيء المسمى بالحيز ، إما أن يقال : إنه عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وإما أن يكون موجوداً . فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، امتنع كون الجسم واجب الحصول فيه . لأن جعل المعدوم ظرفاً للموجود محال . وأما إن كان موجوداً ، فإما أن يكون مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس ، أو غير مشار إليه. والأول باطل . لأن المشار إليه ، بحسب الحس إما أن يكون كذلك بحسب الاستقلال ، أو بحسب التبعية . فإن كان الأول فهو الجسم فالقول بأن الجسم حاصل في الحيز يكون معناه : أن الجسم حاصل في الجسم. فإن كان المراد من هذه الظرفية : الزمان فحينئذ يلزم تداخل الأجسام، وإن كان المراد منها المماسة ، لزم أن يكون كل جسم متصلا بجسم آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . وهو محال .
If it is said: We do not concede that whenever a body exists, it must be spatial. This is because some people say: volumetricity (hajmiyya) and spatiality (tahayyuz) are attributes inhering in a substrate, and that substrate in itself is neither a volume nor spatial. And they call that substrate: the hyle (hayula). And this volumetricity: the form (sura). So why is it not permissible that the matter be thus? And if that essence were devoid of volumetricity, it would not be necessary that it be located in a space at all? Then their argument collapses. We grant that whenever a body exists it is spatial. But your statement: 'and everything that is such must be located in a specific space' is a statement built on the concept of the space itself. So what is meant by this space which you have made a container for this body? Its explanation: we say: this thing called space is either pure non-existence and sheer negation, or it is existent. If the truth is the first, then it is impossible for the body to be necessarily contained in it, because making non-existence a container for existence is absurd. As for if it is existent, then it is either perceptible by the senses or not perceptible. The first is false, because the perceptible by the senses is either so independently or dependently. If it is the first, then it is the body itself; so the saying that the body is located in space would mean that the body is located in a body. If they intend by this containment: time, then the interpenetration of bodies would follow. And if they intend by it: contact, then it would follow that every body is in contact with another body ad infinitum, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of the author's response concerning the first premise. The objector first challenges the claim that every body is necessarily spatial (mutahayyiz). He cites the philosophical theory (attributed to 'some people') that volumetricity (hajmiyya) and spatiality (tahayyuz) are accidental attributes inhering in a non-dimensional substrate, which is called 'hyle' (hayula). The body, in this view, is a composite of hyle and form, not a self-subsisting spatial entity. Therefore, it could be that the substrate (the underlying entity) is not itself a volume or spatial; only the accident of volumetricity gives the appearance of body. If that substrate were stripped of that accident, it would not occupy space. Hence, the objector concludes that the premise that body necessarily implies spatiality is not universally accepted. The author then concedes the point for argument's sake ('we grant that body is spatial') but now attacks the notion of 'space' (hays). He asks: what is this space that you claim is the container of the body? He divides space into two possibilities: either it is pure non-existence (adam mahd) or it is existent. If it is non-existence, it cannot serve as a container for an existent body—that is logically absurd. If it is existent, then we ask whether it is sensible (mushar ilayhi bil-hiss) or not. If it is sensible, then it is either independent (mustaqill) or dependent (tabi'iyya). If it is independent, then that sensible entity is itself a body; therefore, the claim that a body is located in space reduces to 'a body is located in a body', which leads to problems. If the intended meaning of 'containment' is temporal, then interpenetration of bodies (tadakhul al-ajsam) would be entailed. If it means 'contact' (mumassa), then it would lead to an infinite regress of bodies contacting bodies. Both consequences are impossible. The argument thus shows that the very concept of a spatial container is problematic, thereby defending the first premise against the objection.

Key Terms
حيز
عدم محض
وجود
مشار إليه
تداخل الأجسام
مماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of 'space' is unproblematic and self-evident.
Correction: The author shows that 'space' cannot be coherently defined without leading to absurdities, forcing a re-examination of the premise.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would follow if 'containment in space' means contact?
A) Bodies would interpenetrate.
B) Every body would be in contact with another ad infinitum.
C) Space would be non-existent.
D) Bodies would be nothing but forms.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that the concept of space as a container is incoherent, thus preserving the premise that body must be spatial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God must be 'somewhere' in space? How does the author's argument that space cannot be a container for a body challenge that assumption? Will you trust that God's existence is beyond spatial constraints, or will you hold onto your comfortable mental picture?
?
The author shows that even our most basic concepts like 'space' can be logically dismantled. What is one belief about your own existence or the world that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine it as ruthlessly as the author examines space?
?
If space is not a real container, then the idea of God being 'everywhere' takes on a new meaning. How does this change the way you think about His presence with you right now? Will you live today with the awareness that God is not contained, but permeates every moment of your life?

The Finitude of Past States and Cycles
Concludes a proof that past states and cycles are finite by addition of finite units.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of adding finite quantities proves that past states and cycles must be finite.

Core Idea
Adding finite to finite yields finite — thus the past cannot be infinite.

In this concluding passage, the author finalizes a proof that the series of past events cannot be infinite. The argument is elegantly simple: if each added part is finite, the whole must be finite. Let us examine the reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the repetition of 'mutanāh' (finite).
The opening word 'متناه' is a fragment from the previous page but completes the sentence.
2
Identify the core principle
The passage states: 'that which is added to a finite number, by a finite number, must be finite.' This is the basic rule: finite + finite = finite.
This principle is crucial for the whole argument.
3
Apply the principle to past states
The author then applies this rule: since each past state or cycle is finite, the entire collection of them must also be finite.
The reasoning works recursively: each addition of a finite unit keeps the total finite.
4
Note the concluding remark
The phrase 'this is the complete formulation of this argument [and God knows best]' signals that the proof is finalized and the conclusion is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متناه . والزائد على العدد المتناهي ، بعدد متناهي يجب أن يكون متناهياً . فهذا الزائد أيضاً يجب أن يكون متناهياً. فالأحوال الماضية، والأدوار الماضية : يجب أن تكون متناهية. هذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة [ والله أعلم ]
Finite. And that which is added to a finite number, by a finite number, must be finite. So this added part also must be finite. So past states and past cycles must be finite. This is the complete formulation of this argument [and God knows best].
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage completes a proof (tamām taqrīr hādhihi al-ḥujjah) that past states (al-aḥwāl al-māḍiyah) and past cycles (al-adwār al-māḍiyah) must be finite (mutanāhiyah). The reasoning is: if something is finite, and you add a finite amount to it, the result must be finite. Hence the added portion itself is finite. Applying this to the series of past events, since each event is finite and the addition of finite events remains finite, the entire series must be finite. The phrase 'والله أعلم' indicates the author's reliance on divine knowledge.

Key Terms
متناه
العدد المتناهي
الأحوال الماضية
الأدوار الماضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the series is finite from the start.
Correction: The proof does not assume finitude; it proves it by showing that adding finite parts always results in a finite total, thus an infinite series cannot be formed.

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental principle used in this proof to conclude that past cycles are finite?
A) The past cycles are finite by definition.
B) Adding a finite number to a finite number yields a finite number.
C) The celestial motions are indivisible.
D) The objector concedes that each cycle is finite.

Key Takeaway
The finitude of past states and cycles is proven by the principle that adding finite increments cannot produce an actually infinite series.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Seventh Refutation: The Duration of God's Essence
The seventh refutation argues that the duration of God's essence is longer than any specific period, implying no beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a self-evident comparison of durations leads to a reductio ad absurdum that refutes the opponent's argument for a beginning.

Core Idea
If duration comparisons force a beginning for God, then God is not eternal.

This lesson presents the seventh refutation against those who argue for a first moment of past states. The author uses a simple but powerful comparison: the duration of God's essence from eternity to today is longer than to the Day of the Flood. If the opponent's logic requires a beginning for past states, it would also require a beginning for God Himself—an absurd consequence. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the self-evident claim at the start.
Pay attention to the terms 'امتداد دوام' and 'الأزل'.
2
Understand the self-evident premise
The author states that we know by necessity that God's duration from eternity to today is longer than to the Flood. This is presented as obvious.
This premise is used to set up the reductio.
3
Trace the conditional argument
The author then says: if the opponent's aspects (arguments) force a first for past states, then they would also force a first for God's existence. This is the core logical move.
The opponent's own reasoning is turned against them.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that such a consequence is absurd (فاسد), so the opponent's reasoning is invalid.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السابع : نحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن امتداد دوام ذات الله تعالى من الأزل إلى هذا اليوم ، أطول (‫١‬) من امتداد دوامه من الأزل إلى يوم الطوفان. وحينئذ يعود كل ما ذكرتموه من الوجوه فيه. فإن كانت تلك الوجوه توجب إثبات أول للأحوال الماضية، لزم كونها موجبة إثبات أول لوجود الله تعالى ، وإنه فاسد
The seventh refutation: We know by necessity that the extension of the perpetuity of the essence of God Most High from eternity to this day is longer than the extension of His perpetuity from eternity to the Day of the Flood. Then all the aspects you mentioned apply to it. For if those aspects necessitate affirming a first for past states, they would necessitate affirming a first for the existence of God Most High, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh refutation. It begins with a self-evident truth: the duration of God's essence from eternity to the present day is longer than from eternity to the Day of the Flood (a past event). If the opponent's arguments (الوجوه) about infinite regress or prior states require positing a first moment for past states, then by analogy, they would also require a first moment for God's existence itself. Since God's existence is eternal and without beginning, the opponent's reasoning is invalid. The refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum: from the opponent's principle, an absurdity follows, so the principle must be rejected.

Key Terms
امتداد دوام
ذات الله
الأزل
أول
فاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that duration comparisons imply a temporal dimension in God
Correction: The passage uses them only to refute the opponent's logic, not to attribute temporality to God.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh refutation conclude from comparing God's duration from eternity to today versus to the Flood?
A) Both durations are equal
B) The longer duration proves God's temporal nature
C) If a beginning is required for past states, then God would have a beginning
D) The Flood is the beginning of time

Key Takeaway
This refutation uses a self-evident comparison to show that the opponent's reasoning would absurdly require a beginning for God's existence, thus invalidating their argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Seventh Refutation: Duration of God's Existence
Seventh refutation: self-defeating argument about the beginning of durations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh refutation demonstrates that the opponent's argument for a first beginning in past states leads to an absurd conclusion about God's existence.

Core Idea
If every duration must have a beginning, then God's existence would need a beginning — impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a refutation that turns the opponent's own reasoning against them. By comparing the duration of God's essence from pre-eternity to today versus to the flood, the argument shows that if past states require a first, then God's existence itself must have a first, which is false. This exposes a contradiction in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The author states a necessary truth: the duration of God's essence from pre-eternity to today is longer than from pre-eternity to the day of the flood. This is a fact we know by necessity.
Pre-eternity (al-azal) means without beginning, so any duration starting from al-azal is infinite in the past.
2
Apply the opponent's reasoning
The opponent's 'aspects' (وجوه) from earlier arguments are now applied to this longer duration. If those aspects forced a first beginning for past states, they should also force a first beginning for this duration.
The opponent cannot escape by saying the duration is special; it is the same kind of duration.
3
Draw the refutation
If the opponent's reasoning is correct, it would imply a first beginning for God's existence itself, since God's existence has been ongoing from pre-eternity. But that conclusion is false (fāsid), so the opponent's reasoning must be flawed.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's premise leads to an impossible conclusion, so the premise cannot stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السابع : نحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن امتداد دوام ذات الله تعالى من الأزل إلى هذا اليوم ، أطول من امتداد دوامه من الأزل إلى يوم الطوفان. وحينئذ يعود كل ما ذكرتموه من الوجوه فيه. فإن كانت تلك الوجوه توجب إثبات أول للأحوال الماضية، لزم كونها موجبة إثبات أول لوجود الله تعالى ، وإنه فاسد
The seventh refutation: We know necessarily that the extension of the duration of God's essence from pre-eternity to this day is longer than the extension of His duration from pre-eternity to the day of the flood. Then all the aspects you mentioned return in regard to it. For if those aspects necessitate establishing a first for the past states, then they would necessitate establishing a first for the existence of God, and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (naqḍ) argument. The author argues by noting that we know by necessity that the duration of God's existence from pre-eternity (al-azal) until today is longer than that from pre-eternity until the day of the flood. If the opponent's reasoning (the 'aspects' mentioned earlier) proves that past states must have a first beginning, then the same reasoning would apply to the duration of God's existence itself, implying that God's existence must also have a first beginning. But that is false (fāsid) because God's existence is eternal without beginning. Thus the opponent's argument is self-defeating.

Key Terms
أزل (al-azal)
دوام (dawām)
أول (awwal)
فاسد (fāsid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh refutation is about measuring the length of time between historical events.
Correction: The argument is about the logical implication of the opponent's reasoning: if every duration must have a beginning, then God's duration would need a beginning, which is impossible. It is not about measuring actual time but about logical necessity.

Checkpoint

B) It would prove the eternity of the world.

D) It would demonstrate the impossibility of a flood.

Key Takeaway
The seventh refutation shows that the opponent's reasoning leads to the absurd conclusion that God's existence must have a beginning, thereby refuting their position.

The Tenth Argument: The Temporality of the Body Necessitates Regress
An argument that the attribute of temporal origination must itself be temporal, leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the temporality of a body, if a real attribute, leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the body's temporality is real, then it too must be temporal, causing endless regress.

This argument tackles a crucial point: is the body's 'being temporal' a mere negation of pre-existence, or a positive attribute? The author shows that treating it as a negation leads to absurdity, while treating it as a real attribute forces a regress. We will examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping to analyze. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The argument is condensed; take your time.
2
Identify the two key moves
The argument first rejects that temporality is a negation, then argues that if it's real, it must be temporal itself. Find these moves in the text.
3
Reflect on the regress
Consider why this regress is problematic. Why does the author think it's impossible? Connect to the broader context of arguments for creation.
This is a classic kalam regress argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن عندكم : أن الجسم حادث. فحدوثه ليس نفى وإلا لزم كونه حادثاً في الزمان الثاني وذلك محال . لأن حدوثه في الزمان الثاني يقنضي أن يقال : إن الموجود قد وجد مرة أخرى . وهو محال ، وإذا ثبت هذا ، فذلك الحدوث لا بد وأن يكون حادثاً . فحدوث الحدوث زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل
The tenth: According to you, the body is temporally originated (ḥādith). So its temporality (ḥudūth) is not a negation; otherwise it would imply that it becomes originated in a second time, and that is impossible. Because its originating in the second time would necessitate that one say: 'The existent has existed again', and that is impossible. And when this is established, then that temporality must itself be temporally originated. So the temporality of the temporality is additional to it. And an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument, which deals with the attribute of temporal origination (حدوث) of the body. The author builds on the position that the body is temporally originated. He asserts that this temporality is not a mere negation of pre-existence; if it were, the body would have to become originated again in a second moment (because the negation would be a state that itself becomes temporal), which is absurd. Therefore, temporality is a positive attribute. Now, if this attribute is real, then it too must be temporally originated (i.e., it must have a beginning). This leads to a 'temporality of the temporality', which is another attribute, and so on, resulting in an infinite regress. The argument follows the pattern of previous ones: real attributes require further attributes of the same kind.

Key Terms
الحدوث
الجسم
التسلسل
الزمان
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the body's temporality is a simple fact, not an attribute needing further explanation.
Correction: The argument shows that treating it as a real attribute leads to regress; thus, it must be a mere negation or the regress is unavoidable.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth argument, why does the temporality of the body need to be itself temporally originated?
A) Because temporality is a negation.
B) Because if it were not originated, it would be eternal.
C) Because the body is already originated, so its attribute must also be originated.
D) Because it is an accident that cannot subsist by itself.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of a body cannot be a mere negation; if it is a real attribute, it leads to an infinite regress of temporalities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that explaining temporal things by more temporal things leads to an infinite chain with no foundation. The Tenth Argument: Body's Contingency and Infinite Regress
Presents the tenth argument that a body's contingency leads to infinite regress and concludes that denying this leads to sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the contingency of a body cannot be a mere negation, why this forces the admission of an infinite regress, and how denying that leads to sophistry.

Core Idea
Contingency demands a real cause, not a negation, or else reality collapses into sophistry.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument from contingency that challenges a particular view. The author argues that if the body's contingency were merely a negative attribute, it would lead to absurd consequences. Instead, the contingency itself must be contingent, leading to an infinite chain. Understanding this argument helps clarify the necessity of admitting infinite regresses in certain contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if it were negation, then...'
2
Identify the initial premise
The argument begins with the opponent's view that the body is contingent. Note how the author uses this as a starting point.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Trace why the contingency cannot be a negation. If it were, the body would be contingent in a second time, requiring a new act of existence—an impossibility.
This step shows the absurd consequence of the negation view.
4
See how infinite regress follows
Since the contingency itself must be contingent, it adds an additional contingency, leading to an infinite chain. Recognize that this is the core of the proof.
The author uses this to justify admitting infinite regresses.
5
Understand the final conclusion
The author concludes that denying this infinite regress leads to sophistry, so we must accept that realities necessitate such infinite series.
This connects back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن عندكم : أن الجسم حادث. فحدوثه ليس نفى وإلا لزم كونه حادثاً في الزمان الثاني [ وذلك محال ] . لأن حدوثه في الزمان الثاني يقنضي أن يقال : إن الموجود قد وجد مرة أخرى . وهو محال ، وإذا ثبت هذا ، فذلك الحدوث لا بد وأن يكون حادثاً . فحدوث الحدوث زائداً عليه . ولزم التسلسل . واعلم : أن في هذه الأمثلة كثرة وفيما ذكرناه كفاية في المقصود . لأنه إذا كان لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذه المعاني ، إلا بالتزام هذه التسلسلات ، وبالاعتراف بدخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود ، علمنا : أن إنكار هذا الأصل ، يوجب الحقائق والمعاني . ويوجب الدخول في السفسطة . وذلك محال . فعلمنا : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بدخول ما لا نهاية له في الوجود ، وذلك يبطل ما ذكر نموه .
The tenth: You hold that the body is contingent. Its contingency is not a negation, otherwise it would be contingent in the second time, and that is impossible, because its contingency in the second time necessitates that it be said: the existent has been brought into existence again, and that is impossible. And if this is established, then that contingency must itself be contingent. So the contingency of the contingency is additional to it, and infinite regress follows. And know: in these examples there is much, and what we have mentioned is sufficient for the purpose. For if there is no way to establish these meanings except by committing to these infinite regresses, and by admitting what has no end into existence, we know that denying this principle destroys realities and meanings, and leads to sophistry, and that is impossible. So we know that we must admit the entry of what has no end into existence, and that invalidates what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from contingency (ḥudūth) that forces the opponent to accept either infinite regress (tasalsul) or sophistry (safsaṭa). The argument begins with the opponent's position that the body is contingent. The author argues that this contingency cannot be a mere negation, because if it were, the bodys would be contingent in a second time, requiring a new act of bringing into existence. This leads to an infinite chain of contingencies. The author then generalizes: if one denies the reality of such infinite series, all real distinctions collapse into sophistry—since relations like causal efficacy and attribute-description would be merely mental. The only consistent alternative is to admit that some infinite series exist. This passage is a defense of the necessity of admitting infinite regresses in certain ontological contexts.

Correction: The author argues only that infinite regress is unavoidable in certain contexts like the contingency of contingency, not that every infinite regress is acceptable.

Refuting the Claim that the Infinite Cannot Be Judged
Author refutes by showing self-contradiction in opponent's position on judging infinite past events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that an infinite series of past events cannot be judged, by exposing self-contradictions in the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Opponent's claim that the infinite is beyond judgment is itself a judgment – a self-contradiction.

In this passage, the author responds to a key objection against the proof for temporal origination. The opponent argues that an infinite series of past events cannot be subject to any judgment, thereby undermining the proof. The author shows that this very assertion is self-contradictory, because the opponent makes judgments about that series. Let's examine how he exposes this contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two refutations, the second containing three points.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first contradiction
The opponent's statement that 'it is impossible to judge the totality' is itself a judgment on that totality. So they have already judged it.
This is a performative contradiction.
3
Analyze the second contradiction
When the opponent says 'past states are infinite,' the subject of that proposition must be the aggregate, not each individual. This admits that the aggregate can be a subject of judgment.
This undercuts their denial.
4
Examine the third contradiction
The opponent describes the series as 'continuous from eternity to eternity.' The subject of that description is the aggregate, contradicting their claim that it cannot be judged.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
The author shows that the opponent's own statements imply the very judgments they deny, thus their objection is self-defeating.
This is a classic refutation by inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل : أنهم أقاموا الدليل على بطلان الخاص ، ثم استدلوا به على بطلان العام . وذلك باطل . وأما نحن فقد أقمنا الدلالة على صدق الخاص ، ثم استدللنا به على صدق العام . وذلك حق . والوجه الثاني : إن قولهم : إن جملة الحوادث الماضية ، لا يصح الحكم عليها البتة : كلام متناقض من وجوه : الأول : إنهم حكموا بامتناع الحكم عليها . فهذا حكم على تلك الجملة بهذا الامتناع . فيلزم من هذا الاعتبار . والثاني : إنا إذا قلنا : الأحوال الماضية لا نهاية لها . فهذه قضية . ولكل قضية موضوع ومحمول . فالموضوع في هذه القضية ، إما كل واحد من تلك الأحوال الماضية ، وهو ظاهر الفساد . لأن كل واحد منها لا يصدق عليه أنه أمور متناهية . وإما كل تلك الأمور وجملتها . وحينئذ يكون هذا اعتراف بأنه يمكن الحكم على ذلك الحكم وعلى تلك الجملة ، بأحكام معلومة . وذلك يبطل قولهم : إنه لا يمكن الحكم عليه البتة . والثالث : إنهم يقولون : الأحوال الماضية دائمة مستمرة من الأزل إلى الأبد . فالمحكوم عليه بالاستمرار من الأزل إلى الأبد : ليس هو كل واحد من تلك الحوادث بل مجموعها . وذلك يناقض قولهم : إن ذلك المجموع لا يمكن الحكم عليه
The conclusion: They established the proof for the falsity of the particular, then used it to argue for the falsity of the universal. That is false. As for us, we have established the proof for the truth of the particular, then used it to argue for the truth of the universal. And that is true. The second aspect: Their statement that 'the totality of past events cannot be judged at all' is contradictory in several ways. First: They judged that it is impossible to judge it. So this is a judgment on that totality by this impossibility. Hence it follows from this consideration. Second: If we say 'the past states are infinite,' this is a proposition. Every proposition has a subject and a predicate. The subject in this proposition is either each individual past state—which is obviously false because each individual state is not described as 'infinite matters'—or it is all those matters and their totality. In that case, this is an acknowledgment that it is possible to make a judgment on that totality by known judgments. And that invalidates their statement that it cannot be judged at all. Third: They say, 'The past states are eternal and continuous from eternity to eternity.' The thing judged to be continuous from eternity to eternity is not each individual event but their aggregate. That contradicts their statement that this aggregate cannot be judged.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the proof for temporal origination. The author first summarizes the opponent's flawed method: they prove the particular false and then use that to claim the universal is false, which is invalid. In contrast, the author's method proves the particular true and then deduces the truth of the universal. Then he presents a second refutation: the opponent's claim that the totality of past events cannot be judged is self-contradictory. He gives three proofs. (1) The opponent's very statement that 'it is impossible to judge' is itself a judgment on that totality, so they have already judged it. (2) When we say 'past states are infinite,' this proposition must have a subject. If the subject were each individual state, the statement would be false since each individual is finite. So the subject must be the aggregate, which means they have admitted that the aggregate can be a subject of judgment, contradicting their claim. (3) The opponent themselves describe past states as 'continuous from eternity to eternity'—but the subject of that description is not each individual but the aggregate, again contradicting their denial that the aggregate can be judged.

عام (general)
جملة الحوادث (totality of events)
قضية (proposition)
موضوع (subject)
محمول (predicate)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite aggregate cannot be a subject of any judgment.
Correction: The author shows that the opponent's own statements imply judgment of the aggregate, so the claim is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that the infinite cannot be judged is self-defeating because they themselves judge it.

Fifth Proof: Events Must Have a First as Acts of a Free Agent
The author presents a syllogism proving events have a first, then justifies the first premise via the impossibility of a necessitating cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that all events must have a first beginning because they are acts of a free agent, and the key distinction between a necessitating cause and a free agent.

Core Idea
All events are acts of a free Agent; therefore they have a first.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah that all temporal events must have a first beginning. The author argues that since events are contingent and created by a free agent, they necessarily have a starting point. The key move is showing that the cause of these events cannot be a necessitating cause, which would make the effect eternal, but must be a free agent whose act is preceded by its will. Let us walk through the syllogism and its supporting argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's syllogism
Identify the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. The author states: All events are acts of a free agent; every act of a free agent has a first; therefore events have a first.
This is a straightforward categorical syllogism.
2
Understand the first premise justification
The author explains why all events must be acts of a free agent. He argues that events are contingent, have an effecter, and that effecter cannot be a necessitating cause.
The refutation of the necessitating cause is the core of the argument.
3
Analyze the refutation of necessitating cause
If the effecter were a necessitating cause, the motion (event) would be perpetual as long as the cause exists. But a specific part of motion (a body in a specific locus) cannot endure because that would be rest, not motion. Therefore the effecter cannot be a necessitating cause.
This shows that a necessitating cause would lead to a contradiction.
4
Conclude the free agent's priority
Since the effecter must be a free agent, and a free agent is prior to its act, the act must have a beginning. Thus events have a first.
The passage ends with this conclusion but is cut off; the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في بيان أنه لا بد للحوادث من أول : أن نقول : الحوادث بأسرها فعل فاعل مختار ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فله أول . فالحوادث بأسرها يجب أن يكون لها أول . بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن هذه الحركات أمور حادثة ، وكل حادث فله مؤثر ، وذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون موجباً ، وإما أن يكون مختاراً . لا جائز أن يكون ذلك المؤثر موجباً ، لأن الموجب يلزم من دوامه ، دوام الموجب. فلو كان المؤثر في وجود كل جزء من الأجزاء المفترضة في الحركة الفلكية موجباً بالذات ، لوجب أن يدوم ذلك الجزء من الحركة بدوام ذلك الموجب، لكن دوام جزء من أجزاء الحركة محال . لأن ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة ، عبارة عن حصول الجسم في ذلك الحيز المعين ، بعد أن كان حاصلا في حيز آخر . ولو بقيت هذه الحالة لصار سكوناً لا حركة . فيثبت : أن القول ببقاء الجزء المعين من الحركة يوجب عدم الحركة ، والتقدير تقدير بقاء الحركة. فثبت : أن المؤثر في وجود الحركة ، يمتنع أن يكون موجباً بالذات . فلا بد وأن يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار [ والفاعل بالاختيار ] يجب كونه سابقاً على فعله ، فوجب أن يكون فاعل حركات الأفلاك ، سابقاً في
The fifth proof in explaining that events necessarily have a first: We say: All events are the act of a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), and everything that is such has a first. So all events must have a first. Elucidation of the first premise: These motions are contingent things, and every contingent thing has an effecter (muʾaththir). That effecter is either a necessitating cause (mūjib) or a free agent (mukhtār). It is not possible for that effecter to be a necessitating cause, because from the perpetuity of the necessitating cause, the perpetuity of the necessitated effect follows. So if the effecter in the existence of every assumed part of the motion of the celestial sphere were a necessitating cause by essence, then that part of the motion would have to endure with the endurance of that cause. But the endurance of a part of the motion is impossible, because that specific part of motion is the occurrence of the body in that specific locus after it was in another locus. If this state remained, it would become rest, not motion. Thus it is established that the statement about the endurance of the specific part of motion necessitates the absence of motion, while the assumption is the assumption of the endurance of motion. So it is established that the effecter in the existence of motion cannot be a necessitating cause by essence. Thus it must be a free agent. And the free agent must be prior to its act. So it is necessary that the effecter of the motions of the celestial spheres is prior in...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisah) that all events (ḥawādith) must have a first beginning. The proof is structured as a syllogism: (1) All events are the act of a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). (2) Every act of a free agent has a first. Therefore, all events have a first. The author then explains the first premise. He argues that motions (here, celestial motions) are contingent. Every contingent has an effecter (muʾaththir). This effecter cannot be a necessitating cause (mūjib) because if it were, the effect would be perpetual as long as the cause exists. But a specific part of motion—like a body moving from one location to another—cannot endure, because if it did, it would be rest, not motion. Thus the effecter must be a free agent. The free agent is prior to its act. Therefore, the effecter of celestial motions must be prior (i.e., the act has a beginning). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to conclude that therefore motion has a first beginning as well.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The argument shows that a free agent's act is preceded by the agent's will, so the act itself is not eternal; it begins when the agent chooses to create it.

Key Takeaway

Proof that all temporal events have a beginning
States that whatever is preceded by another's existence has a first moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that anything preceded by another's existence must have a beginning, and why this applies to all temporal events.

Core Idea
Temporal events cannot go back forever—they must have a first moment.

This lesson presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: that every temporal event (hadith) has a beginning. The author states a simple logical principle and applies it to all temporal events. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of the proof.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the key principle
The first part states a general rule: 'And everything that is preceded by the existence of another has a beginning.' This is the major premise of the argument.
3
See how it applies to temporal events
The author then directly applies this principle to all temporal events (al-hawadith), concluding that they all have a first and a beginning. The phrase 'that is what is sought' marks the conclusion.
The argument assumes that temporal events are by definition preceded by something else (e.g., their causes or prior events).
4
Reflect on the theological importance
Think about why this conclusion matters: if temporal events have a beginning, then they cannot be eternal, which points to the need for an eternal creator.
This proof is often used in kalam to argue for the existence of God.

The Second Objection: Does Causing Parts Entail Causing the Whole?
Challenges the necessity of the agent being prior to the whole if prior to each part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical possibilities for a free agent's causation of a composite motion and the objection that priority over parts does not entail priority over the whole.

Core Idea
Causing each part does not automatically cause the whole as a separate entity.

In this objection, the author challenges the earlier proof for the temporal beginning of motion by questioning the relationship between the agent's causation of individual parts and the causation of the whole. The objector presents two scenarios: either the agent causes each part separately, or it causes the whole collectively. By analyzing the logical consequences, he aims to undermine the need for the agent to be prior to the whole. This lesson walks through the tightly reasoned argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the two branches of the objection.
This is a dense dialectical passage; read slowly.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objector asks: does the free agent cause each part individually, or the whole collectively? Mark where each option is introduced.
The text uses 'فإن كان الأول' and 'وإذا ادعيتم كونه تعالى فاعلاً لكل واحد منها والمجموعها أيضاً'.
3
Examine the first horn
If the agent causes each part, priority over each part is admitted. But the objector questions why that implies priority over the whole. He gives a counterexample using non-existence prior to all events.
The objector argues the implication is not necessary.
4
Examine the second horn
If the agent causes both each part and the whole, the objector grants causation of parts but denies causation of the whole as a totality, because the whole necessarily arises from the parts.
This is a key distinction: the whole is a logical resultant, not an independent effect.
5
Reflect on the argument's strategy
Consider how this objection challenges the original proof. What would the original proponent need to defend?
The answer is not provided in this excerpt; it's an open question.

Source Text

السؤال الثاني : هب أن المؤثر فيها فاعل مختار . إلا أنكم تدعون كون ذلك الفاعل المختار فاعلاً لكل واحد من أجزاء تلك الحركة ، أو تدعون كونه فاعلاً لجميعها ؟ فإن كان الأول ، كان اللازم منه كونه متقدماً على كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء. لكن لم قلتم : إنه لما كان متقدماً على كل واحد من تلك الحركات وجب كونه متقدماً على مجموعها ؟ فإن هذه المقدمة إن صحت لم يكن بنا حاجة إلى هذا الدليل الطويل . بل نقول : لما كان العدم سابقاً على كل واحد من الحوادث وجب كونه سابقاً على كلها . فإن لم يجب هذا لم يجب أيضاً ما ذكرتموه . وأما إذا ادعيتم كونه تعالى فاعلاً لكل واحد منها والمجموعها أيضاً فنقول : نسلّم : أنه تعالى فاعل لكل واحد منها لكن لا نسلم : كونه تعالى فاعلاً لمجموع تلك الحركات من حيث إنه مجموع والدليل عليه : أن المجموع واجب الحصول عند حصول كل واحد من الأفراد وما كان واجب الحصول بسبب امتنع افتقاره إلى سبب آخر.
The second question: Grant that the efficient cause in them is a free chooser. But do you claim that that free chooser is an agent for each one of the parts of that movement, or do you claim that it is an agent for all of them? If the first, then it would necessarily follow that it is prior to each one of those parts. But why do you say: Since it is prior to each one of those movements, it must be prior to their totality? For if this premise were correct, we would have no need for this long proof. Rather, we say: Since non-existence is prior to each one of temporal events, it must be prior to all of them. If this does not follow, then neither does what you mentioned. And if you claim that He, exalted, is an agent for each one of them and for their totality as well, we say: We grant that He, exalted, is an agent for each one of them, but we do not grant that He, exalted, is an agent for the totality of those movements insofar as it is a totality. The evidence for this is that the totality is necessarily obtained upon the obtaining of each one of the individuals, and whatever is necessarily obtained by a cause cannot require another cause.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (sul) against the argument for a beginning of temporal events. The objector considers two possibilities: either the free agent is the cause of each individual part of motion, or of the whole collectively. If the first, priority over each part is admitted, but the objector questions whether priority over each part necessarily entails priority over the whole. He argues that the principle 'if non-existence is prior to each event, it must be prior to all' is not self-evident. If the second possibility (agent causes each part and the whole), the objector grants that God is agent of each part but denies He is the agent of the whole as a totality, because the whole necessarily arises when all parts exist, requiring no separate cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causing each part of a whole automatically means causing the whole as an additional effect.
Correction: The whole can be a necessary consequence of the parts, not requiring a separate causal act.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, if the free agent causes each part of a movement, does it necessarily follow that the agent is prior to the whole collection?
A) Yes, because the whole is just the sum of parts
B) No, because priority over each part does not entail priority over the whole
C) Yes, because the whole is caused by the same agent
D) No, because the whole is eternal

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that causing each part does not imply causing the whole as a separate entity, and that the whole is a necessary resultant of the parts.

?

Third Objection: Acts of a Free Chooser Need Not Be Originated
Objection: acts of a free agent need not be originated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the claim that every act of a free agent must be originated, and the dilemma it poses.

Core Idea
Dependence on a free agent does not guarantee temporal origination.

In this passage, the objector challenges a key assumption: that everything dependent on a free agent must be preceded by temporal non-existence. He cites fifteen earlier proofs and presents a dilemma that questions the necessary connection between free choice and temporal origination. This objection forces us to re-examine the nature of causation and temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Note that the objector grants that the movements are from a free agent.
2
Identify the objector's main claim
The objector denies that every act of a free agent must be originated (muḥdath). He points to fifteen previous proofs that dependence on an agent does not require temporal priority.
This is the core of the objection.
3
Understand the dilemma
The objector sets up a disjunction: either the agent has produced all necessary conditions for effectuality or not. If yes, the effect is necessary; if no, additional causes are needed.
This undermines the assumption that free choice implies temporal origination.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this objection challenges the argument that acts of a free agent must be created in time. Consider its relevance to divine acts.
The answer to this objection is not provided here; it will be addressed later.

السؤال الثالث : سلمنا : أن مجموع الحركات فعل فاعل مختار ، فلم قلتم : إن كل ما كان فعلاً لفاعل مختار وجب أن يكون محدثاً ؟ وقد سبق خمسة عشر وجهاً فيما سبق : كل وجه يدل على أن المفتقر إلى الفاعل لا يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم سبقاً زمانياً . والذي نقرره : أن هذا الفاعل المختار إما أن يكون قد حصل عنه كل ما لابد منه في حصول تلك المؤثرية أو لم يحصل ذلك . فإن كان الأول امتنع تخلف الأثر عنه على ما سلف تقريره . وإن كان الثاني افتقر حدوث تلك الاعتبارات إلى مؤثر آخر.
The third question: We grant that the totality of movements is an act of a free chooser. So why do you say: Everything that is an act of a free chooser must be originated? And there have already been fifteen proofs previously: each proof indicates that that which is in need of an agent does not necessarily have to be preceded by non-existence in a temporal sense. And what we establish is that this free chooser either has produced from him everything necessary for obtaining that effectuality, or it has not. If the first, the effect's not occurring is impossible as has been previously established. If the second, then the occurrence of those considerations would need another agent.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The objector concedes that the total series of movements is an act of a free agent, but questions the claim that every act of a free agent must be originated (muḥdath), i.e., preceded by temporal non-existence. He references fifteen previously stated proofs that dependence on an agent does not require temporal priority. He then sets up a dilemma: either the free agent has fully produced everything required for its effect, in which case the effect is necessary, or it has not, in which case additional causes are needed. This challenges the assumption that free choice implies temporal origination.
