The Objection That Time Cannot Have a First Moment
Rejects the argument because it entails a first moment of time, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the argument from temporal events: that the proof would require a first moment of time, which is impossible because every assumed first moment has a prior moment according to clear reason.

Core Idea
Any assumed first moment of time already has a before.

This passage presents the fourth objection to the kalam argument that all temporal events must have a beginning. The objector argues that accepting such a proof would force an impossible first moment of time, because our clear reason affirms that every moment has a prior moment. Thus, the objector rejects the obscure proof in favor of self-evident intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation side by side. Notice the conditional structure: if the proof holds, then time has a beginning. That is deemed impossible.
2
Identify the core claim
The objector states that any assumed first moment of time has a prior moment. This directly contradicts the proof's conclusion.
3
Understand the reasoning
The objector uses clear rational intuition (ṣarīḥ al-‘aql) as a criterion to reject the ambiguous proof. Self-evident intuitions take precedence over obscure arguments.
4
Reflect on the objection's strength
Consider why the objector prioritizes self-evidence over complex argumentation. This is a common epistemological move in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : إن الدليل الذي ذكرتم إن ثبت وصح وجب أن يحصل للمدة ابتداء . وذلك محال . لأن كل وقت يفرض كونه أولاً لكل الأوقات فإن صريح عقلنا يثبت له قبلاً ويحكم بأن عدمه كان متقرراً قبله وإذا كان دليلكم يوجب الانتهاء إلى قبل أول لا قبل قبله وكان ذلك مدفوعاً في بديهة العقل وكان الإقرار بمقتضى البديهيات أولى من الإقرار بمقتضى الدليل الغامض المشتبه الذي ذكرتم . علمنا حينئذ أن كلامكم ساقط والله أعلم.
The fourth question: If the proof you have mentioned is established and sound, it would necessitate that time has a beginning. That is impossible, because every time that is assumed to be the first of all times, our clear reason affirms for it a prior moment and judges that its non-existence was settled before it. And if your proof leads to arriving at a 'before' that is first, with no 'before' before it, and that is rejected by the primary intuition of the intellect, and accepting what is according to primary intuitions is more appropriate than accepting according to the obscure and ambiguous proof you have mentioned, then we know that your statement is invalid. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the argument that all temporal events have a beginning. The objector uses a common kalam argument that time cannot have a first moment because any assumed first moment itself has a prior moment by the very nature of time. Our clear reason (ṣarīḥ ‘aql) dictates that every point of time has a previous moment. Therefore, the proof that necessitates a beginning for time is false, as it contradicts self-evident rational intuition. The objector concludes that accepting such an obscure proof over clear intuition is invalid.

Misconception: The kalam argument for a beginning of events forces time to have a first moment.
Correction: The objector argues that time is continuous, so any assumed first moment already has a prior moment, making a first moment impossible.

Checkpoint

A) It is truly the first
B) It has a prior moment
C) It is eternal
D) It is impossible

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection contends that any proof leading to a first moment of time is invalid because clear reason affirms that every moment has a prior moment.

Razi's Classification of Pre-eternity Views
Razi classifies two groups who affirm pre-eternity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi classifies the positions of those who affirm the pre-eternity of bodies into two main groups based on whether they posit eternal motion or eternal rest.

Core Idea
Razi divides pre-eternity claims into two camps: eternal motion or eternal rest.

In this chapter, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins to systematically refute the arguments for the pre-eternity of bodies. Before launching his critique, he first lays out the landscape of opposing views, identifying two fundamental groups: those who assert that bodies were in motion from pre-eternity, and those who claim they were at rest. This classification is essential for understanding the structure of Razi's argument and the specific targets of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the classification. Notice the two main groups and the subgroups within the first group.
The passage is the opening of a new chapter.
2
Identify the two main groups
Find the two groups: those who say bodies were moving in pre-eternity (al-farīq al-awwal) and those who say they were at rest (al-farīq al-thānī).
3
Examine the subgroups of the first group
Notice that within the first group, there are two subgroups: the Aristotelians (world pre-eternal in matter, form, shape, with perpetual motion) and the Democriteans (indivisible atoms moving randomly, colliding to form heavens).
Razi states that if he disproves pre-eternal motion, both subgroups are refuted.
4
Understand the second group
The second group holds that matter is pre-eternal but form is temporally originated, with matter particles originally at rest. This position is also targeted for refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence; Razi will continue to argue against this position.
5
Reflect on the strategy
Ask yourself why Razi spends time classifying these views before arguing against them. What does this tell you about the importance of precise identification of opposing positions in theological argumentation?
This classification is foundational for the subsequent critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في بيان أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون ساكنا في الأول اعلم أن القائلين بقدم الأجسام فريقان : الفريق الأول : الذين يقولون : إن الأجسام كانت في الأزل متحركة . وهؤلاء فريقان : أحدهما : الذين يقولون : العالم قديم بمادته وصورته وشكله ، وأن الأفلاك والكواكب متحركة ازلاً وأبداً . وهذا قول « أرسطاطاليس ، وأتباعه . والثاني : الذين يقولون : الموجود في الأزل أجزاء غير متجزئة وكانت متحركة حركات مضطربة من الأزل إلى الأبد ، ثم اتفق لها في حركاتها أن تصارمت فتكون منها السموات . وهذا قول : « ديمقراطيس ، وأصحابه . فإذا دللنا على امتناع وجود الحركة الأزلية ، فقد بطل هذان القولان . وأما الفريق الثاني : فهم الذين يقولون : العالم قديم المادة ومحدث الصورة ، وزعموا : أن مادة العالم أجزاء صغيرة ، وكانت ساكنة في الأزل . ثم
Chapter Three: On explaining that a body is impossible to be at rest in the beginning. Know that those who affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of bodies are two groups. The first group: those who say that bodies were in pre-eternity (azal) moving. And these are two groups: one of them: those who say that the world is pre-eternal in its matter, form, and shape, and that the spheres and planets are moving eternally (azalan wa abadan). This is the saying of Aristotle and his followers. And the second: those who say that what exists in pre-eternity are indivisible parts (ajzā' ghayr mutajazzi'a) and they were moving with disordered motions from pre-eternity to eternity, then it happened in their motions that they collided and thus the heavens came into being. This is the saying of Democritus and his companions. So if we demonstrate the impossibility of the existence of pre-eternal motion, then both these sayings become invalid. As for the second group: they are those who say: the world is pre-eternal in matter and temporally originated in form, and they claimed that the matter of the world is small parts, and they were at rest in pre-eternity. Then...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter in Razi's work, where he classifies and critiques the views of those who hold that bodies are pre-eternal (qadīm). He identifies two main groups. The first group affirms that bodies were moving in pre-eternity. Within this group, there are two subgroups: the Aristotelians, who hold that the world (the heavens and planets) is pre-eternal in its matter, form, and shape and is in perpetual motion; and the Democriteans, who assert that pre-eternal existence consists of indivisible atoms moving randomly, eventually colliding to form the heavens. Razi states that if he can prove the impossibility of pre-eternal motion, both of these subgroups' positions are refuted. The second group holds that matter is pre-eternal but form is temporally originated, and that the material particles were originally at rest. The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break, but the argument clearly aims to refute all positions that posit pre-eternal bodies, whether moving or at rest, by establishing the impossibility of a body being at rest eternally or moving eternally.

أزل (azal)
أجزاء غير متجزئة (ajzā' ghayr mutajazzi'a)
حركة أزلية (ḥaraka azaliyya)
سكون (sukūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi's classification is merely a general overview of different theories of the eternity of the world.
Correction: In reality, Razi specifically focuses on the state of bodies (motion or rest) in pre-eternity, which is crucial for his argument that both pre-eternal motion and rest are impossible for bodies.

Key Takeaway
Razi's classification of pre-eternity views into those positing eternal motion and eternal rest lays the groundwork for his systematic refutation.

How will you actively remind yourself today that everything depends on Him?

The Fourth and Fifth Proofs for the Creation of Bodies
Two proofs for the creation of bodies with a concluding sectarian note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments for the creation of bodies (the fourth and fifth proofs) and how they lead to the conclusion that all bodies are created, as well as the limitation that anthropomorphists cannot use these proofs consistently.

Core Idea
Bodies' motion and change in place prove they are created, not eternal.

The author presents two powerful arguments that build on observable features of bodies: motion and change of place. The fourth proof draws on the philosophers' own admission that celestial bodies are in motion, while elemental bodies can move; thus all bodies are contingent. The fifth proof notes that bodies do not return to their original places by nature, indicating they are not necessary. These proofs are then extended to all bodies through the principle of similarity. Finally, the author warns that those who claim God is in a place cannot use these arguments without self-refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the two proofs.
Notice the division into two numbered proofs and the concluding remark.
2
Analyze the Fourth Proof
Understand the division of bodies into celestial and elemental, and how motion implies contingency.
Celestial bodies are necessarily moving according to philosophers; elemental parts can move. Hence all bodies are capable of motion.
3
Analyze the Fifth Proof
Understand the argument from place: bodies exit places and do not revert naturally, indicating contingency.
This shows bodies are not necessarily tied to specific places.
4
Generalization and Principle
Note how the author generalizes using the principle of similarity of bodies (tamāthul al-ajsām).
If some bodies are created, all bodies are created because bodies are similar.
5
Sectarian Limitation
Understand why anthropomorphists (Mujassima, Karrāmiyya) cannot use these proofs.
The same reasoning would imply that God, if in a place, is also created—contradicting their claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : وهي الزامية. وهي أن الأجسام ، إما فلكية ، وإما عنصرية. أما الفلكيات فإنه يجب كونها متحركة ، على مذهب الفلاسفة . وأما العنصريات فإن كل واحد من أجزاء العنصر الواحد ، يمكن كونه متحركاً . وذلك يدل على أن جميع الأجسام قابلة للحركة
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة ، تخرج عن أحيازها ، وإذا خرجت عن أحيازها ، وزالت من إخراجها عن تلك الأحياز، فإنها لا تعتد بطابعها إلى الأحياز المتقدمة ، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الأجزاء لا يجب حصولها في أحياز معينة، وأنه يمكن خروجها عن تلك الأحياز المعينة ، وذلك كاف في إقامة الدليل على حدوث هذه الأجسام
•
ثم إذا ثبت هذا المطلوب في هذه الأجسام ، ثبت أيضاً في سائر الأجسام بالبناء على وجوب تماثل الأجسام
واعلم أن المجسمة والكرامية، وكل من زعم أن الله مختص بحيز معين ، وحاصل في جهة معينة، فإنه لا يمكنه تقرير هذه الدلائل البتة . لأنها بأسرها تصير منقوضة على قوله بذات الله تعالى
The fourth proof: It is a compelling proof. It is that bodies are either celestial or elemental. As for celestial bodies, they must be moving, according to the philosophers. As for elemental bodies, each part of a single element can be moving. This indicates that all bodies are capable of motion.
The fifth proof: We say: there is no doubt that these sensible bodies exit from their places, and when they exit from their places and cease to be in those places, they do not revert by their nature to the previous places. This indicates that these parts are not necessarily confined to specific places, and that it is possible for them to exit from those specific places. This suffices to establish the proof of the creation (ḥudūth) of these bodies.
Then, if this conclusion is established for these bodies, it is also established for all other bodies by building on the necessity of the similarity of bodies.
Know that the Mujassima, the Karrāmiyya, and everyone who claims that God is confined to a specific place and located in a specific direction cannot establish these proofs at all. Because all of them are refuted by his statement concerning God's essence.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the creation of bodies. The fourth proof is described as 'compelling' (ilzāmiyya). It states that bodies are either celestial or elemental. Celestial bodies are necessarily in motion according to the philosophers, while elemental bodies can be in motion. From this, the author concludes that all bodies are capable of motion, which implies contingency. The fifth proof observes that sensible bodies move out of their places and do not return to them by nature, showing that they are not necessarily tied to specific places, and therefore they are created. The author then generalizes this to all bodies based on the principle of similarity (tamāthul). Finally, he notes that anthropomorphists (al-Mujassima and al-Karrāmiyya) who claim God is in a place cannot use these proofs, as the same reasoning would imply that God is also created—a refutation of their position.

Key Terms

الحركة
الفلاسفة
تماثل الأجسام
المجسمة
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The same reasoning that proves bodies are created can also be applied to God if He is in a place.
Correction: The author argues that this would be a refutation of anthropomorphism; the proofs only apply to bodies, not to God's essence, and those who claim God is in a place cannot use them without self-refutation.

Checkpoint
What principle allows the author to generalize the proof of creation from sensible bodies to all bodies?
A) The principle of causality
B) The principle of the similarity of bodies (tamāthul al-ajsām)
C) The principle of motion
D) The principle of place

Key Takeaway
The motion and place-dependence of bodies demonstrate their creation, and the principle of similarity extends this to all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your own body's constant motion and change of place prove it is created and dependent, how does that reshape your daily awareness that every breath you take is a gift from the Creator? Will you pause today to thank Him for simply being able to move?
?
The author warns that anyone who claims God is confined to a place cannot even use these proofs. Do you ever unconsciously imagine God as being 'up there' in a specific location? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your understanding of Allah’s transcendence, and what will you do today to correct that mental image?
?
The principle of similarity tells us that if some bodies are created, all are created. Are you living as though your body is merely a temporary vessel, or do you act as if it will last forever? What specific habit will you change today to acknowledge that your body is created and accountable to its Creator?

First Ash'ari Proof: Whatever Can Cease Cannot Be Eternal
Author presents the first Ash'ari proof that whatever can cease cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that anything liable to cease cannot be eternal, and why the three possible ways of ceasing are impossible.

Core Idea
The pre-eternal cannot cease because its ceasing would require an impossible cause.

The Ash'aris built their case for divine eternity on a simple but powerful logical argument: if something can cease, it cannot be eternal. In this passage, the author presents their first proof, which examines what would have to happen for the pre-eternal to cease. The argument reduces the possibilities to three—all of which are rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
The author references an earlier discussion; note that he is not repeating it here.
2
Identify the three ways ceasing could occur
The text lists three possible ways for the pre-eternal to cease: annihilation by an external cause, conflict with an opposite, or failure of a condition. Identify each one in the text.
3
Understand why each way is impossible
Although the author does not detail the impossibilities here, reflect on why each of the three cannot apply to the pre-eternal. An eternal being cannot be annihilated, cannot have an opposite, and its conditions cannot cease.
The author says these divisions are 'bāṭil' (invalid).
4
Note the author's reference to earlier work
The author mentions he has elaborated this proof elsewhere. This shows that theological arguments are often developed across multiple works.
This is a common feature in classical texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان جائز الزوال، فإنه يمتنع كونه أزلياً . لوجوه من الدلائل : الحجة الأولى : وهي التي قول عليها الأشعرية . أن قالوا : القديم لو عدم ، لكان عدمه إما أن يكون بإعدام معدم ، أو بطريان ضد ، أو بانتفاء شرط . والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فالقول بعدم القديم باطل . واعلم أنا بالغنا في تقرير هذه الحجة، في تقرير قول من يقول : الأجسام يمتنع أن تصير معدومة ، بعد أن كانت موجودة . ولما ذكرنا هذه الحجة هناك مع الزوائد الكثيرة ، والتقريرات اللطيفة ، فلا فائدة في الإعادة
And indeed we said: that everything that is liable to cease—its being eternal is impossible. And that is due to aspects of proofs: The first proof: which is the one upon which the Ash'aris relied. They said: if the pre-eternal were to cease, its ceasing would either be by annihilation of an annihilator, or by the advent of an opposite, or by the non-existence of a condition. And the three divisions are invalid, so the statement of the ceasing of the pre-eternal is invalid. And know that we have elaborated in establishing this proof in establishing the statement of those who say: it is impossible for bodies to become non-existent after having existed. And since we have mentioned this proof there with many additions and subtle expositions, there is no benefit in repeating it.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that anything subject to change (liable to cease) cannot be eternal. The author states the rule and then gives the argument relied upon by the Ash'ari school: If the pre-eternal were to cease, that ceasing would have to occur through one of three ways—either by an external annihilator, or by the arrival of an opposite, or by the failure of a condition. All three are impossible: an annihilator would require a cause, opposites cannot coexist, and conditions for an eternal being cannot fail. Therefore, the pre-eternal cannot cease. The author notes that they have already fully elaborated this proof elsewhere in their works on the impossibility of bodies ceasing, so they do not repeat it here in detail.

Key Terms
جائز الزوال
أزلي
قديم
عدم
إعدام
ضد
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this proof requires a detailed analysis of what non-existence is, or that the three ways are not exhaustive.
Correction: The proof is simpler: it shows that any possible mode of ceasing is impossible for the eternal being, so ceasing itself is impossible. The three ways are considered exhaustive for change.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari proof mentioned, what are the three ways in which the ceasing of the pre-eternal could occur?
A) By annihilation, by separation, or by transformation.
B) By annihilation of an annihilator, by advent of an opposite, or by non-existence of a condition.
C) By exhaustion, by change, or by loss of essence.
D) By divine action, by natural decay, or by withdrawal of sustenance.

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternal cannot cease because all possible modes of ceasing—annihilation by a cause, conflict with an opposite, or failure of a condition—are rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah is eternal and cannot cease. But examine your heart: do you truly rely on Him as the only permanent reality, or do you place your ultimate trust in temporary things—health, wealth, relationships—that are liable to cease? What would change in your daily actions if you really lived as if only Allah remains?
?
The proof shows that the pre-eternal cannot cease because no cause can bring about its demise. Yet you often fear the loss of things that are not eternal. Ask yourself: why do you fear the loss of what is bound to fade, while you feel little awe for the One who never changes? How can you shift your fear and hope toward Him alone?
?
If the eternal Allah cannot cease, then your relationship with Him is the only investment that will never be destroyed. In what specific area of your life are you currently investing more in what fades than in what lasts? Name one concrete action you will take today to align your priorities with the eternal.

4 min
The eternal: necessary in itself or possible in itself
Razi begins the second proof with a division and rejects free agency as a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof begins by dividing the eternal into necessary and possible, and why a free agent cannot be the cause of an eternal being.

Core Idea
An eternal being cannot be caused by a free agent because intention applies only to originated things.

This lesson opens Razi's second proof for the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist. He starts by categorizing the eternal: either it is necessary in itself or possible in itself. If necessary, it cannot cease. If possible, it needs a cause. We then examine the first type of cause: the free agent. This section shows why a free agent cannot produce an eternal being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two-fold division
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Razi divides the eternal into two categories: wājib li-dhātih and mumkin li-dhātih. This division determines the entire argument.
This is a classic kalam method: exhaustive division.
2
Understand the necessary-in-itself branch
Razi says that if the eternal is necessary in itself, non-existence is impossible by definition. The essence of a necessary being is not receptive to non-existence. Grasp this foundational point.
3
Analyze why a free agent cannot cause the eternal
If the eternal is possible in itself, it needs a cause. The first type of cause considered is the free agent (fā'il mukhtār). Razi argues that a free agent acts by intention, and intention only applies when the thing does not yet exist or is emerging. Since the eternal always exists, intention cannot be directed to it. Thus, the eternal cannot be caused by a free agent.
This refutes the idea that God could be a free agent who creates the eternal—because the eternal is not created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهي التي عولنا عليها في الكتب الكلامية : أن يقال : القديم إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته، أو ممكناً لذاته فإن كان واجباً لذاته ، امتنع عليه العدم. لأن المراد من الواجب لذاته ما تكون حقيقته غير قابلة للعدم . وما كان كذلك ، امتنع العدم عليه . وأما إن كان ممكناً لذاته ، فنقول : كل ما كان ممكناً لذاته ، فله مؤثر . وذلك المؤثر ، إما أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً ، وإما أن يكون موجباً بالذات . والأول باطل . لأن الفاعل المختار هو الذي يفعل بواسطة القصد . والقصد إلى تكوين الشيء حال بقائه محال . بل القصد إلى التكوين : إنما يمكن إما حال عدمه ، وإما حال حدوثه . وعلى التقديرين ، فكل ما يقع بالفاعل المختار ، فهو حادث . والقديم ليس بحادث . فامتنع إسناد القديم إلى الفاعل المختار . والقسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : ذلك القديم معلل بعلة قديمة. فنقول : تلك العلة القديمة ، إن كانت ممكنة عاد التقسيم فيه ، وإن كانت واجبة لذاتها ، فإما أن يكون تأثيرها في وجود ذلك القديم غير موقوف على شرط ، أو كان موقوفاً على شرط . فإن كان الأول ، لزم من امتناع العدم على تلك العلة القديمة ، امتناع العدم على ذلك المعلول القديم، وذلك يفيد صحة قولنا : إن الأزلي لا يزول. وأما الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إن تأثير تلك العلة القديمة ، في ذلك المعلول القديم، يتوقف على شرط. فنقول : ذلك الشرط، إن كان ممكنا عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، وإن كان واجباً ، فحينئذ تكون العلة المؤثرة في وجود ذلك القديم ، واجبة لذاتها ، ويكون شرط ذلك التأثير أيضاً : واجباً لذاته . وعلى هذا التقدير فإن ذات العلة : واجبة لذاتها . وشرط تأثيرها في ذلك القديم : واجب لذاته . ويلزم من وجوب وجود هذين الأمرين : وجوب دوام ذلك المعلول ، وامتناع عدمه. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل ما كان قديماً ، فإنه يمتنع عدمه. فلما دللنا على أن كل سكون فهو جائز الزوال ثبت أن السكون يمتنع أن يكون أزلياً والله أعلم
The second proof, which we relied upon in the theological books, is that it is said: The eternal (al-qadīm) is either necessary in itself (wājib li-dhātih) or possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātih). If it is necessary in itself, then non-existence is impossible for it, because what is meant by 'necessary in itself' is that whose essence is not receptive to non-existence, and whatever is like that, non-existence is impossible for it. As for if it is possible in itself, we say: Everything that is possible in itself has an efficient cause (mu'aththir). That efficient cause is either a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) or an agent by essence (mūjib bi-dhāt). The first is invalid, because the free agent is one who acts by means of intention (qaṣd), and intending to bring something into existence while it is enduring is impossible; rather, intending its coming into existence is only possible either at the time of its non-existence or at the time of its origination. On both suppositions, everything that occurs by a free agent is originated (ḥādith), and the eternal is not originated, so attributing the eternal to a free agent is impossible. The second division is that it is said: That eternal is caused by an eternal cause. We say: That eternal cause, if it is possible, the same division applies to it, and if it is necessary in itself, then either its effect in the existence of that eternal is not conditional upon a condition, or it is conditional upon a condition. If the first, then from the impossibility of non-existence for that eternal cause, the impossibility of non-existence for that eternal effect follows, and that yields the correctness of our statement that the eternal (al-azalī) does not cease. As for the second, which is that it is said: The effect of that eternal cause upon that eternal effect is conditional upon a condition. We say: That condition, if it is possible, the first division applies to it, and if it is necessary, then in that case the efficient cause of the existence of that eternal is necessary in itself, and the condition of that effect is also necessary in itself. On this supposition, the essence of the cause is necessary in itself, and the condition of its effect upon that eternal is necessary in itself. From the necessity of the existence of these two things follows the necessity of perpetuity of that effect and the impossibility of its non-existence. So it is established by what we have mentioned that everything that is eternal, its non-existence is impossible. Since we have proved that every rest (sukūn) is subject to passing away, it is established that rest cannot be eternal. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist. It is a proof that proceeds by logical division. The author first divides the eternal into two categories: necessary in itself (wājib li-dhātih) and possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātih). If it is necessary in itself, then non-existence is impossible by definition because a necessary being's essence does not admit non-existence. If it is possible in itself, then it requires an efficient cause (mu'aththir). That cause is either a free agent (fā'il mukhtār) or an agent by essence (mūjib bi-dhāt). The free agent acts by intention, and intention to bring something into existence can only occur when the thing is non-existent or when it is being originated; but the eternal is never non-existent nor originated, so a free agent cannot be the cause of an eternal. Therefore, the cause must be an agent by essence, i.e., a necessary cause. That cause itself is either possible or necessary. If possible, the same division applies, leading to an infinite regress. So it must be necessary in itself. Then the effect of this necessary cause is either unconditional or conditional upon a condition. If unconditional, then the impossibility of non-existence of the cause entails the impossibility of non-existence of the effect. If conditional, the condition itself must also be necessary, and both the cause and condition being necessary entail the perpetual existence of the effect. Thus, in all cases, the eternal cannot cease to exist. The author then applies this to the specific case of 'rest' (sukūn) to show that rest, being subject to passing away, cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
مؤثر
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the eternal is possible in itself, it could be brought into existence by a free agent.
Correction: The argument shows that a free agent acts by intention, which is only relevant when the thing is not yet existing or is originating. The eternal always exists, so a free agent cannot cause it.

Checkpoint
Why can a free agent not be the cause of an eternal being?
A) Because the eternal is necessary in itself.
B) Because the free agent acts by intention, which only applies to originated things.
C) Because the eternal is possible in itself.
D) Because the free agent is originated.

Key Takeaway
The first part of the proof establishes that if the eternal is necessary, it cannot cease; if possible, its cause cannot be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist, but you are not necessary in yourself. Do you truly live with the awareness that at every moment you depend on the Necessary Being? Where in your daily life do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Razi argues that an eternal being cannot be the result of free choice—it must be necessary. If God is not arbitrary, what does that mean for your trust in His decrees? Do you sometimes wish God were more 'free' to change His mind according to your desires?
?
This proof strips away any excuse that the universe could be self-caused or caused by accident. Are you willing to accept the full implication that you are completely contingent and that without the Necessary Being you are nothing? How would your life look if you truly believed that?

The eternal cause and its effect: conditional or unconditional
Razi completes the proof by examining the agent by essence and the conditional sub-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal cause must be necessary in itself and how the conditional or unconditional nature of its effect leads to the perpetuity of the eternal effect.

Core Idea
An eternal effect necessarily follows from an eternal necessary cause, whether conditionally or unconditionally, guaranteeing its perpetuation.

In this lesson, we continue Razi's second proof by examining the second type of cause: the agent by essence (mūjib bi-dhāt). Razi argues that the eternal cause must itself be necessary, and then its effect is either unconditional or conditional. In both scenarios, the impossibility of non-existence of the cause transfers to the effect, establishing that the eternal cannot cease. The lesson concludes by applying this to the case of 'rest' (sukūn) to show that it cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the agent by essence and the division of the cause
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Razi now considers the second possibility: the efficient cause is an agent by essence (mūjib bi-dhāt). This cause itself must be either possible or necessary.
If it is possible, the same division applies, leading to an infinite regress, so it must be necessary.
2
Understand the conditional vs unconditional sub-branch
Once the cause is necessary, its effect is either not conditional upon a condition or conditional. Razi argues both lead to the perpetuity of the effect.
If unconditional, impossibility of non-existence of cause entails that of effect. If conditional, the condition must also be necessary, and the combination necessitates perpetuity.
3
Connect to the final conclusion about rest (sukūn)
Razi concludes that everything eternal is impossible to cease. He then applies this to 'rest' (sukūn), arguing that since rest is known to be subject to passing away (jā'iz al-zawāl), it cannot be eternal.
This refutes those who claim rest is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist. It is a proof that proceeds by logical division. The author first divides the eternal into two categories: necessary in itself (wājib li-dhātih) and possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātih). So it must be necessary in itself. Then the effect of this necessary cause is either unconditional or conditional upon a condition. If unconditional, then the impossibility of non-existence of the cause entails the impossibility of non-existence of the effect. If conditional, the condition itself must also be necessary, and both the cause and condition being necessary entail the perpetual existence of the effect. Thus, in all cases, the eternal cannot cease to exist. The author then applies this to the specific case of 'rest' (sukūn) to show that rest, being subject to passing away, cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
علة قديمة
شرط
واجب لذاته
ممكن
أزلي
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the effect of an eternal cause is conditional, it could cease when the condition is removed.
Correction: The argument shows that the condition itself must be necessary and eternal, so the effect remains perpetual.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude if the effect of an eternal necessary cause is conditional upon a condition?
A) The condition must be possible, and the effect can cease.
B) The condition must be necessary, and the effect remains eternal.
C) The effect can cease if the condition is removed.
D) The cause is not truly eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second part demonstrates that an eternal effect necessarily follows from an eternal necessary cause, whether conditionally or unconditionally, establishing the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the eternal cannot cease, then everything you cling to that is not eternal will eventually vanish. What one attachment in your life right now are you treating as if it is eternal, and how will you detach from it starting today?
?
The proof shows that only the Necessary Being (Allah) is permanent by His essence. When you feel anxious about the future, do you truly trust in His permanence, or do you secretly rely on temporary conditions? What specific step will you take this week to ground your hope in Him alone?
?
You have just understood that an eternal effect requires an eternal cause and eternal conditions. What does that mean for your own actions? Are you building your life on the eternal (obedience to Allah) or on fleeting worldly gains? Write one concrete change you will make today to align your priorities with what is truly everlasting.

Does God Create by Essential Necessity or Choice?
Third objection questioning whether God creates necessarily or by choice, with author's response (cut off).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the Islamic doctrine of creation, which argues that if God's causation is essential, the world would be eternal, and if by choice, the implications for divine power.

Core Idea
God's creation cannot be by blind necessity; it must be by deliberate choice.

The third objection presents a dilemma: either God creates by essential necessity, like fire burning, or by capacity to choose. The objector tries to force a conclusion that undermines God's omnipotence. This lesson unpacks the first horn of the dilemma and begins the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure of the objection: two possibilities, each with consequences.
The text cuts off mid-argument.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objector presents two ways God could affect the world: essential causation (al-ījād al-dhātī) or by capacity (al-ṣiḥḥa). Recognize each.
Essential causation means necessary, like cause-effect; capacity means ability to act or refrain.
3
Examine the first horn
If essential causation, the objector claims the world would be eternal because God's essence is eternal. Think why that follows.
This is a classic argument against the Ash'ari view.
4
Consider the second horn
If by capacity, the objector argues that the agent must be capable of both action and inaction. If not, the agent becomes a necessitator.
This leads to a definition of qādir. The text ends here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى مؤثر في وجود هذا العالم ، فتأثيره فيه إما أن يكون على سبيل الإيجاد الذاتي ، وإما أن يكون على سبيل الصحة . فإن كان الأول ، لزم من دوام ذات الله تعالى، دوام العالم . وإن كان الثاني فنقول : المؤثر على سبيل الصحة هو الذي يكون قادراً على الفعل والترك . لأنه لو صح منه الفعل ، ولم يصح منه الترك ، فحينئذ يخرج عن كونه قادراً ، ويصير موجباً بالذات . وكل من كان قادراً على الفعل والترك ، وجب أن يكون الترك
The third question: He, exalted, is an agent (muʾaththir) in the existence of this world. His effect upon it is either by way of essential causation (al-ījād al-dhātī) or by way of capacity (al-ṣiḥḥa). If the first, then from the permanence of the essence of God, exalted, the permanence of the world follows. If the second, we say: The agent by way of capacity is the one who is capable (qādir) of action and inaction (al-fiʿl wa al-tark). Because if action is possible from him and inaction is not possible, then he exits from being capable and becomes a necessitator (mūjib) by essence. And everyone who is capable of action and inaction, it is necessary that inaction... [cut off]
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection regarding how God creates the world. The objector poses a dichotomy: either God's effect on the world is by essential causation (meaning He necessarily causes, like fire burns) or by way of capacity (meaning He can choose to act or not). If essential causation is true, then since God's essence is eternal, the world would also be eternal (which is against the Islamic doctrine of creation). If capacity (choice) is true, then the objector argues that an agent who acts by capacity must be capable of both doing and refraining from the action. If He could only act and not refrain, He would no longer be capable (qādir) but would be a necessitator (mūjib) by essence. The passage is cut off, but the argument likely continues to discuss implications for God's attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's power is like a natural cause, automatically producing effects.
Correction: The objector tries to force a view where God either creates necessarily (which would make the world eternal) or by choice but then must be capable of not creating. The author's response (if complete) would clarify God's voluntary creation.

Checkpoint

D) The world would be created in time.

Key Takeaway
The third objection presents a dilemma: either God creates necessarily (making the world eternal) or by choice, which raises questions about divine power and volition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objector argues that if God could not refrain from creating, He would not be truly powerful. Do you ever treat God's power as automatic—as if He must respond to your rituals or prayers? How can you today cultivate a deeper reverence for His absolute freedom and sovereignty?
?
The passage highlights that God is capable of both action and inaction. Are you comfortable with the idea that God may choose to not act in ways you desire? What does this teach you about trust and submission to His wisdom, especially in areas where you are waiting?

The Third Objection: God's Influence and the World's Eternity
Poses an objection that God's influence entails either eternal world or an eternal opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against temporal origination, which argues that God's influence on the world leads to the eternity of the world or its opposite.

Core Idea
God's influence on the world forces a dilemma: either the world is eternal or its opposite is eternal.

This lesson examines a sophisticated objection raised against the doctrine that the world originated in time. The objector argues that God's status as an influence on the world's existence forces a dilemma: either the world is eternal or an opposite of the world is eternal. By following the logic, you will see how the objection challenges the very possibility of temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dilemma
The passage opens by stating that God is an influence on the world's existence, and this influence must be either by essential origination or by way of possibility. Recognize this as the two horns of the dilemma.
The term 'influence' (muʾaththir) is central—it implies agency.
2
Analyze the First Horn
If the influence is by essential origination (ījād dhātī), then because God's essence is eternal, the world must also be eternal. This is a direct consequence.
3
Analyze the Second Horn
If the influence is by possibility (ṣiḥḥa), then God must be truly capable of both action and inaction. Otherwise He would be an essential necessitator (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt).
Capability requires the possibility of both action and its opposite.
4
Examine the Argument on Inaction
The objector argues that inaction (tark) cannot be mere absence of the act, because power cannot affect non-existence (pure negation) and the original non-existence already remains. So inaction must be an act of a contrary (ḍidd).
This step refutes the possibility of simple non-action.
5
See the Conclusion
Thus, in eternity, God either acts (creates the world) or leaves it (creates its opposite). Either way, something eternal exists, undermining the claim that the world originated in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : وهو أنه تعالى مؤثر في وجود هذا العالم ، فتأثيره فيه إما أن يكون على سبيل الإيجاد الذاتي ، وإما أن يكون على سبيل الصحة . فإن كان الأول ، لزم من دوام ذات الله تعالى، دوام العالم . وإن كان الثاني فنقول : المؤثر على سبيل الصحة هو الذي يكون قادراً على الفعل والترك . لأنه لو صح منه الفعل ، ولم يصح منه الترك ، فحينئذ يخرج عن كونه قادراً ، ويصير موجباً بالذات . وكل من كان قادراً على الفعل والترك ، وجب أن يكون الترك مقدوراً له . فنقول : هذا [ الترك ] (١) لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن العلم الأصلي المستمر ، لوجهين : الأول : إن العدم نفي محض، والقدرة صفة مؤثرة ، فامتنع كون القدرة مؤثرة في العدم الثاني : إن العدم الأصلي باق. والقدرة لا تأثير لها في الباقي . لما ثبت أن تحصيل الحاصل محال . فيثبت : أن الترك يمتنع أن يكون عبارة عن عدم الفعل، فوجب أن يكون عبارة عن فعل [ ضد (۲) ] الشيء . وإذا ثبت أنه تعالى قادراً في الأزل . فهو في الأزل، إما أن يكون فاعلاً للعالم ، وإما أن يكون تاركاً له . فإن كان الأول ، لزم قدم العالم ، وإن كان الثاني لزم قدم ضد العالم . وبتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، فإنه يمتنع دخول العالم في الوجود إلا عند زوال ذلك الضد الأزلي ، فيكون هذا قولاً بجواز العدم على القديم . وهو يبطل كلامكم .
The third question: He—exalted be He—is an influence (muʾaththir) in the existence of this world. His influence upon it is either by way of essential origination (ījād dhātī) or by way of possibility (ṣiḥḥa). If the first, then from the perpetuity of God's essence the perpetuity of the world follows. If the second, we say: The influence on the way of possibility is one who is capable (qādir) of action and inaction. For if action is valid from him and inaction is not valid from him, then he leaves being capable and becomes an essential necessitator (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt). And everyone who is capable of action and inaction must have inaction (tark) as something possible for him. We say: This inaction (1) cannot be expressed as the original continuous knowledge, for two reasons: First: Non-existence (ʿadam) is pure negation, and power is an effective attribute, so it is impossible for power to be effective in non-existence. Second: The original non-existence is remaining, and power has no effect on what is remaining, since it is established that obtaining what is already obtained is impossible. Thus it is established that inaction cannot be expressed as the absence of the act, so it must be expressed as an act of the opposite (2) of the thing. And when it is established that He is capable in eternity, then in eternity He is either a doer of the world or a leaver of it. If the first, the pre-eternity of the world follows; if the second, the pre-eternity of the opposite of the world follows. And assuming the matter is so, the world cannot come into existence except with the removal of that eternal opposite, and this is a statement allowing non-existence upon the eternal, and it invalidates your statement.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection (as-suʾāl ath-thālith) against the temporal origination of the world. It argues that God's influence on the world's existence must be either essential origination (ījād dhātī) or by way of possibility (ṣiḥḥa). If essential, God's eternality entails the world's eternality. If by possibility, then the agent must be capable of both action and inaction. The objector then argues that inaction (tark) cannot be mere absence of action, because that would require power over non-existence, which is impossible (since power is effective and non-existence is pure negation). Also, the original non-existence remains, and power cannot affect what already exists. Hence inaction must be an act of a contrary (ḍidd). Therefore, in eternity, God either acts (creates the world) or leaves it (creates its opposite). If He acts, the world is eternal; if He leaves, the opposite is eternal, and the world could only come after that opposite ceases, implying change in the eternal. This refutes the claim of temporal origination.

Key Terms
مؤثر (muʾaththir)
إيجاد ذاتي (ījād dhātī)
صحة (ṣiḥḥa)
قادر (qādir)
فعل (fiʿl)
ترك (tark)
عدم (ʿadam)
ضد (ḍidd)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's inaction is simply not acting, a mere absence of action.
Correction: The objection argues that for a capable agent, inaction cannot be mere absence; it must be a positive act of bringing about a contrary, because power cannot be effective in pure non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, why can't 'inaction' (tark) be simply the absence of the act?
A) Because inaction is a positive act.
B) Because power cannot be effective in pure non-existence and the original non-existence already remains.
C) Because God must always be acting.
D) Because inaction implies a defect in God.

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that God's influence on the world forces either the world's eternity or the eternity of its opposite, thereby challenging creation in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's influence could force the world's eternity, what does that mean for your belief that God freely chose to create you? When you feel your existence is somehow forced or meaningless, how does this lesson bring you back to the reality of God's free and personal act of creation? Will you today thank Him for that free gift of existence?
?
The objection argues that your inaction is not neutral but a positive act. Think of a situation today where you chose not to do something good. Can you see that inaction as an actual act of commission? How will you change your view of your own omissions before God? Commit to one specific good action you will not omit today.
?
The dilemma claims that if God were capable of both creating and not creating, He must have done one or the other in eternity. Do you ever struggle with the idea that God's action might be constrained by logical necessities? How does this passage challenge you to submit to the absolute freedom of God even when His ways are beyond your logic? Will you trust that His action for you is perfectly wise, even when you cannot see the alternative?
